\section*{DIAGNOSTIC IMAGING OVERVIEW}

\author{
DR A. AYWAK Senior lecturer DDIRM University of Nairobi
}

\section*{Introduction}
n Diagnostic Imaging is the study of healthy and disea sed organs and tissues by imaging. The intervention al branch provides treatment to certain disease proce sses.
n The information acquired makes it a primary tool in clinical medicine at primary, secondary and tertiary care.
n The images are an extension of patient examinatio n
n The practicing physician must know from physical e xamination when to opt for an imaging Test, which

\section*{Introduction cont..}

Indeed every speciality of medicine utilizes imaging at one point or other to
n help establish a diagnosis
n assess disease extend
n and more often than not, provide information on prognosis esp ecially in cases of malignancies and their spread to other areas.

\section*{Introduction cont.}

As a student in your clinical years of study you should be able to answer the following questio ns in every given clinical case.
n Does the patient require an imaging examination?
n If so which imaging modality will be most suitable a nd cost effective.
n What is the selected imaging modality likely to confi rm or exclude

\section*{Introduction cont.}
n Does the patient require an imaging examinati on?
n If so which imaging modality will be most su itable and cost effective.

\section*{Computerised Tomography(CT)}

\section*{n CT uses X. rays to produce images}
n In the initial equipments, the $x$-ray tube spins around the patient while detectos moved around the patient in opposite direction to the tube
n A computer then performs calculations of densities in each square of a slice to produce the imanas

Computed Tomography (CT)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b62e9a2a7f7fcd25b5acg-25.jpg?height=964&width=849&top_left_y=742&top_left_x=1438)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b62e9a2a7f7fcd25b5acg-26.jpg?height=1304&width=868&top_left_y=414&top_left_x=156)

In computerised tomography (CT) the X-ray source rotates around a plane of the body, taking seria 1 pictures with a detector (instea d of a film) which are synthesize d by a computer.

The resulting picture created by t he computer is like a section of $t$ he body and can be recorded on a film. CT pictures are therefor e like X-ray images.

\section*{Ultrasound}
- Uses high frequency sound to make images
$-2-15 \mathrm{MHz}$ typically used
- The sound is produced and detected with the same device: TRANSDUCER
- Transducer
- Speaker: sound into patient
- Microphone: sound coming back from patient
- Analogous to SONAR used in undersea warfare

\section*{Ultrasound}
n Ultrasound uses mechanical waves of frequencies beyond the audible range.
n These waves are reflected to various degrees from junction s of tissues of different nature.
n Ultrasound pictures require considerable skill to Produce and interpret.
n Ultrasound has a great advantage - it does not cause cellul ar damage when used in quantities required for imaging

\section*{Magnetic Resonance Imaging(MRI)}

Magnetic resonance imaging exploits the existence of induced nuclear magneti sm in the patient.

Magnets with an odd number of photons or neutrons possess a weak but obser vable nuclear magnetic moment.

Hydogen nucleus is the most commonly imaged, although (13C, Phosphorous $(P)$ sodium $(\mathrm{Na})$ and Fluorine $(F)$ are also of significant int erest.

The nuclear moments are normally randomly oriented, but they align when pla ced in a strong magnetic field (typically 0.2-1.5 T).

The NMR signal from a human is due predominantly to water protons. frequency.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b62e9a2a7f7fcd25b5acg-42.jpg?height=1462&width=2328&top_left_y=178&top_left_x=0)

\section*{MR SPECTROSCOPY}

\section*{How does MR spectroscopy work?}
- MR spectroscopy is conducted on the same machine as conventional MRI.
- Spectroscopy is a series of tests that are added to the MRI scan of brain or spine to measure the chemical metabolism of a suspected lesion.
- There are several different metabolites, or products of metabolism, that can be measured to differentiate between tumor types.

\section*{Nuclear Scintigraphy}
- Often called NUCLEAR MEDICINE
- Uses radioactive tracers that emit radiation
- Electromagnetic OR particulate
- Often these are injected into the vein
- Different tracers go to different organs or parts of the body
- Images are made by detecting the radiation coming out of the patient

\section*{Interventional Radiology}
- Radiologists do invasive procedures guided by images for either diagnosis or treatment
- Basically anything that breaks the skin
- Needles for biopsy or fluid removal
- Catheters to make angiograms
- Catheters with balloons to open blood vessels
- Stents to hold blood vessels open
- Coils and material to block blood vessels
- Catheters to drain abscesses
- Tubes for feeding
- etc etc

<a id="chapter-02"></a>

APPROACH TO IMAGING REQUESTS IN CLINICAL APPROACH

\section*{WHAT IS AN IMAGING REQUEST}
t An imaging request is consultation between the clinician and the radiological team (radiologist/radiographer)
t Consultation - deliberation of 2 or more persons on some matter

\title{
OUTLINE OF AN IMAGING REQUEST DESIGN
}
t Patient's biodata
t Radiological examination being requested
t Relevant clinical information
t Patient's past medical history and previous imaging findings

\section*{PATENT'S BIODATA}
t Unique ID - Hospital No., clinic no. etc.
t Name
t Age
t Sex
t Date
t Address - ward, clinic, telephone, physical etc.
t LMP (female patients)

\section*{CLINICAL INFORMATION}
t There still exists no substitute for proper history and physical examination on clinical practice
t Signs and symptoms narrow down to a particular system/organ. In some cases a definitive diagnosis is made while in others differential diagnoses are inferred clinically
t Explicit relevant information of your clinical findings guides the radiologist/radiographer to resonate with your expectations both in technique and interpretation of findings
t For female patients of reproductive age, Last Menstrual Period is important
t History of allergies if known needs to be included

\section*{FACTORS TO CONSIDER WHEN GENERATING AN IMAGING REQUEST}
t Appropriateness of the examination and modality
t Patient preparation for the examination
t Limitations of the modality

\section*{DECIDING THE APPROPRIATE RADIOLOGICAL INVESTIGATION}
t What is the clinical question you want answered by radiology?
t What system/organ is of interest? What imaging modalities are relevant to that system/organ
t How will
t Analyze:
t Patient's stability, immediate and special needs
t Availability of the preferred modality
t Cost
t Radiation safety considerations
t Details that need to be derived from the imaging modality
t Expertise of the imaging team

\section*{INVESTIGATION: PATIENT'S STABILITY, IMMEDIATE AND SPECIAL NEEDS}
t In acute life threatening situations, patients' stability overrides any imaging and therefore the latter is done without any danger to the patient's life
t Airway, breathing, circulation (hemodynamic state) need to be sorted our before transfer of patient to the imaging department
t Special needs have to be addressed e.g. hypothermia in neonates and infants. Allergies when performing contrast studies, metallic implants when performing MRI are all risks to be considered, Can you think of any other special needs?

\section*{INVESTIGATION AVAILABILITY AND COST CONSIDERATIONS}
t These are not absolute determinants but need to be factored in particularly in regard to the influence the radiological examination will have on the patient's outcome

\section*{INVESTIGATION: RADIATION SAFETY CONSIDERATIONS}
t lonizing vs. non-ionizing radiation
t If a clinical question can be adequately answered by a modality that utilizes noionizing radiation then go for it over one that will use ionizing radiation
t Children, pregnant mothers carry the greatest risk
t Some organs are more radiosensitive than others e.g. gonads, thyroid gland, eye lens

\section*{INVESTIGATION: \\ DETAILS EXPECTED FROM THE INVESTIGATION}
t Each of the imaging modalities have their strengths and weaknesses when it comes to detailing anatomical structures and pathological processes
t Moreover, even for the appropriate modality, extent of the examination including field of view, views required and where applicable contrast introduction need to be optimized.

\section*{PATIENT PREPARATION}
t Some radiological examinations require special preparation
t I.V contrast - renal function tests
t Upper GI contrast studies - fasting at least 6 hours
t Contrast enema - bowel preparation
t IVU - bowel preparation
t Abdominal US - Fasting at least 6 hours
t Pelvic US - Full bladder
t Plain radiography - Poor soft tissue detail, representation of disease in 2D, ionizing radiation
t Contrast radiography - ionizing radiation. Lack of extra-luminal details, poor soft tissue details
t Ultrasound - operator dependence, inability to penetrate gas e.g. bowel disease, bone disease
t CT - radiation dose, cost, availability
t MRI - availability, cost, limited expertise, metallic implants, claustrophobia
t Angiography - invasive, limited expertise, radiation
t Inadequate information
t Poor/ineligible handwriting
t Failure to pay attention to details
t Treating imaging examination casually/poor concentration
t Use of non-standard abbreviations

\section*{REMEMBER}
t If not sure of the appropriate radiological investigation consult the radiologist

\section*{Imaging of The GIT}

\section*{Dr. Angeline. A. Aywak}
¬ß The discovery of X-rays made radiological procedures play a leading role in the visualization of anatomy and diseases of the gastrointestinal system.
¬ß Later the advent of fibre optic endoscopic examination has changed the role of imaging of GIT pathology.
¬ß Even so Imaging still has a vital role in management of gastrointestinal conditions.

\section*{Why Irnage?}

\section*{¬ß Imaging is needed to make or confirm a diagnosis and show disease extend.}
¬ß Help to plan treatment
¬ß To follow up disease response after treatment.

\section*{¬ß Listed below are current gastrointestinal imaging modalities}
A) Plain X-rays
B) Ultrasound
C) Contrast studies
D) Computerized Tomography
E) Radionuclide Scanning
F) Magnetic Resonance Imaging
G) Angiography

\section*{A. Plain X-RAY Films}
¬ß With plain X-rays the gastrointestinal organs cannot be separately visualized, however the normal or pathological gas accumulations, air fluid levels, calcification and motility of gas containing intestine can be seen.

\section*{Plain Films}

\section*{Indications}
¬ß Suspected obstructive conditions
i. Congenital e.g. TOF, Pyloric stenosis, duodenal atresia.
ii. Acquired e.g. intussusception, strangulated hernia.
¬ß Localization of radio-opaque foreign bodies
¬ß Suspected perforation of intestinal tract.
¬ß Suspected calculus.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_47dd6f3563e65d0f7badg-10.jpg?height=1251&width=1241&top_left_y=322&top_left_x=642)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_47dd6f3563e65d0f7badg-12.jpg?height=1155&width=1825&top_left_y=360&top_left_x=336)

\section*{B. Ultrasound examination.}
¬ß In the hollow organs an increased wall thickness, increased degree of fluid content, pathological motility and status of surrounding environment (organs, peritoneal space) can be clearly visualized by ultrasound.
¬ß All solid orgarns and the peritoneal cavity can be assessed by ultrasound.

\section*{Indications for ultrasound}

\section*{¬ß Suspected masses \\ ¬ß Suspected peritoneal fluid collections \\ ¬ß Suspected calculus \\ ¬ß Suspected inflammatory conditions like appendicitis}
¬ß Suspected intusussception
¬ß The jaundiced patient

\section*{Endosonography}
¬ß With this method the internal lumens of the oesophagus, stomach, duodenum, rectum and their surroundings can be examined.
¬ß Wall structure, pathological processes and their relationship with surrounding organs can be visualized.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_47dd6f3563e65d0f7badg-20.jpg?height=1783&width=1035&top_left_y=43&top_left_x=742)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_47dd6f3563e65d0f7badg-21.jpg?height=958&width=1481&top_left_y=672&top_left_x=548)

\section*{Controlst Studies}
¬ß Contrast material is introduced into the system. Ba sulphate, Air, Ionic water soluble as well as non-ionic water soluble contrast materials used.
¬ß Choice of contrast depends on suspected pathology .

\section*{Single Contrast.}
¬ß The gastrointestinal lumen is filled with positive contrast material usually (Barium sulfate).
¬ß This then makes easy visualization of pathological conditions such as stenosis, dilatations, intraluminal filling defects, organ contour deformity and external compression.
¬ß The examination can also verify function of the system i.e. emptying and peristalsis.

\section*{Double contrast studies}
¬ß This examination applies use of two contrast materials a positive one (Barium sulfate or gastrografin) and a negative one (usually air).
¬ß The wall of the organ is relaxed by smooth muscle relaxant. Membrane is coated by contrast material and lumen distended with gas.
¬ß This provides more information than single contrast examination as detailed visualization of gut mucosa is achieved, the method however does not afford a functional information.

\section*{Types of contrast studies}

\section*{$X$ ray versus other imaging modalities}
- Plain x ray in the mainstay in trauma imaging
- One needs two orthogonal views at right angles to each other.
- The status of soft tissues is poorly depicted.
- Scintigraphy is very sensitive in the detection of stress fractures.
- CT is more sensitive and specific in the description of regions of complex anatomy such as the face, spine and pelvis.
- MRI excellent for soft tissue depiction.
- Use of US is increasing.

- Two occasions - repeat x- rays

\section*{Bone tumours}
- Radiology is essential for full evaluation of a bone tumour.
- Definitive diagnosis is established by histopathologist.
- This is not always possible.
- Also pathology findings should not be considered in isolation.
- Team work vital between clinician, radiologist and pathologist.

\section*{1. Detection}
2. Diagnosis
3. Staging
prebiopsy
- presurgen

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_9ef9e69b9f2d05bc7a99g-38.jpg?height=1200&width=1038&top_left_y=452&top_left_x=1458)

\section*{BONE TUMOURS: thols of the trade}
- Radiography.
- Sonography.

- Scintigraphy.

\section*{Radiographic features verrur clastification.}
- The classification of a lesion is not as important as an understanding of the radiographic features that make the abmormality look immocuous or aggressive.
- Type 1a lesions are at one end of the spectrum as the most innocuous and nonaggressive appearing, and type 3 lesions are at the other end of the spectrum as the most aggressive appearing.

Osteomyelitis and localized Langerhans cell histiocytosis are benign processes that can have an aggressive permeated appearance, and a giant cell tumor may look well defined but be locally aggressive and, on rare occasions, may even metastasize.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_9ef9e69b9f2d05bc7a99g-41.jpg?height=721&width=1349&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=1147)

Osteomyelitis and localized Langerhans cell histiocytosis also look "blue" on hematoxylin and eosin stained specimens.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_9ef9e69b9f2d05bc7a99g-41.jpg?height=1159&width=2493&top_left_y=709&top_left_x=6)

\section*{APPROACH.}
- The two most important aspects of evaluating a bone tumor are the location of the tummor and the age of the patient.
- Knowledge of this information alone is enough to narrow the differential diagnosis without even looking at any images.
- The specific radiographic appearance should then help narrow the list even further and will often lead to the single correct diagnosis.

\section*{Pillar to radiologic diagnorir of bone lumorr.}
1. Age.
2. Location.
3. Margins.
4. Periosteal reaction.
5. Tumor opacity.
6. Matrix mineralization.
7. Size and number.
8. Cortical involvement.
9. Soft tissue involvement.
10. Advanced imaging.

\section*{I.PRTIEAT RGE.}
- Most bone tumors have a predilection for a specific age group.
- For example, simple bone cysts and chondroblastomas occur in skeletally immature people, while giant cell tumors occur in skeletally mature people.
- According to Edeihem, about 80\% of mallgmant tumors can be correctly diagmosed on the basis of age alome even without looking at the radiograph.

\footnotetext{
Kricun ME. Radiographic evaluation of solitary bone lesions. Orthop Clin North Am 1983,14:39-64.
}
\begin{tabular}{|l|l|l|}
\hline Age (y) & Benign & Malignant \\
\hline <20 & Fibrous cortical defect, nonossifying fibroma, simple bone cyst, aneurysmal bone cyst, chondroblastoma, Langerhans cell histiocytosis, osteoblastoma, osteoid osteoma, osteofibrous dysplasia, chondromyxoid fibroma, fibrous dysplasia, enchondroma & Leukemia, Ewing sarcoma, osteosarcoma (conventional, periosteal, telangiectatic), metastatic disease (rare), neuroblastoma, retinoblastoma, thabdomyosarcoma, Hodgkin lymphoma \\
\hline 20-40 & Enchondroma, giant cell tumor, osteoblastoma, osteoid osteoma, chondromyxoid fibroma, fibrous dysplasia & Osteosarcoma (parosteal), adamantinoma \\
\hline >40 & Fibrous dysplasia, Paget disease, non-Hodgkin lymphoma, chondrosarcoma, malignant fibrous histiocytoma, osteosarcoma (secondary to Paget disease and radiation) & Metastatic disease (most common), myeloma \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

\section*{2.LOGATION.}
- Most bone tumors, regardless of whether they are benign or malignant, often occur in a characteristic location in the skeleton.
- Thus, some tumors eg, esteesarcoma have a predilection for sites of rapid bone growth, usually the metaphyseal region, while other tumors eg, Ewing sarcoma tend to follow the distribution of red marrow.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_9ef9e69b9f2d05bc7a99g-47.jpg?height=1844&width=2464&top_left_y=28&top_left_x=0)

\section*{s.marcins (lodwick).}
- Bone lesions may range from a discrete well-defined abnormality to an ill-defined infiltrative process.
- The margin of the lesion and the zone of transition between lesion and adjacent bone are hey factors in determining if a lesion is aggressive.
- A lesion with sharp margins and a narrow transition zone is radiographically considered nonaggressive, particularly when the margins have a sclerotic border.

Resnick D. Diagnosis of bone and joint disorders. 4th ed.
Philadelphia, Pa: Saunders, 2002;3757:3922-3924.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_9ef9e69b9f2d05bc7a99g-49.jpg?height=1392&width=1777&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=349)
- Type la geographic lesion.
- Intraosseous lipoma of the calcaneus, with a sclerotic rim.
- Solid or unilamellated periosteal reaction is a monaggressive appearance, simce it indicates that the underlying lesion is slow growing and is giving the bone a chance to wall the lesion off.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_9ef9e69b9f2d05bc7a99g-51.jpg?height=881&width=814&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=1)

Onion okin.
- A multilamellated or "omionskin" appearance suggests an intermediate aggressive process, such as one that wases and wanes or one that the bone is continually trying to wall off but cannot.
- Interruption (i.e., regional disruption) of either the uni- or multilamellated periosteal reaction suggests an aggressive process that has broken through the periosteum.

\section*{Sunburk periorkerl elevalion.}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_9ef9e69b9f2d05bc7a99g-52.jpg?height=603&width=751&top_left_y=534&top_left_x=294)
- A spiculated, or "hair-on-end" (perpendicular to the cortex) or sunburst pattern, is the most aggressive appearance and is highly suggestive of malignancy.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_9ef9e69b9f2d05bc7a99g-53.jpg?height=1457&width=2407&top_left_y=17&top_left_x=31)

Lateral radiograph in patient with osteosarcoma shows the elevated periosteum forming Codman triangle (long arrow).
Notice the tumor-induced new bone formation (short arrows.)

\section*{6.Tumor matris.}
- The radiographic opacity of a lesion can also be affected by the mineralization of its matrix.
- The term matrix refers to the type of tissue of the tumor-such as osteoid, chondral, fibrous, or adipose, all of which are radiolucent-and mineralization refers to calcification of the matrix.
- This concept of matrix mineralization is important to understand, because the pattern of mineralization can be a clue to the type of underlying matrix and, thus, the diagnosis.

Lateral radiograph of proximal portion of tibia shows enchondroma with punctate and arclike mineralization (arrows).

\section*{7. Size and number.}
- The size of a lesion can also be a clue to its diagnosis, since some entities have size criteria.
- For example, osteoid osteoma and osteoblastoma are histologically similar lesions, but they differ in size:
- The nidus of an osteoid osteoma is less than 1.5 cm in diameter, while the osteoblastoma is larger than 1.5 cm (19).
\begin{tabular}{|l|l|}
\hline Lesion Characteristic & Lesion \\
\hline Scierotic & Multiple bone islands (osteopoikilosis) \\
\hline & Osteoblastic metastatic carcinoma (prostate, breast, lung, colon, mucin-producing adenocarcinoma) \\
\hline Lytic & Osteolytic metastatic carcinoma (prostate, breast, lung, colon, etc) \\
\hline & Myeloma \\
\hline & Non-Hodgkin lymphoma \\
\hline & Benign vascular lesions: hemangioma, cystic angiomatosis (rare tumor) \\
\hline & Malignant vascular lesions (rare tumors): hemangioendothelioma, angiosarcoma, hemangiopericytoma \\
\hline & Brown tumors of hyperparathyroidism (should have other radiographic features of hyperparathyroidism) \\
\hline & Multiple enchondromatosis (Ollier and Maffuci disease; may have internal mineralization or appear as lucent linear seams of cartilage within bone) \\
\hline & Fibrous dysplasia (usually ground-glass lytic but may be mixed lytic and sclerotic; bone may be deformed) \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

\section*{8. Gorlieal involvement.}
- In addition to lesions that specifically arise within the cortex, the cortex may be affected by processes that originate in the medullary canal or the periosteum or surrounding soft tissue.
- For example, as a medullary process expands, it may cause erosion of the inner surface of the cortex, called endosteal scalloping (Fig 4).
- If the medullary lesion is so aggressive that it erodes the inner aspect of the cortex without giving the periosteum a chance to lay down new bone, the cortex will eventually be completely destroyed and breached by the lesion.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_9ef9e69b9f2d05bc7a99g-59.jpg?height=1456&width=1322&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=605)
- Anteroposterior radiograph of humerus in a patient with periosteal chondrosarcoma shows periosteal buttress (short white arrow).
- Note well-defined saucerization of humeral shaft (black arrows) and faint mineralization of the matrix (long white arrow).

\section*{9.Soft tireue involvement.}
- The presence of a soft-tissue component with a bone lesion suggests a malignant process.
- The tumor may have frankly destroyed the cortex as it expanded, or it may have permeated through the haversian canals of the cortex to reach the surrounding tissue.
- The soft-tissue component may displace adjacent fat planes.
- Tumors that often have a soft-tissue component are osteosarcoma, Ewing sarcoma, and lymphoma.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_9ef9e69b9f2d05bc7a99g-61.jpg?height=1431&width=1105&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=637)
- Lateral radiograph of distal portion of femur shows osteosarcoma with amorphous tumor-induced new bone formation (black arrows).
- Note the large soft-tissue mass (white arrows) that displaces adjacent fat.

\section*{I O. Rdvanced tooll.}
- While radiographs are often sufficient to enable a diagnosis, advanced imaging is sometimes needed.
- MR images and CT scans may provide additional information by virtue of their tomographic nature, multiplanar capability, and better soft-tissue contrast than radiographs.
- CT is useful for evaluating subtle mineralization in a lytic lesion, for demonstrating radiographically occult bone destruction, or for demonstrating the lucent nidus of an osteoid osteoma amid a large area of reactive sclerosis.
- MR imaging has become the standard for evaluating the local extent of a malignant process for the purposes of staging and assessing tumor response to chemotherapy.
- However, it must be stressed that CT and MR images should only be interpreted with concurrent radiographic correlation.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_9ef9e69b9f2d05bc7a99g-63.jpg?height=1367&width=2495&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=4)
- MR imaging has become the standard for evaluating the local extent of a malignant process for the purposes of staging and assessing tumor response to chemotherapy.
- However, it must be stressed that CT and MR images should only be interpreted with concurrent radiographic correlation.

\section*{Take home.}
- Tumors have a bypical patient age range- $420,20-40$, and >40 years old=and a favored location in the streleton.
- The margin of a lesion and type of periosteal reaction are indicators of lesion aggressiveness, but not necessarily of whether it is benign or malignant:
- A well-defined lesion with a sclerotic rim and thicts unillamellar periosteal reaction is the most innocuous appearance, while a permeated pattern with spiculated periosted reaction is the most aggressive.
- The pattern of mineralization of the tumor matrix is often the clue to the type of tumor:

IMAGING IN OSTEOMY'ELITIS
- Early diagnosis greatly improves outlook.
- Must use available tools optimally.
- often xrays are all you have.

\section*{WHY IS IMAGING VITAL?}
- The symptoms and signs of skeletal infections are varied and often non specific.
- They mimic many other conditions most significantly degenerative disease.
- Thus imaging has a crucial role in the detection, discrimination and follow-up of infections of bone, joint and soft tissues.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_9ef9e69b9f2d05bc7a99g-67.jpg?height=1875&width=2469&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=0)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_9ef9e69b9f2d05bc7a99g-68.jpg?height=1372&width=2463&top_left_y=271&top_left_x=37)

\section*{Scope}

Methods of imaging
- Urinary system
-Male/ female genital system
- Indications
- Advantages
- Disadvantages
- Imaging pathology
2. Plain tilms - KUB
3. Ultrasound
4. Intravenous urogram (pyelogram) - (IVU, IVP; excretion urography)
5. Micturating cystogram or cystourethrogram (MCUG)
6. Cystogram
7. Urethrogram (ascending urethrogram)
8. Retrograde pyelogram
9. Antegrade pyelogram
10. Radionuclide studies:
11. Computed tomography (CT)
12. Magnetic Resonance Imaging (MRI)
13. Angiography

\section*{1. ULTRASOUND}
- investigation of choice in children
- In adults, depending on indication Ultrasound or IVU/CT pyelogram maybe considered.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-02.jpg?height=411&width=642&top_left_y=945&top_left_x=384)

\section*{Indications of ultrasound}
1. Assess the kidneys in suspected renal pathology; location, size, echotexture, masses, cysts, hydronephrosis, calcifications
2. Vascularity of the kidney, renal masses or suspected vascular lesions
3. Resistive index of renal arteries in suspected renal artery stenosis
4. Ultrasound guided procedures e.g. Percutaneous kidney biopsies, drainage catheter placement
5. Assess renal transplants; size, structure, vascularity, fluid collections
6. Obstructive uropathy; level of obstruction and sometimes the cause of the obstruction
7. Bladder outline, bladder volume, residual volume
8. Assess the surrounding structures in the retroperitoneum/extraperitoneum for fluid
1. it is cheap and readily available
2. it is safe - no danger of contrast reactions, can still assess kidneys in renal failure
3. no radiation
4. Can be used in point-of-care or emergency settings

\section*{Ultrasound; Disadvantages}
1. the ureters cannot be seen unless dilated.
2. calyceal detail cannot be seen which is necessary for assessment of chronic pyelonephritis, papillary necrosis and tuberculosis
3. Uro-endothelial (transitional cell) tumours of the renal pelvis/calyces are not visualised until large.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-04.jpg?height=1272&width=1271&top_left_y=91&top_left_x=119)
nephrolithiasis
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-04.jpg?height=429&width=632&top_left_y=1571&top_left_x=445)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-05.jpg?height=481&width=734&top_left_y=356&top_left_x=243)

Renal cell
cancer

\section*{TRANSPLANTED KIDNEY}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-05.jpg?height=510&width=1264&top_left_y=1211&top_left_x=126)
2. PLAIN FILMS - KUB
1. Calcifications- 85-90\% of renal stones calcify.
$\square$ Stag horn calculus ; large calculus occupying much of the pelvi-calyceal system.
$\square$ Nephrocalcinosis - multiple calcifications in the parenchyma.
2. Displacement of bowel loops in abdominal mass
3. Spinal metastases in prostatic cancer, spinal anomalies in a child with urinary problems
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-06.jpg?height=657&width=481&top_left_y=633&top_left_x=349)

- Renal parenchymal
stones-
- medullary sponge kidney

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-06.jpg?height=555&width=885&top_left_y=1448&top_left_x=398)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-07.jpg?height=688&width=1264&top_left_y=224&top_left_x=126)
2. GAS IN THE URINARY TRACT

Gas in the bladder lumen may be seen in
vesico-intestinal fistula

Cystitis due anaerobic infection in diabetics
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-07.jpg?height=569&width=471&top_left_y=1013&top_left_x=715)

Invertograms Vs prone cross-table laterals
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-08.jpg?height=405&width=986&top_left_y=92&top_left_x=398)
3. INTRAVENOUS PYELOGRAM (IVP)
1. Intravenous Urogram is indicated in suspected urinary tract pathology when ultrasound has failed to make a diagnosis.
2. Is NOT sensitive in hypertension
3. Iodinated water-soluble contrast medium is given intravenously and series of films taken

Indications for IVU
1. Haematuria
2. dilated ureter on ultrasound
3. inconclusive ultrasound scan

Less common indications:
1. to show the position of ureters for planning surgery or X-ray therapy
2. in suspected calyceal deformity in tuberculosis, papillary necrosis and chronic pyelonephritis
3. Trauma - if ultrasound is inconclusive and CT not available

\section*{Contra-indications: IVU}

Intravenous pyelography is not indicated in:
1. Advanced renal failure - use ultrasound
2. Prostatism
3. Polycystic renal disease - better seen on ultrasound
4. Vague abdominal pain
5. Acute urinary tract infection
6. Renal artery stenosis
7. Previous reaction to iodinated contrast

\section*{Relative contraindications: IVU}

Intravenous pyelography should be performed with caution in:
1. diabetic patients
2. very small children
3. myelomatosis
4. patients with sickle cell disease
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-09.jpg?height=306&width=811&top_left_y=1734&top_left_x=128)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-10.jpg?height=1078&width=1271&top_left_y=92&top_left_x=122)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-10.jpg?height=517&width=1032&top_left_y=1411&top_left_x=245)

\section*{4. MICTURATING CYSTOGRAPHY \\ MCU - study of bladder emptying after water soluble contrast has been inserted into the bladder, usually by catheter.}

Its main use is to show:
Ureteric reflux
Abnormality of the posterior urethra - urethral valves or stricture.
Vesico-vaginal fistula (usually just a cystogram is adequate)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-11.jpg?height=1102&width=1260&top_left_y=936&top_left_x=128)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-12.jpg?height=653&width=1264&top_left_y=568&top_left_x=126)
5. ASCENDINGURETHROGRAPHY

This is only performed in males. Indications are: $\qquad$
1. urethral stricture
2. trauma
3. congenital abnormalities
4. fistulae or false passages due to previous catheterisation/ instrumentation.

It is contraindicated in:
acute urinary infection
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-13.jpg?height=580&width=786&top_left_y=208&top_left_x=354)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-13.jpg?height=516&width=622&top_left_y=1040&top_left_x=124)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-13.jpg?height=348&width=798&top_left_y=1692&top_left_x=341)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-14.jpg?height=268&width=798&top_left_y=94&top_left_x=341)

\section*{6. RETROGRADE PYELOGRAM}

Its use has reduced with the advent of computed tomography and ultrasound

Main indication:-
- Demonstrate detail of the pelvi-calyceal system or ureter that has not been adequately seen in IVU especially in suspected Transitional cell tumor
${ }^{\circ}$ It is contra-indicated in acute urinary infection.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-14.jpg?height=569&width=426&top_left_y=1329&top_left_x=327)

Ureteric intra-luminal lesion obstructing the ureter and causing proximal dilatation.

Complications include:
damage to the ureter or perforation
infection

\section*{7. ANTEGRADE PYELOGRAM}

Used to demonstrate the pelvicalceal system and ureter in obstructive uropathy $\qquad$
May be combined with percutaneous nephrostomy in order to relieve obstruction as a temporary measure.

Contraindication- acute infection.
${ }^{\circ}$ Contrast is injected through a fine needle introduced percutaneously into the pelvi-calyceal system under ultrasound or fluoroscopic guidance.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-15.jpg?height=692&width=1260&top_left_y=938&top_left_x=128)
8. RADIONUCLIDESTUDIES
1. Static scan:

Radiopharmaceutical attaches to normal functioning renal tissue.
Demonstrates the size, outline and amount of normal renal parenchyma
(Dimercaptosuccinate-99mTc).
1. Indication- renal parenchymal disease
2. Dynamic scan:

Measures the excretion the radiopharmaceutical by the kidneys primarily by GFR and its clearance down the ureters

Functional study (99mTc-DTPA).
1. Indications- hypertension, renal tubular acidosis, renal transplant
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-16.jpg?height=692&width=1004&top_left_y=524&top_left_x=280)
9. COMPUTED TOMOGRAPHY.

Used in the assessment of
1. renal masses
2. Urinary tract obstruction
3. retroperitoneal disease
4. renal and bladder neoplasms; staging
5. Trauma; CT is the imaging method of choice in renal trauma.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-17.jpg?height=1351&width=1264&top_left_y=198&top_left_x=126)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-17.jpg?height=334&width=464&top_left_y=1706&top_left_x=371)

CT urography
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-18.jpg?height=344&width=478&top_left_y=98&top_left_x=368)

\section*{9. ANGIOGRAPHY}

Ultrasound, and especially Doppler studies, CT have reduced the need for diagnostic renal angiography.

Main indications:
1. prior to interventional techniques e.g. angioplasty
2. to diagnose renal artery stenosis
3. vascular lesions e.g. arterio-venous fistula, angioma, aneurysm
4. anatomical detail prior to renal transplant or suspected vascular occlusion following surgery

Angiography

Flush Aortography vs. Selective renal
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-18.jpg?height=572&width=1050&top_left_y=1338&top_left_x=299)

\section*{10. MRI SCANNING}
1. Main indication-
1. staging renal, bladder and prostatic tumours.
2. MR angiography can show renal vein involvement in renal cell carcinoma.
3. MR urography to demonstrate the urinary system
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-19.jpg?height=559&width=854&top_left_y=1011&top_left_x=436)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-19.jpg?height=320&width=387&top_left_y=1720&top_left_x=480)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-20.jpg?height=352&width=380&top_left_y=94&top_left_x=480)

MRA- to assess vascular anatomy
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_a6ad3bc3c93f24de6f80g-20.jpg?height=482&width=454&top_left_y=664&top_left_x=636)

\section*{ULTRASOUND}
¬ß The ability to differentiate normal from abnormal is directly related to the skill of the ultrasonagrapher
¬ß Resolution of pathology at an earlier stage is being recognised by improvement in US equipment.
¬ß So updating of skills and equipment is also needed

\section*{NUCLEAR MEDICINE}

\section*{"Unclear Medicine" ?}

The use of small amounts of radioactive materials to diagnose and treat disease.
NUCLEAR MEDICINE SHOWS PHYSIOLOGY

Whereas
RADIOLOGY SHOWS ANATOMY

\section*{GENERATOR SYSTEM}

The basis of the generator system (the cow) is that the daughter is physically separable from the parent.
- This process is termed "milking".

The eluate contains sodium pertechnetate.
Since the generator system is small and can be shipped easily, it makes the routine use of 99 mTc possible in nuclear medicine
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_37a2ab3686fd0f6ff7aag-40.jpg?height=763&width=827&top_left_y=722&top_left_x=1615)

\section*{BILATERAL TESTICULAR INFARCTION}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_37a2ab3686fd0f6ff7aag-41.jpg?height=782&width=2281&top_left_y=470&top_left_x=116)
- Testicular torsion leads to testicular infarction. Testicular ischemia may also result from epididymitis as in this pt.
- Early scintigraphic flow images show markedly increased flow to scrotal regions bilaterally. Tissue-phase scintigram shows increased peri-testicular activity with bilateral central regions of diminished activity.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_37a2ab3686fd0f6ff7aag-42.jpg?height=1624&width=1720&top_left_y=240&top_left_x=572)

\section*{NUCLEAR MEDICINE}

\section*{"Unclear Medicine" ?}

The use of small amounts of radioactive materials to diagnose and treat disease.
NUCLEAR MEDICINE SHOWS PHYSIOLOGY

Whereas
RADIOLOGY SHOWS ANATOMY

\section*{GENERATOR SYSTEM}

The basis of the generator system (the cow) is that the daughter is physically separable from the parent.
- This process is termed "milking".

The eluate contains sodium pertechnetate.
Since the generator system is small and can be shipped easily, it makes the routine use of 99 mTc possible in nuclear medicine
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_6ceb7a45f205f5366c4bg-28.jpg?height=763&width=827&top_left_y=722&top_left_x=1615)

\section*{GENITOURINARY IMAGING}

\section*{Dr.B.N.Mugi \\ Consultant Radiologist, Kenyatta National Hospital. Nairobi.}

\section*{GEIVERILINDICATIONS}
¬ß Abdominal (loin) pains
¬ß Abdominal mass
¬ß Haematuria
¬ß Recurrent UTI
¬ß Difficulty in micturition
¬ß Pelvic mass
¬ß Presence of congenital abnormalities ;-
¬ß Vacterl syndrome
¬ß Trauma

\section*{The Pediatric age group}

Congenital abnormalities -
Bear in mind the embryology of the genital urinary system.
¬ß Renal anomalies
¬ß Ureteric anomalies
¬ß Bladder anomalies
¬ß Urethral anomalies
¬ß Other pelvic anomalies
¬ß Acquired abnormalities

\section*{Inaging modalities}

Plain abdominal X-ray
Ultrasound
Radionuclide scan
IVU
MCU
CT Scan
MRI
Arteriography
Interventional procedures
Others

\section*{Inaging modalities}
1. PLAIN ABDOMINAL X - RAY (KUB)

Kidneys surrounded by perirenal fat.

\section*{2, ULTRASOUND}
¬ß Is the investigation of 1st choice
¬ß Absence of ionizing radiation.
¬ß Transducer choice - 5 Mhz sector probe or a higher frequency transducer.
¬ß Approach is trans-abdominal or pelvic
¬ß No major patient preparation required.

\section*{Applications}
¬ß Differentiate normal from abnormal.
¬ß Normal kidney - echogenic renal sinus, lower echo cortex
¬ß In neonates and infants renal cortex is thinner \& has the same echogenicity as the liver.
¬ß After the third month the kidneys have the same appearance as the adult kidney with central echoes and a sonolucent outer cortex.
¬ß The renal pyramids are prominent and sonolucent.
¬ß The size and volume of the kidneys and any mass is accurately measured by ultrasound.
\begin{tabular}{|l|l|}

\hline XLo NP & \\
\hline 0 NP & \\
\hline NPo ZS & \\
\hline o ZST & \\
\hline o ZST & \\
\hline 0 ZST & \\
\hline ZST & DN BCPWF \\

\end{tabular}
¬ß Parenchymal lesions easily demonstrated
¬ß Differentiate cystic vs solid lesions/masses
¬ß Renal vascularity can be determined by Doppler
¬ß Vascular involvement of renal tumors/tumor invasion into renal vein \& IVC
¬ß Involvement of other visceral organs
¬ß Exclude renal vein thrombosis.
¬ß In renal failure measure renal size characterize the echogenicity of the kidneys, presence or absence of obstruction.

\section*{Calculous dr}

\section*{¬ß Detect \& locate radiolucent as well as radioopaque calculous}

\section*{Transplant kidneys}
¬ß Exclude obstruction, urinoma, lymphocoele.

\section*{Post-surgical follow up}
¬ß Re-implanted ureters to exclude obstruction
¬ß Bladder augmentation post reconstruction for extrophy or neurogenic bladder.
¬ß Bladder outflow obstruction
¬ß Check upper tracts for obstruction and document post micturition residue.

\section*{The male pelyis}
¬ß Scrotal anomalies.
¬ß 1) Visualize testes
¬ß 4\% of the testes undescended in term infants, in 1025\% are retractile
¬ß 0-7\% cryptochirdism.
¬ß The testes descend to the scrotum at 7-8 months (in utero) or 4-6wks after birth.
¬ß On U/S homogenous medium level echogenicity spherical or oval in shape ( $7-10 \mathrm{~mm}$ ) undescended small oval or elongated.

\section*{2) Acute scrotum}

\section*{¬ß Testicular torsion Uncommonly in newborn or in - utero}
¬ß Testicular masses
¬ß Tumors
¬ß Haematomas
¬ß Infection - Epididymitis

\section*{The female pelvis}

Ovaries \\ ¬ß -Cysts \\ ¬ß -Malignancies \\ ¬ß -Infections \\ Uterus \& Vagina \\ ¬ß -Congenital anomalies \\ ¬ß -Malignancies \\ ¬ß -Infections \\ ¬ß -Pregnancy
}

\section*{Interyentionsl procedtures}

\section*{¬ß Guide for needle aspiration cytology ¬ß Cyst aspiration ¬ß Needle nephrostomy}

Trauma
¬ß Determine area \& extent of injury

\section*{INTRAY YNOUS UROGRAPENY}
¬ß Is the most valuable investigation of the Urinary Tract
¬ß Excellent anatomical images of the kidneys \& to some extent indication of function.

\section*{Indications}
¬ß Suspected renal or ureteric pathology
¬ß Retroperitoneal pathology

\section*{Contra-indications}
¬ß Previous hx of severe rxn to iodinated CM
¬ß Renal failure with Urea levels $>18 \mathrm{mmol} / \mathrm{l}$

\section*{Puttent Drepuration}

Younger children.
¬ß NBM for at least 4 hrs
¬ß May give enema before the examination
The older children ( $>6 \mathrm{yrs}$ )
¬ß NBM 6-8 hrs prior to examination
¬ß Dulcolax
¬ß Light diet for 3 days
¬ß Well hydrated

\section*{Contrast media}

\author{
¬ß Water sol CM \\ ¬ß Amount-1-1.5mls/kg
}

\section*{Film sequence}
¬ß Preliminary film - KUB, Supine AP renal area.
¬ß Check bowel preparation
¬ß Exposure factors
¬ß Pathology e.g. calcification

\section*{Injection of contrast}
¬ß Bolus - Maximize density of nephrogram

\section*{The immediate film}
¬ß 10-14 sec after injection (Nephrogram)
2-5 min film
¬ß AP renal areas
¬ß Assess excretory function and whether symmetrical or not.
15 min full length
¬ß Assess the pelvi-calyceal system, ureters \& bladder.
¬ß May give a fizzy drink
¬ß Pelvi-calyceal system well outlined against gas filled stomach.
¬ß Bladder view
¬ß After micturition bladder view

\section*{Additional films}

\section*{¬ß Oblique views \\ ¬ß Prone views \\ ¬ß Delayed films (up-to 24 hrs )}

\section*{4. MICU \& ASCENDING UREIFIROGRAIY}

\author{
¬ß Indications \\ ¬ß Contra-indications \\ ¬ß Patient preparation \\ ¬ß Technique
}

\section*{5. C'T SCAN}
¬ß Presence of abundant retro - peritoneal fat.
¬ß Evaluate \& stage renal masses
¬ß Differentiate cyst from tumor when ultrasound is equivocal.
¬ß Exclude small tumors in patients with haematuria \& normal findings on other investigations

\section*{Renal transmal}
¬ß Show extent of injury better than ultrasound

\section*{Lower wringry tracts}
¬ß Stage pelvic tumors.
¬ß Follow-up after surgery, radiotherapy \& chemotherapy

\section*{Calculous disease}
¬ß Precise localization of calculus
¬ß Retroperitoneal pathology, Retroperitoneal fibrosis or tumor
¬ß Filling defects in the pelvi-calyceal system

\section*{6. IXIRI}
¬ß Excellent soft tissue contrast
¬ß Multi-planar capability
¬ß Substitute for CT in patients who cannot tolerate iodinated contrast
¬ß Equivocal CT study

\section*{7, RADIONUCLIDI IYIAGING}

\author{
Indications \\ ¬ß In impaired renal function \\ ¬ß Poor perfusion \\ ¬ß Renal tubular dysfunction \\ ¬ß Obstruction \\ ¬ß Vesico- ureteric reflux \\ ¬ß Infection (Gallium Citrate) \\ ¬ß Dynamic imaging - 99TCM-DTPA \\ ¬ß Static imaging - 99TCM-DMSA
}

\section*{8. $A R^{\prime} I^{\prime} R I O G R A P F M$}
¬ß May be used for embolization
¬ß Study renal arterial anatomy in renal transplant donor
¬ß To exclude renal artery stenosis or thrombosis

\section*{9. INTRRY PNTIONAL PROCIDURDS}
¬ß Retrograde pyelography
¬ß Antegrade pyelography

\section*{10. OPEIPRS}
¬ß Genitogram - Ambigous genitalia
¬ß Colostogram - In ARMs, to rule out fistulous communication to the bladder \& urethra.

<a id="chapter-09"></a>

\title{
INTERPRETING THE CHEST X-RAY
}

\author{
BY: DR. I. MATHENGE
}

DATE: 27th/10/2016

\section*{READING THE CHEST RADIOGRAPH}
t Before you look at a chest film, you should have provisional clinical diagnosis after taking history and physical examination
t The CXR findings should confirm your physical findings rather than be an aid to make your physical findings later

\section*{THE GOLDEN RULES}
1. Technical quality.
2. Chest wall and diaphragm.
3. Heart and mediastinum.
4. Lungs.
5. Review areas.

\section*{CONT.}
t Chest radiographs are done in a postero-anterior (PA) and left lateral positions.
t The PA view is done to minimize cardiac magnification.
t Lateral view is done to allow 3-D interpretation of the chest film findings (see alter).
t In sick patients, an AP chest film is done either supine or upright depending on how mobile the patient is.
t On AP view, it is sometimes hard to assess cardiac enlargement, pleural effusion and lung parenchyma, especially if the patient did not take good inspiratory effort
t When taking a PA X ray, the patient is facing the X-ray film cassette and the X-ray beam enters the patient's back.

\section*{PA VIEW TECHNIQUES}
1. Ensure the film belongs to the patient, the date is correct, the side marker is present
2. Adequate penetration of the mediastinum
$t$ Is the thoracic spine seen?
3. Has the patient taken good inspiratory effort
t About 10 posterior thoracic ribs (7 anterior) should be seen through the lungs.
4. Is there any rotation of the chest
$t$ Assess by checking the upper thoracic spinous process (oval) in relation to the medial ends of the clavicles (lines 'a') this CR is rotated to the left.

\section*{EXPOSURE}
t Overexposure masks opacities in the lung field
t Poor inspiration apparent cardiomegaly

\section*{LEFT LATERAL VIEW}
t The patient's left side is against the film and the X-ray beam travels from the right to the left side.
t This view is used to localize:
$t$ A lung lesion to a specific segment of the lung
$t$ Other pathology if you know your anatomy of the lateral CXR
t Assess cardiac enlargement (Left/right ventricle CXR)
t Hilar enlargement, thoracic spine etc.
t In a normal CXR there is increased trans-radiency as one goes down

\section*{KNOW YOUR ANATOMY}
t For proper interpretation of a CXR, a good knowledge of te anatomy of the lungs and mediastinum is essential.

\section*{LUNGS}
t Compare one hemithorax to the other:
t Size
t Translucency
t Vascular markings
t Superior mediastinum
t Anterior mediastinum
t Middle mediastinum

\section*{IERMINOLOGY USED IN IH EINTERPRETATION OF CXR}
t Acinar pattern
t Round or elliptical ill-defined 4-8 mm opacities in the lung
t Air bronchogram
t Air containing bronchus peripheral to the hilum surrounded by an airless lung
t Reticular pattern
t A collection of innumerable small linear opacities that together produe an appearance resembling a net. The pattern can be fine, medium and coarse
t Reticulo-nodular
t Miliary pattern
t Tiny discrete $\mathbf{2 m m}$ or less, uniform opacities
t Silhouette sign
$t$ The effacement of anatomic soft tissue borders within the thorax or by a process of equal similar density in contiguous lung or pleural e.g. pneumonia in medial segment of the right middle lobe will silhouette the riaht heart border. lingula the left heart

\section*{LEFT TO RIGHT SHUNTS}

Results from abnormal communication between chambers or great arteries
Clinical and radiological features appear with relatively big shunts and high rate of flow across the shunt
If sys:pulm flow is >2:1 patient develops pulmonary plethora; if shunt is extreme (4:1) pulmonary hypertension, heart failure, Eisenmenger reaction (uncommon except in infants)
If flow <2:1 there are neither clinical symptoms nor abnormal radiological features on conventional radiology
Chamber enlangement occurs to the receiving R chamber and those R chambers distal to it
The L chamber shunting blood enlarges (except ASD and some high VSD) together with those L chambers proximal to it

\section*{ASD}

Clinical Features
- most are asymptomatic in infancy and childhood
- presentation frequently > age 40 secondary to benign course
- mildly symptomatic (60\%): dyspnea, fatigue, palpitations, arrhythmias
- severely symptomatic (30\%): cyanosis, heart failure

\section*{ASD}

Radidogical Features
Plain:
- normal (if shunt <2 x systemic blood flow)
- LA not enlarged except in some cases of ECD and Lutembacher syndrome
- loss of visualization of SVC and small appearing aorta with normal aortic knob (due to clockwise rotation of heart due to RV hypertrophy and RA enlargement)
- normal size of LA after shunt reversal (due to immediate decompression into RA) in Eisenmenger syndrome
- enlargement of pulmonary trunk + arteries (pulmonary plethora)

<a id="chapter-11"></a>

CARDIOVASCULAR IMAGING COMPILED BY EFFIE NAILA

\section*{OUTLINE}
1. INTRODUCTION 2. EMBRYOLOGY 3. IMAGING MODALITIES 4. CONGENITAL HEART DISEASES 5. SPECIFIC CONDITIONS

\section*{INTRODUCTION}

The fetal heart develops from a series of complex and rapid changes which occur between the $2^{\text {nd }}$ and $8^{\text {th }}$ week of intrauterine life.
During the $3^{\text {rd }}$ to $5^{\text {th }}$ weeks cardiac structures develop most actively and are most susceptible to adverse external influence e.g. rubella virus or drugs such as thalidomide resulting in congenital heart diseases (CHD).

\section*{EMBRYOLOGY}

The heart originates from the splanchnic mesoderm.
The endocardial tube is the initial basic structure.
The development is characterized by rapid and complex changes
5 dilatations develop in an initially straight longitudinal midline tube. These include:
1. Common truncus arteriosus
2. Bulbous cordis
3. Common primitive ventricle
4. Common primitive atrium

Adu1t derivatives of the primitive heart
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-05.jpg?height=1549&width=3184&top_left_y=318&top_left_x=0)

\section*{NORMAL FETAL CIRCULATION}

Oxygenated blood is carried by the umbilical vein (UV) through the ductus venosus (DV) and IVC into the RA through the foramen ovale.

SVC \& IVC blood is passed into RA, RV \& PA. Most of the RV output passes through the PDA into the descending aorta to supply lower parts of fetus and the umbilical arteries which supply the fetal placental plexus.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-07.jpg?height=1874&width=2684&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=2)

\section*{CHD IMAGING MODALITIES}

Plain radiography
Echocardiography First line choice to assess anatomy and physiology.
Cardiac catheterization For definition of vascular anatomy.
Radionuclide imaging To quantify L- R shunts \& assess relative pulmonary perfusion.
CT For description of cardiac and vascular anatomy in relation to other structures of the chest

\section*{CHEST RADIOGRAPH: \\ INDICATIONS}

Suspected CHD
Cyanosis
Cardiac failure
Failure to thrive
Difficult in feeding in the neonatal period
Fainting snells

\section*{CHEST RADIOGRAPH CONT.}

\section*{Standard views include:}

\section*{1. Erect PA/AP chest}

\section*{2. Left lateral}

The roles of chest radiographs include providing clues to underlying CHD and planning further investigations.
It possesses limited use in neonates and young children and may show related skeletal

CHEST RADIOGRAPH ALLOWS ASSESSMENT OF:

Cardiac and visceral situs situs solitus, situs inversus, situs ambiguous

NB: 2/3 of the heart should be on the left side and $1 / 3$ of the heart should be on the right, if vice versa dextrocardia
Cardiac size \& shape
Mediastinum Aortic arch, PA, tracheal indentation
Pulmonary vasculature e.g. normal, plethora, oliaemia congestion
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-12.jpg?height=1830&width=3266&top_left_y=42&top_left_x=17)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-13.jpg?height=1655&width=1562&top_left_y=57&top_left_x=95)
\$" 3 \%*' \$ 4*, \&. \&" 463 . \& 5

\section*{CARDIAC SIZE MEASUREMENT}

R- max Extension of the heart to the right of the midline
L- max Extension of the heart to the left of the midline
Transverse diameter of the heart $=\mathrm{R}+\mathrm{L}$
The transverse diameter of the heart should be 50-55\% of the maximum transverse diameter of the thorax. It goes up to $60 \%$ in children.

\section*{ECHOCARDIOGRAPHY}

This gives a wealth of anatomical and physiological information without ionizing radiation, patient discomfort or significant risk It is obtained by placing an U/S transducer on the chest wall to image the heart structures as a real- time 2- D slice.
It permits the rapid assessment of cardiac structure and function.
LV wall thickness and EF can be estimated

\section*{TYPES OF ECHOCARDIOGRAPHY}

1- Dimensional (M Mode)
2- Dimensional MC used
3 D
Doppler echocardiography:
CFI; ‚Äònon-invasive‚Äô angiogram
Pulsed doppler echo (valves)
Continuous wave doppler
Contrast echocardiography

\section*{Contrast echocardiography}

This involves the use of IV contrast.
Effects are produced when micro- bubbles of gas present in solution are injected into a peripheral vein or selected area of the heart via a catheter.
Micro- bubbles are generated by the rapid injection of saline, blood, indocyanine.

\section*{INDICATIONS}

Myocardial perfusion can be investigated directly/indirectly after intra- coronary/ venous contrast injection with/without DSA.
Better definition of anatomy not shown well on 2D E.g. imaging the anomalous venous connections to the heart

Enhancement of endocardial boundaries increasing sensitivity of wall motion abnormalities eq valvular requiraitation

\section*{Dopp1er echocardiography}

Used to study venous flow from IVC, SVC, pulmonary vessels and flow across the valves
It allows the measurement of direction and speed of blood flowing through the heart and blood vessels
It enables one to check for abnormal communication between left and right side of the heart and regurgitation
It allows calculation of CO/ EF
Color flow- Doppler:
1. Flow towards the transducer RED
2. Flow away from the transducer BLUE
3. Turbulence flow YELLOW (mixture of red and blue)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-20.jpg?height=1874&width=1978&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=2)
A. Para- sternal long axis view shows regurgitant jet consistent with massive aortic insufficiency
B. Apical 4c view systolic murmur
C. Apical 4c view
severe left vent. Systolic dysfunction. Tricuspid regurgitation is shown
D. Para- sternal long axis shows aorto- right ventricular fistula
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-21.jpg?height=1455&width=1913&top_left_y=157&top_left_x=851)

\section*{CLINICAL APPLICATIONS OF ECHOCARDIOGRAPHY}

Congenital heart disease
Anatomical defects, shunts
Stress echo
Pericardial disease
Coronary artery disease
Cardiomyopathies
Valvular disease
Cardiac masses
Diseases of the aorta

\section*{ANGIO- CARDIOGRAPHY}

This is an important technique for cardiac and great vessel anatomy.
Coronary angiography is the commonest modality.
The trans- femoral approach is done using Judkins or Amplatz catheters
Pulmonary angiography: for possible embolism.
Advantage: offers high resolution images of the heart and great vessels.
Disadvantages:
Requires experienced hands and cardiac monitoring

\section*{CONT.}

\section*{Indications:}

CHD
Valvular/myocardial disease
Ventricular function assessment
Technique;
Right sided structures are studied by introducing a catheter into a peripheral vein, i.e., femoral or ante- cubital or basilica
Left sided structures are studied by introducing a catheter into the femoral artery in retrograde fashion.
CM-LOCM $370 ; 1 \mathrm{ml} / \mathrm{kg}$ at $18-20 \mathrm{mls} / \mathrm{s}$ (adults), $1.5 \mathrm{ml} / \mathrm{kg}$ ( 0 $1 \mathrm{yr}) ; 1.0 \mathrm{ml} / \mathrm{kg}$ ( $>5 \mathrm{yrs}$ )***

\section*{EQUIPMENT}

Equipment:
Fluoroscopy unit on a C-arm with DSA facilities
Pressure recording device
ECG monitor
Blood oxygen analyser
Catheters ( pigtail, NIH)
Cournand catheter for pressure measurements
NB: Angled views which place the pathological lesion at right angles to the X- ray beam increase diagnostic accuracy of cardiography

\section*{COMPLICATIONS}

\section*{1. Sudden death \\ 2. Arrhythmias \\ 3. MI \\ 4. Contrast Media side effects}

\section*{CARDIAC MRI}

Advantages: Provides for the study of anatomy as well as dynamic motion studies of function, flow and chemistry with minimal risk to the patient Disadvantages:

Expensive
Unavailability
Metallic implants pose a challenge

\section*{CLINICAL APPLICATIONS}

Ventricular function analysis
Myocardial function at rest and during stress
Myocardial perfusion
Measurement of coronary flow
Valvular heart disease
Pericardial pathology
Cardiac tumors and thrombi
Cardiomyopathies
Assessing great vessels- aortic/pulmonary disease

\section*{Pu1se sequences used}

Tissue characterization done using:
T1W best anatomic depiction
Blood shows signal void
T2W
Contrast enhancement
Spectroscopy
Gradient echo Impart bright signal to coherently flowing blood (white blood)
Fast spin echo Vascular wall bright; Blood

\section*{C/MRI -cont' d}

Gradient recalled echo-technique applied for motion studies, to show flowing blood as well as cardiac motion
ECG gated MRI of the heart Velocity mapping sequences
Images tomographic slices of any selected plane

\section*{GADOLINIUM AS CONTRAST IS USED IN:}

Myocardial perfusion MR angiography:
Time of flight MRA
Phase contrast MRA
Contrast enhanced MRA
Myocardial infarction Ischemia

Good images of thoracic anatomy and major cardiac structures and large vessels CECT $1^{\text {st }}$ line in imaging aortic rupture or d√≠ssection

Disadvantages:
lonizing radiation

\section*{CARDIAC SCINTIGRAPHY}

Injection of radio- pharmaceutical to study the heart Indications:

Assessment of myocardial perfusion
Ischemia
Infarction
Viability.
Ventricular function
Diagnosis and quantification of abnormal passages

\section*{PERFUSION STUDIES}

Used to diagnose ischemia and m/infarction
Radio- pharmaceuticals used include; Thallium 201
Technetium 99m labeled tracers like tetrofosmin

\section*{TECHNIQUE}

Scans are taken in stress and resting phases Stress is induced by:

Physical exercise test e.g. treadmill or bicycling.
Pharmacologic stress test: dipyridamole, adenosine, dobutamine.
The heart is scanned in longitudinal, vertical and short axes

\section*{PERFUSION SCANS}

\section*{Normal scans:}

Horse- shoe shaped in vertical and long axes

Doughnut- shaped in short axis
Stress and rest images are the same in normal myocardial perfusion
Ischemia normal at rest and reduced at stress

\section*{CLINICAL APPLICATIONS OF RADIONUCLIDE IMAGING (RNI)}

Diagnosis of coronary heart disease
Prognosis of CAD
Myocardial infarction; infarct detection
Myocardial viability and hibernation
Other myocardial disease; alcoholic induced, viral, metabolic
Valve disease
Arrhythmias

Viscero- atrial situs solitus, isolated dextrocardia.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-52.jpg?height=1328&width=1540&top_left_y=314&top_left_x=965)

Situs ambiguous- central cardiac apex, transverse liver.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-53.jpg?height=1345&width=1208&top_left_y=442&top_left_x=1263)

\section*{SITUS INVERSUS}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-54.jpg?height=1226&width=1174&top_left_y=501&top_left_x=1297)

\section*{CHD HAS AN INCIDENCE OF 6-8 PER 1000 BIRTHS}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-55.jpg?height=1307&width=2487&top_left_y=397&top_left_x=521)

\section*{RISK FACTORS OF CHD}
v Genetic
v Downs syndrome-25\%
v Holt-Oram Syndrome
y Ellis van Creveld Syndrome
v Turner's syndrome
v Noonan's syndrome
v Alcohol
v Prenatal Rubella infection, Toxoplasmosis.
v Drugs: Thalidomide toxicity 17\% have a cardiac lesion.
v Gestational diabetes

\section*{Patent Ductus Arteriosus}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-57.jpg?height=1695&width=2935&top_left_y=180&top_left_x=0)

\section*{Patent Ductus Arteriosus}

Persistence beyond 10 days is considered abnormal.

Accounts for 15\% of all CHDs. Commoner in females (M:F 1:2-3) Associated with:

\author{
Low birth weight
}

Prematurity

\section*{CXR}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-59.jpg?height=1498&width=1260&top_left_y=208&top_left_x=731)

\section*{PDA FINDINGS: t Prominent central pulmonary arteries. t Cardiomegaly. t Enlarged left atrial appendage.}

\section*{2D + Color Doppler Echo (PDA)}
ztent ductus arteriosus
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-60.jpg?height=1140&width=1592&top_left_y=510&top_left_x=973)

\section*{Secundum ASD - accounts for 80\%}

Defect at the center
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-61.jpg?height=881&width=669&top_left_y=691&top_left_x=975)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-61.jpg?height=928&width=711&top_left_y=748&top_left_x=1890)

\section*{SECUNDUM ASD}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-62.jpg?height=1459&width=1379&top_left_y=410&top_left_x=442)
t Apical 4 chamber view echo.
t Note the enlarged right chambers.

\section*{ASD BEFORE EISENMENGER}

Note the pulmonary plethora:
Large heart
Large central pulmonary arteries.
Large peripheral pulmonary arteries.
Recruitment of upper zone arteries( Cephalization)
No pulmonary edema.
Patient had ASD.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-63.jpg?height=1544&width=1289&top_left_y=291&top_left_x=2043)

\section*{COMPLICATIONS OF ASD}
1. Eisenmenger syndrome.
2. Enlargement of the right atrium causing tachyarrhythmias.
3. Paradoxical thromboembolic stroke.
4. This patient initially had a large ASD with a major left to right shunt. Note the large central pulmonary arteries with peripheral pruning. Arrow
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-64.jpg?height=1217&width=1421&top_left_y=395&top_left_x=1871) points @ calcified It. pulmonary artery.

\section*{VSDS}

One of the commonest anomaly (2 ${ }^{\text {nd }}$ only to bicuspid aortic valves). Incidence 3570/1m live births.

50\% of patients with VSD have associated cardiac anomaly e.g. coarctation of the aorta. $80 \%$ are peri- membranous \& the vast majority(75\%) of small ones close spontaneously.

\section*{ANATOMY}

The Inter- ventricular septum has two parts, the larger muscular and the smaller membranous part.
The membranous part is the postero-superior region.
Membranous VSDs account for 80\% of all VSDs.
Muscular VSDs tend to close spontaneously.
Its possible to have multiple VSDs
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-66.jpg?height=1307&width=1017&top_left_y=350&top_left_x=2043) at any part of the interventricular septum.

\section*{CONGENITAL CYANOTIC HEART DISEASE}

For clinically detectable central cyanosis, there must be at least 5 g of reduced Hb per 100 ml of aortic blood.

This occurs in 3 ways:
Direct right to left shunt e.g. TOF Transposition of great arteries.
Common chambers e.g. Common atria, common ventricle, truncus arteriosus.

\section*{TETRALOGY OF FALLOT}

Most common cyanotic heart lesion.
Incidence: 3-5 per 10,000 live births.
Primary hypoplasia of infundibular septum secondary to unequal partitioning of the cono- truncus.
Cono- truncal septum displaced anteriorly, and its lowermost component, the Infundibular septum fails to fuse with the top of the IV septum.
Features include:
1. Infundibular right ventricular outflow tract stenosis
2. Subaortic VSD
3. Over-riding aorta

\section*{CONT}

Desaturated blood enters RA, flows through RVOT into lungs.
Some blood shunts right to left through VSD into ascending aorta.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-69.jpg?height=1375&width=1170&top_left_y=299&top_left_x=2162)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-70.jpg?height=1172&width=2461&top_left_y=17&top_left_x=559)

Graphic: sub- valvular (infundibular) stenosis, small pulmonary valve, large aortic valve, over-riding aorta, VSD, RVH, rt arch.
CTA cor- hypoplastic MPA, high VSD, over-riding aorta.

\section*{RADIOLOGY}
"Boot-shaped heart" "coeur en sabot".
Right ventricular hypertrophy.
Concave pulmonary artery segment.
Decreased pulmonary vascularity(oligemia).
$\varnothing$ Normal heart size at birth.
Right aortic arch, mirror image branching (25\%).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-72.jpg?height=1205&width=2489&top_left_y=129&top_left_x=442)

Boot shaped heart, concave pulmonary artery segment.
Right aortic arch, elevated cardiac apex, oulmonarv oliaemia
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-73.jpg?height=1162&width=2527&top_left_y=244&top_left_x=382)

Parasternal long axis- VSD, overriding aorta, CF doppler- riaht to left flow from RV

\section*{Ebstein anoma1y}

Downward displacement of septal \& posterior leaflets of tricuspid valve.
Insufficient separation of tricuspid valve leaflets and chordae tendineae from Rt ventricular endocardium.

Massive tricuspid regurgitation.
Associated anomalies-PFO, ASD in 90\%.
Normal or decreased vascularity
Rt. to Lt. shunt thru PFO causes cyanosis

\section*{CONT.}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-75.jpg?height=1349&width=2803&top_left_y=346&top_left_x=529) RVOT and enter PA.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-76.jpg?height=1184&width=2434&top_left_y=167&top_left_x=450)

Graphic-Downward displacement of posterior valve leaflet, incorporated into RV.
MR cine- Dilated RA, low placement of tricuspid

\section*{IMAGING}

Radiography: Severe right sided cardiomegaly.
Echo: Rt chamber enlargement, enlarged tricuspid annulus, tricuspid regurgitation, PFO with Rt. to Lt. shunting.
Treatment: Tricuspid valve replacement and/ or valvuloplasty
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-78.jpg?height=1022&width=2378&top_left_y=327&top_left_x=474)
t Massive globular cardiomegaly (RA dilatation), pulmonary oligemia.
t DDX: pericardial effusion
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-79.jpg?height=860&width=2536&top_left_y=306&top_left_x=382)
A. Subcostal 4 chamber - displacement of tricuspid valve leaflets inferiorly.
B. Color Doppler- severe regurgitation of the dysplastic tricuspid valve.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_b226802d70e2bead093dg-80.jpg?height=1221&width=2421&top_left_y=210&top_left_x=448)

Mri cine- 16 mm distance btn septal leaflets of MV \& TV(arrow).
CECT- RA dilatation, apical displacement of septal
