<a id="chapter-14"></a>

\title{
Craniocerebral trauma (Head injury)
}

\author{
Dr N. M. Kimani Lecturer DIRM \\ $8^{\text {th }}$ Dec. 2016
}

\section*{Craniocerebral trauma(head injury)}
t Injuries to the skull and brain
-blunt
-penetrating.
t Commonest indications for neuroimaging.
t A major cause of;
- disability,
- morbidity
- mortality
t Has significant direct and indirect financial costs to individuals and society.
t Imaging plays an important role in diagnosis and management.

\section*{Epidemiology}
t Over 10 million people sustain TBIs resulting in death or extensive hospitalization. (WHO;2011 estimates )
t Over 4.5 million deaths occur annually due to TBIs (10\% deaths in the world).
t BY 2020 TBIs will be the leading cause of death and disability.(WHO)
t TBIs account for 6-7\% of all EDvisits (USA, and Canada )

\section*{Kenya situation}
t Over 3,000 Kenyans die as a result of RTA every year and about 40 \% are pedestrians.
t TBI accounts for about 50\% of all RTA deaths
(Saidi, Macharia, \& Atinga, 2005).
t In Kenya, the economic cost of road crashes is 5.6\% of the GDP (about 300 billion KSHS annually).

\section*{NTSA comparison of fatalities 2014/2015}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-05.jpg?height=990&width=2134&top_left_y=433&top_left_x=164)

\section*{CAUSES OF HEAD INJURY}
t Motor vehicle accidents
-commonest cause.
t Assault.
t Firearms ; intentional or unintentional.
t Falls.
t Sport related.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-06.jpg?height=922&width=1206&top_left_y=336&top_left_x=1135)
t Non-Accidental
Trauma in children

\section*{Causes of head injur}
t Motor vehicle accidents -commonest c
t Assault.
t Firearms ; intentional or unintentional.
t Falls.
t Sport related.
t Non-Accidental Trauma in childrer
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-07.jpg?height=761&width=1719&top_left_y=904&top_left_x=546)

\section*{Motor vehicle accidents -commonest} cause
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-08.jpg?height=1503&width=2175&top_left_y=371&top_left_x=176)
t Assault.
t Firearms ; intentional or unintention
t Falls.
t Sport related.
t Non-Accidental Trauma in children
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-09.jpg?height=1588&width=1060&top_left_y=185&top_left_x=1437)

\section*{Traumatic Injuries}

Major Causes of Traumatic Brain Injuries
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-10.jpg?height=929&width=1085&top_left_y=518&top_left_x=644)

Source: National Center for Injury Prevention and Control, CDC

\title{
Review of anatomy
}
t scalp,
t skull
t meninges
t Vascular anatomy of the brain.

\section*{scalp}
t S: Skin(3-8mm)
t C: Connective tissue
t A: Aponeurosis/Galea
t L: Loose connective tissue/subgalea
t P: Periosteum.

These are important for location of haematoma.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-12.jpg?height=1168&width=1330&top_left_y=216&top_left_x=1127)

\section*{Skull}

\section*{t Two layers of cortical bone -inner \& \\ - outer table separated by cancellous bone(diploe)}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-13.jpg?height=1086&width=1401&top_left_y=404&top_left_x=1053)

\section*{Meninges}

Three layers of protective membranes
1. Dura:( 2 layers)

Periosteal-outer layer attached to bone
meningeal: inner layer closer to brain.
The two layers are fused except in areas enclosing dural sinuses.
2. Arachnoid: middle layer 'spider web like.'
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-14.jpg?height=993&width=1152&top_left_y=377&top_left_x=1197)
3. Pia : Attached to brain. Pia and arachnoid form the leptomeninges.

\section*{BRAIN ARTERIAL SUPPLY/TERRITORIES}

Cortical vascular territories
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-15.jpg?height=1197&width=1602&top_left_y=435&top_left_x=245)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-15.jpg?height=1697&width=1088&top_left_y=108&top_left_x=1331)

\section*{Cerebral venous drainage}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-16.jpg?height=1416&width=2297&top_left_y=322&top_left_x=165)

\section*{Imaging modalities}
t Plain skull radiographs (X-Ray): No role in the modern evaluation of TBI.

1/4 of patients with fatal brain injuries have no skull fracture at autopsy.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-17.jpg?height=1155&width=932&top_left_y=625&top_left_x=902)

\section*{NECT}
o Fast, available, sensitive for both bone and brain injury.
o Both bone and soft tissue reconstruction algorithms should be used. Soft tissue reconstructions should be viewed with both narrow ("soft tissue") and intermediate ("subdural") windows.
o 3D reconstruction and curved MIPs of the skull have been shown to improve fracture detection over the use of axial sections alone.

\section*{MRI}

T2* MRI is more sensitive than CT in detecting;
t all stages of ICH,
t non hemorrhagic contusions,
t injuries in the posterior fossa,
t brainstem and
t DAI.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-19.jpg?height=971&width=827&top_left_y=181&top_left_x=1430)

Susceptibility weighted imaging(SWI) MRI for head trauma increases sensitivity for detection of microhemorrhages and hemorrhagic axonal injury.
(sensitive to compounds which distort the local magnetic field and as such make it useful in detecting blood products, calcium)

\section*{IMAGING MODALITIES}

\section*{t ANGIOGRAPHY o (CTA/CTV/MRA/ MRV): Suspected intracranial arterial or venous injury. \\ o Conventional angiography: In inconclusive CT or MR angiography and for intervention.}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-20.jpg?height=1289&width=1313&top_left_y=387&top_left_x=1114)

\section*{CISTERNOGRAPHY}
o High-resolution non contrast skull base CT may be helpful to identify the source of the leak.
o CT or radionuclide cisternography(I-111 DTPA ) may have a secondary role if skull base CT is inconclusive.
o High-resolution MRI may have a role if posttraumatic cephalocele is suspected .
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-21.jpg?height=664&width=2118&top_left_y=1211&top_left_x=296)

\section*{MR CISTERNOGRAPHY}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-22.jpg?height=1024&width=1902&top_left_y=400&top_left_x=235)
t A-defect roof of sphenoid sinus
t B-T1 fat sat GD-DTPA contrast leakage in sphenoid sinus

\section*{Choice of imaging modality}
t Availability
t Cost
t Speed of performance
t Diagnostic information desired
t Clinical circumstances
t Limitation of technique

\section*{WHAT TO LOOK FOR ON IMAGING}
t Mnemonic which is used to read an emergency head CT scan is:

\section*{Blood Can Be Very Bad.}
t B: blood EDH, SDH, IPH,IVH,SAH and extracranial hemorrhage.
t C: cisterns: Presence of blood, effacement and asymmetry in four key cisterns
(circummesencephalic, suprasellar, quadrigeminal and sylvian cistern s).
t B: brain:Asymmetry or effacement of the sulcal pattern, GW matter differentiation, structural shifts and abnormal hypo/hyperdensities.
t V: ventricles:IVH, ventricular effacement/shift and hydrocephalus.
t B: bone:skull fracture.Paranasal sinuses fluid in the setting of trauma should raise the possibility of a skull fracture; intracranial air.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-25.jpg?height=1462&width=1924&top_left_y=200&top_left_x=307)

\section*{SCALP INJURIES}
t Scalp injuries:
Lacerations, hematomas.
t Laceration:
o Variable extent and thickness
o Foreign bodies, subcutaneous air common.
t Hematoma: Hemorrhage in or between scalp layers
o Caput succadenium
o Subgaleal hematoma
o cephalohematoma
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-26.jpg?height=712&width=1007&top_left_y=288&top_left_x=1033)

Fig. 2 a, b. Hemorrhagic cerebral contusions and subgaleal hematoma in a 13 year old boy injured in a motor vehicle accident; a Sagittal spin-echo (SE) T1weighted image; b axial turbo SE T2weighted image. Intracranially, there are hemorrhagic cerebral contusions in the right frontal and anterior temporal lobes. Extracranially, there is marked bilateral swelling of the subcutaneous soft tissues due to a massive subgaleal hematoma which extends from the frontal to the posterior parictal region
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-26.jpg?height=766&width=1458&top_left_y=1036&top_left_x=974)

\section*{CAPUT SUCCADENIUM}
t It is a manifestation of birth
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-27.jpg?height=1544&width=1006&top_left_y=331&top_left_x=1353)

\section*{CEPHALOHEMATOMA}
t Subperiosteal hematoma
o Between outer table of calvaria, periosteum.
o Elevates periosteum.
o Does not cross sutures.
o Extracranial equivalent of intracranial epidural hematoma.
o Usually unilateral.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-28.jpg?height=1267&width=939&top_left_y=368&top_left_x=1399)

\section*{SUBGALEAL HEMATOMA}
t Hematoma under aponeurosis (galea) of occipitofrontalis muscle.
t External to periosteum.
t Not limited by sutures
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-29.jpg?height=1499&width=1312&top_left_y=376&top_left_x=1187)

\section*{t Ball point pen in-situ t Ct scan}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-30.jpg?height=1060&width=1060&top_left_y=449&top_left_x=1345)

\section*{SKULL}

\section*{FRACTURES}
1. Linear skull fracture: Sharply marginated lucent line(s).
2. Depressed skull fracture:

Comminuted fragments imploded inwardly.
3. Elevated skull fracture: Elevated, rotated skull segment.
4. Diastatic skull fracture: Widened suture or synchondrosis. Usually accompanied by linear skull fracture.
5. "Growing" skull fracture.
o Difficult to detect in acute stage.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-31.jpg?height=1704&width=904&top_left_y=89&top_left_x=1353)
o Progressively widening, unhealing fracture.
o Lucent lesion with rounded, scalloped margins.
o CSF and soft tissue trapped within expanding fracture.
o Brain is usually encephalomalacic. 2/3 of patients with moderate TBI have an associated skull fracture.

\section*{t Intra-axial:. Brain parenchyma}
t Extra-axial: outside brain

\section*{t Brain herniation:}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-32.jpg?height=1337&width=1212&top_left_y=205&top_left_x=1253)

\section*{INTRACRANIAL HERNIATION SYNDROMES}
t Subfalcine herniation.
t Descending transtentorial hearniatic
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-33.jpg?height=1268&width=1595&top_left_y=348&top_left_x=899)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-34.jpg?height=1576&width=2214&top_left_y=132&top_left_x=189)

\section*{Subdural hematoma}
t Between inner border cell layer of dura and arachnoid .
t - Crescent-shaped extra-axial fluid collection - May cross sutures, not dural attachments - May extend along falx, tentorium, and anterior and middle fossa floors .

\section*{NECT:}
t Hyperacute(<6hrs)heterogenous/hypodense, Acute-hyperdense,
t Subacute-iso/hypodense\& Chronic-hypodense.
t MRI: Variable signal depending on stage
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-35.jpg?height=1531&width=1130&top_left_y=344&top_left_x=1278)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-36.jpg?height=1053&width=1331&top_left_y=344&top_left_x=68)

Illustration demonstrates the different hematoma ages and manifestations on CT
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-36.jpg?height=460&width=989&top_left_y=1415&top_left_x=1508)

\section*{Traumatic Subarachnoid hemorrhage}
t Trauma is the most common cause(tSAH found in 33\% with moderate TBI, 60\% with severe TBI).
t Location
o Can be focal or diffuse.
o Focal SAH adjacent to contusion,
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-37.jpg?height=669&width=1115&top_left_y=310&top_left_x=1382) subdural/epidural hematoma, fracture, laceration.
o Most common Sylvian fissure, inferior frontal subarachnoid spaces.
o Isolated convexity sulci (adjacent to contusion)
o Diffusely in subarachnoid space \&/or basal cisterns.
o Layering on tentorium
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-37.jpg?height=654&width=1116&top_left_y=1076&top_left_x=1380)

\section*{Subdural Hygroma}
- Extraaxial collection of CSF caused by extravasation of CSF from SA space
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-38.jpg?height=648&width=553&top_left_y=199&top_left_x=1439) through a traumatic tear in arachnoid mater
- Acute: Children >> adults
- Subacute and chronic: Following surgery for head injuries in operative bed or opposite site
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-38.jpg?height=642&width=546&top_left_y=891&top_left_x=1315)

1 week after injury

\section*{PNEUMOCEPHALUS}
t Presence of air or gas within skull in any compartment.
t Mechanism: Dural tear.
t NECT(Best diagnostic tool). Very low density (-1,000 HU).
t MR: Foci of absent signal on all sequences,
t Present in 3\% of all skull fractures, 8\% of paranasal sinus fractures.
t Complications: CSF leak (50\%) Infection (25\%).Mortality 15\%.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-39.jpg?height=1248&width=1075&top_left_y=326&top_left_x=1344)

\section*{Intraparenchymal Hematoma}
- Parenchymal vessel rupture from blunt or penetrating forces
- May not lose consciousness (unlike DAI, contusion)
- Hematoma at primary trauma site (usually frontal and temporal)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_710f9b92bf8db5688eebg-40.jpg?height=1293&width=1254&top_left_y=412&top_left_x=999)

-Vertebrae---24
vertebrae

\section*{Plain film(X-Ray)}
t Most important in spinal trauma
t -Available in any hospital
t -Can be performed without moving the patient
t -Gives best information about dislocation.
t - Important structures such as facet joints in the cervical spine are well shown.
- Can be performed in provocation allowing
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_544de9f9b3983de66e81g-05.jpg?height=1875&width=834&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=1662)
t Traumatic lesions differ on the kind and extent of trauma
t Distinction is drawn from spinal fractures with and without neurological features -important for therapy
t Spinal cord injury
-concussion
-Spinal contusion
-Torn spinal cord
t Fractures and dislocations with /without neurological symptoms
t Hemorrhage
-epidural
- subdural (rare)
t Root avulsion injury .

<a id="chapter-16"></a>

\section*{BREAST IMAGING}

DR AYWAK A.A.

SENIOR LECTURER UON [RADIOLOGY]

\section*{Breast Imaging}

\section*{Learning objectives}

At the end of the lecture you will be able to
- List appropriate imaging modalities available for the breast.
- Recognize need of selecting appropriate modality for each clinical scenario
- recognize indications / contraindications for each modality

\section*{The breast}
- The breast is a symbol of femininity that has a unique anatomical structure to match with its complex physiology and function.
- The course of its development passes through different stages under different hormonal influences.
- This subsequently may result in different disease processes that can be congenital, developmental or acquired through the women's lifetime.
- The latter are the commonest ones and vary from a mild inflammatory process to an advanced malignancy.
- Male breast can also be inflicted especially by cancer.

\section*{THE BREAST}
- The breast is an important organ not only as a mammary gland but also as a sign of beauty in female.
- Unfortunately it is also a source cancer morbidity and mortality the world over.
- Appropriate and early
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-04.jpg?height=763&width=888&top_left_y=578&top_left_x=1265) management of breast diseases is thus invaluable

\section*{The breast gland}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-05.jpg?height=933&width=393&top_left_y=656&top_left_x=403)

Each breast has 15 to 20 sections (lobes) arranged like the petals of daisy

Each lobe has many smaller structures ( ) that ond in tiny sacs ( milb )

Bulbs can produce milk in response to hormonal signals
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-05.jpg?height=297&width=260&top_left_y=1293&top_left_x=801)

\section*{The breast as a nutrition provider}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-06.jpg?height=1050&width=1804&top_left_y=489&top_left_x=113)

\section*{Breast imaging modalities}

Besides physical examination, the following imaging modalities are used to evaluate the breast.
- Mammography film screen and digital
- Ultrasonography
- Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI)
- Computerised Tomography (CT)
- Radionuclide Imaging (RNI)

\section*{NEWER BREAST IMAGING MODALITIES}
- Molecular breast imaging
- Ultrasound elastography
- Breast tomosynthesis
- Dedicated Breast CT mammography

\section*{Ultrasonography}
- Ultrasonography is non invasive and does not use ionising energy. It would have been ideal for screening but is highly operator and technique dependent.
- It may not pick small calcifications commonly seen in early cancers.
- However it has vital role in evaluation of breast masses as shown in the indications below

\section*{Breast Ultrasonography}
- INDICATIONS:

To characterise mammographic or palpable masses as cystic or solid

To evaluate palpable masses in the young(<30yyrs),pregnant and lactating patients

To evaluate non-palpable abnormalities for which mammographic diagnosis is uncertaln

To help exclude a mass thought to be cause an area of mammographic asymmetric density

To guide interventional procedures such cyst aspiration, core biopy, abscess drainage etc.

\section*{ULTRASOUND}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-11.jpg?height=850&width=1375&top_left_y=538&top_left_x=591)

Fig. 1. Diagram shows dock-face locations by breast as used to annotate ultrasound images. (Courtes) of Wendie A. Berg, MD, PhD, FACR, Baltimore, MD. ¬© Copyright 2006.)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-12.jpg?height=1390&width=2485&top_left_y=267&top_left_x=15)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-13.jpg?height=956&width=802&top_left_y=543&top_left_x=460)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-13.jpg?height=1018&width=550&top_left_y=521&top_left_x=1412)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-14.jpg?height=961&width=1602&top_left_y=559&top_left_x=455)

\section*{Mammography}

Indications
i) As a screening tool
currently mammography is the most important imaging modality used as a primary screening fool for breast cancer. The sole purpose of screening is to detect breast cancers early and decrease morbidity and mortality

Effective screening program relies on sensitivity, accuracy, availability affordability and documented high benefit and low risk.
So far Mammography meets most of the above.

\section*{Role of screening}

Survival rate
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-16.jpg?height=684&width=1139&top_left_y=522&top_left_x=789)
Minimal cancer Distant metastases
Negative nodes Positive Nodes
- In a screening program, 310 cancers were diagnosed in a large sample of asymptomatic patients.
- 148 were palpable retrospectively
- 102 of these were in the upper outer quadrant palpable only with the patient upright not supine.

This study underscores importance of thorough physical examination

\section*{Mammography continued}
ii) As a diagnostic tool

Besides further evaluating the palpable mass, mammography can reveal an unsuspected impalpable cancer in a patient with a clinically benign lesion.

It may also show unsuspected cancer in the contra lateral breast.

\section*{Mammography}

In mammography two standard views are done the craino-caudal and the medial lateral oblique
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-19.jpg?height=661&width=795&top_left_y=559&top_left_x=1095)

A and C MLO views

B and D CC view

\section*{Digital mammography}

The x ray film is replaced by solid state detectors that convert x rays into electrical signals.
* The detectors are similar to those found in digital cameras
* clectrical signals are used to produce images of the breast that can be seen on a computer screen or printed on special film similar to conventional mammograms.

\section*{Computer-aided detection (CAD)}
- systems use a digitized mammographic: image that can be obtained from either
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-20.jpg?height=776&width=872&top_left_y=760&top_left_x=1267) a conventional film mammogram or a digitally acquired mammogram.

\section*{Digital mammo v}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-21.jpg?height=642&width=795&top_left_y=648&top_left_x=253)
- On the left, a normal digital mammogram; a normal mammogram from traditional X-ray fllm is on the right
- Digital mammo offers
- Improved contrast resolution, may improve diagnostic capability
- Currently hoth have equal breast radn. dose, however devs in detector design may eventually result in decreased radn dosc for digital mammo.
- several studies have demonstrated no significant difference in cancer detection rates between the two techniques

\section*{Indications}
- Women $>35$ yrs with focal signs
* Screening programes for women > 35yrs(3 yearly):

Women >47-50 yrs (U.K) by request/refferal
Women over 73 yrs by self request
- Screening moderate -high risk of familial CA

Breast CA pt- to exclude multifocal disease/ multicentric / bilateral disease
* Follow-up breast CA
* Metastatic disease of unknown origin

\section*{Mammography contraindications}

Not indicated in:
Asymptomatic women without familial history $<35 \mathrm{yrs}$

Investigation of non-focal signs e.g difluse breast pain.

Assessment of silicon implant integrity

\section*{Mammography contraindications}

Not indicated in:
Asymptomatic women without familial history $<35 \mathrm{yrs}$

Investigation of non-focal signs e.g diffuse breast pain.

Assessment of silicon implant integrity

- Benign

Soft
Smooth outline
Freely mobile
Not fixed to skin

Malignant
Hard
Irregular outline
Poorly mobile
Fixed to skin

\section*{Cranial caudal}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-27.jpg?height=856&width=1579&top_left_y=602&top_left_x=572)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-28.jpg?height=843&width=1800&top_left_y=296&top_left_x=385)

Breast compositioa and its mammographic appearance. ${ }^{1}$
A younger woman has denser or filbro-glandular breasts. Her mammogram will look very white or "cloudy"
Middle-aged women have a mixture of fibrous and glandular tissues ( $\mathbf{5 0 - 5 0}$ breast). Their mammograms look black and white.
In a mature breast, most of the fibrous tissue is replaced with fatty tissue. The mammograms tend to look black or gray (Fatty Replaced hreast)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-29.jpg?height=1244&width=1028&top_left_y=306&top_left_x=704)

Mammogram with cancer (A) and benign lesion (B)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-30.jpg?height=1318&width=939&top_left_y=272&top_left_x=790)

\section*{MRI}
- The indications for MRI are similar to those of ultrasound. Like ultrasound MRI does not use radiation energy but may not be cost effective for mass screening.
- MRI is used as problem solving in dense breast or the very young at high risk as a screening tool
d.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-34.jpg?height=826&width=2047&top_left_y=546&top_left_x=216)

\section*{Newer breast imaging modalities}
- Molecular breast imaging.
- Breast tomosythesis.
- Ultrasound elastography.
- Dedicated CT

Molecular breasting imaging (MBI)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-36.jpg?height=862&width=881&top_left_y=545&top_left_x=363)

Images show a cancer that would have been missed on mammo identified on MBI
- Molecular breasting imaging (MBI) is a method for detecting breast cancer.

The technique is also known as a Miraluma (pronounced mee-ra-LOOma) test, sestamibi (pronounced ses-ta-Mlbee), scintimammography. or breast specific gamma imaging.

\section*{Breast Tomosythesis}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-37.jpg?height=753&width=842&top_left_y=628&top_left_x=145)
mammo
- Breast tomosynthesis takes multiple images of the entire breast. It allows our specialized breast radiologists to see through layers of tissue and examine areas of concern from all angles. Benefits can include:
- Earlier detection of small breast cancers that may be hidden during digital mammography
- Grealer accuracy in pinpoinling size, shape and location of abonormalilies
- Fewer unnecessary biopsies or addilional lests
- Grealer likelihood of delec ling ruulliple breost tumors, which occur in 15\% of broast cancor pationts
- Clearor images of dense breast tissue

\section*{Ultrasound elastography}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-38.jpg?height=457&width=604&top_left_y=454&top_left_x=355)

Benign lesion
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-38.jpg?height=380&width=601&top_left_y=1063&top_left_x=334)

Malignant lesion

Breast elastography is a new sonographic imaging technique which provides information on breast lesions in addition to conventional ultrasonography (US) and mammography.

Elastography provides a noninvasive evaluation of the stiffness of a lesion. Today, two technical solutions are available for clinical use: strain elastography and shear wave elastography.

Elastography substantially improves the possibility of differentiating benign from malignant breast lesions thereby limiting recourse to biopsy and considerably reducing the number of benign breast biopsy diagnoses.

\section*{DEDICATED BREAST CT AS A DIAGNOSTIC TOOL}
- Dedicated breast CT breast imaging can be used:

Adjunctive screening
Diagnostic evaluation
Guiding biopsies
extent of disease evaluation
Monitoring response to neoadjuvant chemo.
Breast CT could become an important imaging tool.
- The radiation dose from breast CT is similar to, and is within the range of, conventional diagnostic mammography
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_bdc4ec32775d3057118fg-39.jpg?height=333&width=872&top_left_y=398&top_left_x=1286)

\title{
Imaging of the CNS can be fun!
}

\section*{Dr Alfred Odhiambo}

\section*{Fellow, Neuroradiology MIR}

\author{
(USA)
}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_d555cccf124ab7e42d5ag-01.jpg?height=1314&width=942&top_left_y=440&top_left_x=1557)

\section*{Fascination with the brain......}

The brain and the brain alone is the source of our pleasures, joys, and amusement, as well as our sorrow, pain, grief and tears. It is especially the organ we use to think and learn to identify the ugly from the beautiful. The brain is also the seat of madness, delirium, sleeplessness and awkward thoughts that result in eccentricities.

Hippocrates, 400BC.

\section*{But it can be challenging....}

\section*{"One difficulty in understanding the brain is that it is like so much as a lump of porridge".}

From eye brain; by R.L. Gregory.

\section*{The Brain - Lateral View}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_d555cccf124ab7e42d5ag-04.jpg?height=1404&width=2498&top_left_y=376&top_left_x=1)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_d555cccf124ab7e42d5ag-10.jpg?height=1573&width=2277&top_left_y=259&top_left_x=102)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_d555cccf124ab7e42d5ag-12.jpg?height=1366&width=2498&top_left_y=385&top_left_x=1)

\section*{Fibre tracts}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_d555cccf124ab7e42d5ag-13.jpg?height=1606&width=2321&top_left_y=262&top_left_x=99)

\section*{Neuroradiology today.}
- Most neuroradiology examinations now consist of crossectional imaging.
- CT and MRI steal the limelight.
- Plain radiography is assuming a historical role.
- This does not take away the responsibility of radiologists of being familiar with plain films of the skull.

\section*{Innovations rampant.}
- Non invasive vascular imaging techniques such as MRA and CTA now compete with catheter cerebral angiography replacing it for many indications.

\section*{Plain radiography.}
- Only radiographs of the highest quality are acceptable in clinical neuroradiology.
- This is even more vital now that specific non invasive methods such as CT and MRI are in contention.
- Positioning and film screen choices must be of the highest ebb.

\section*{The standard views.}
- In many clinical situations the lateral skull film suffices.
- The full complement will also include;
1. OF
2. Townes.
3. SMV.

\section*{Regions of the skull.}

For anatomical purposes the basal portion of the skull is divided into 3 fossae;
- Anterior.
- Middle.
- Posterior.

\section*{Anterior cranial fossa.}
- This is the space above the orbital roofs.
- It is anterior to the ridge formed the greater and lesser wings of the sphenoid.
- It contains the frontal lobes and the olfactory bulbs as well as the tracts.

\section*{Middle cranial fossa.}
- It is posterior to the sphenoid ridge on either side of the basisphenoid.
- It is bounded laterally by the squamous temporal bone and postero-inferiorly by the petrous ridge.
- It contains the temporal lobes.

\section*{The posterior cranial fossa.}
- Comprises all the space below the tentorial hiatus and above the foramen magnum.
- It is bounded by the clivus, basisphenoid and basiocciput in the midline.
- Anterolaterally is the posterior surface of the petrous bone and elsewhere is the occipital bone.

\section*{Routine CT of the head}
- Conventionally carried out in the axial plane.
- Centering parallel to the orbital roof will minimize radiation to orbital contents.
- Must optimize slice thickness and window settings.

\section*{Head injury.}
- Imaging should include bone review modes.
- Spiral (volume) would be of value in the restless patient but also allows multiplanar reformation.

\section*{The pituitary gland.}
- Use 2 to 3mm cuts with narrower FOV.
- Coronal views add additional information.
- Dental amalgam can be a problem.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_d555cccf124ab7e42d5ag-24.jpg?height=1875&width=1161&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=1178)

\section*{The craniocervical junction.}
- Best imaged by MRI.
- Indications will be subject to local requirements.
- Investigation of this region is not routine.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_d555cccf124ab7e42d5ag-25.jpg?height=1423&width=1369&top_left_y=452&top_left_x=1127)

\section*{Protocol.}
- Axial plane is approprite using 2 mm or less in slice thickness in spiral or multislice modes.
- MPR with shaded surface rendering can be very useful in interpretation.

\section*{Intravenous contrast.}
- NECT should be done first.
- Dose ranges between 15 to 30grams.
- Most select cases for iv contrast.
- Indwelling catheters advisable.

\section*{CT cisternography.}
- Main role today is to show site of CSF leak.
- Patients must be leaking at the time of the examination.
- Pressure over jugulars enhances chances of success.

\section*{Cerebral perfusion.}
- Two methods are available for assessing blood flow;
1. Xenon inhalation.
2. Bolus iodine.

\section*{Xenon.}
- This is a stable gas with an atomic number close to that of iodine.
- Attenuates an xray beam in a similar fashion.
- Unlike iodine xenon is freely diffusable and penetrates BBB.

\section*{Perfusion CT.}
- Tracks transient attenuation changes in blood vessels and brain parenchyma during first passage of iv CM.
- Images taken at 1 to 2 second intervals.
- Can obtain maps of cerebral blood flow, mean transit time and cerebral blood volume.

\section*{Stating the basics of CT.}

CT interpretation is most rewarding when systematic.
- The principles can be mastered rapidly with practise.
Especially in the brain anatomic depiction is most basic.
- CT usually sees so little.
- The findings must be correlated with clinical pointers to arrive at the diagnosis. This statement is not negotiable.

\title{
There are 3 fossae.
}

Anterior.
Middle.
Posterior.

\section*{L 10vv alooul lobes?}
-Frontal.
Parietal.
-Temporal.
-Occipital.
- Insular.

\section*{Its no quantum leap to add the side!}

\section*{Right.}

Left.

\section*{And now some attenuations.}

The yardstick is the cortex.
- Lesions may be iso-dense hyper-dense or hypo-dense.
-Memorizing Hounsefield units is a waste of inter-neuron circuitry.
- CSF pathways provide useful leads.
-Finally assess mass effect and contrast uptake and you are off the ramp.

\section*{increased censty at} CT.

\section*{Signifies cellular compactness of lesion.}

\section*{Other causes include recent hemorrhage calcifications and Fibs.}
-Within a cyst high density reflects higher protein content.

\section*{causes or oecreasea c 1 density.}
- Increased water content. Common with tumors including inflammations.
- When seen around the main lesion it is called edema.
- Interstitial micro-cysts are a feature of some tumors.
- Necrosis causes decreased CT density.
- Contained fat or air.

\section*{enhancement.}

Reflects vascularity and degree of breakdown of BBB .
- Iodine is attached to macromolecules so that CE is proportional to the amount of macromolecules that pass into the lesion.
- For intra-axial lesions this signifies disruption of the BBB while for extra-axial lesions contrast accumulates in sinusoids and other vascular pools in the lesion.

\section*{nat is the structure or the BBB?}

It consists of tight junctions between the capillary, the glial footplate that wraps around the capillary and various transport mechanisms in both the capillary endothelial and glial cells.

\section*{And the tenth rule is as basic as the first.}

Look at your CT images before eyeballing the report if for nothing else but at least confirm it belongs to the patient.

\section*{Brain MRI.}
- Dedicated head coil is essential for most indications.
- Axial images should use the same baseline as CT.
- Section thickness is 4 to 5 mm .
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_d555cccf124ab7e42d5ag-67.jpg?height=941&width=1280&top_left_y=569&top_left_x=1216)

\section*{Standard study.}
- Scout.
- T1WI.
- T2WI.
- Proton density.
- FLAIR.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_d555cccf124ab7e42d5ag-68.jpg?height=1212&width=1586&top_left_y=663&top_left_x=910)

\section*{Claustrophobia.}
- Arrange for patient to visit department before booking.
- Oral sedation useful on day of study.
- Alternatively GA may be considered.
- I.V sedation in cage is not advisable.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_d555cccf124ab7e42d5ag-69.jpg?height=817&width=1465&top_left_y=1023&top_left_x=489)

\section*{MRI - Interpretation.}

More complex due to multiple parameter
> Proton density
> T1
> T2
> Chemical shift
> Susceptibility
- Diffusion
- Perfusion
> rf absorption
> Flow
> T1 in rotating frame

\section*{GBM}
- CECT: nodular rim enfancement

\section*{BSG}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_d555cccf124ab7e42d5ag-74.jpg?height=1611&width=2495&top_left_y=145&top_left_x=1)

Various pathologies increase proton density:-
These are:
- edema
- infection
- inflammation
- acute hemorrhage
- some tumors, cysts. Etc.

Decrease in proton density is seen in
> scar formation
> filbrosis
> some tumors
capsule /membrane formation
> Calcification.

\section*{Can see vessels without intravascular contrast agents}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_d555cccf124ab7e42d5ag-77.jpg?height=1006&width=2303&top_left_y=377&top_left_x=156)

\section*{Head to toe in 2 minutes}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_d555cccf124ab7e42d5ag-78.jpg?height=1694&width=1510&top_left_y=180&top_left_x=495)

\section*{DWI}
- Exploits principle of Brownian motion of water molecules to produce image contrast.
- Achieved by applying a pair of diffusion sensitizing gradients.
- Mobile molecules acquire phase shifts which prevent their complete rephasing so that there is signal loss.

\section*{Appearance.}
- Areas of diffusion restriction appear bright.
- The same areas with decreased diffusion are dark on ADC.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_d555cccf124ab7e42d5ag-80.jpg?height=900&width=1943&top_left_y=975&top_left_x=215)

\section*{MR perfusion.}
- Exploits magnetic susceptibility effects within the brain tissue during first pass of an intravenously injected gadolinium based contrast agent.
- During fast pass through the brain CM causes a transient signal drop on T2* MRI.
- Images are acquired with a temporal resolution of every 1to 2seconds similar to CT perfusion.

\section*{MR and MR perfusion.}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_d555cccf124ab7e42d5ag-82.jpg?height=891&width=2498&top_left_y=562&top_left_x=1)
gold
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_d555cccf124ab7e42d5ag-83.jpg?height=1589&width=1452&top_left_y=286&top_left_x=680)

<a id="chapter-20"></a>



\section*{OBJECTIVES}
- Describe the modalities available for GIT imaging
- Indications er contraindications and patient preparation for each modality
- Limitations of the described imaging modalities
- Examples of pathological processes demonstrated in each modality
- Bring our a decision making protocol for imaging in acute abdomen setting

\section*{ANATOMICAL DIVISIONS}
- Fore gut: Oral cavity, pharynx, esophagus, stomach, part of duodenum
- Mid gut: Part of duodenum, small bowel (jejunum, duodenum), part of large bowel
- Hind gut: Mid - transverse, descending, sigmoid colon
- Accessory structures

\section*{GIT PATHOLOGY}
- Congenital anomalies
- Inflammation
- Obstruction
- Neoplasia
- Trauma
- Motility disorders
- Vascular
- metabolic

\section*{INTRODUCTION}
- There are pathological conditions that one cannot see on imaging despite their presence due to:
- Wrong imaging modality/technique
- Disease is only microscopic and has not induced any visible anatomical change unless the imaging method is functional

\section*{MODES OF GIT IMAGING}
- Plain radiography
- Contrast radiography
- Ultrasound
- CT scan
- MRI scan
- Radionuclide imaging (Nuclear medicine)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_4f41c3911a3c006f938eg-06.jpg?height=277&width=282&top_left_y=1197&top_left_x=3010)
- Angiography

\section*{PLAIN RADIOGRAPHY}
- They are frequently still the $1^{\text {st }}$ investigation in the acute abdomen
- For non - acute situations, the relatively small yield of information from a plain radiograph does not justify the radiation dose that it entails, unless it is on follow up basis
- Plain radiographic findings must corroborate with clinical findings: sometimes even obvious signs can be non - specific generating a plethora of differential diagnoses. A good example is the presence of air - fluid levels which may be benign or a pointer to intestinal obstruction
- NOTE: normal air fluid levels:

\section*{PLAIN RADIOGRAPHY PROJECTIONS}
- Supine AP projection
- Patient lies supine with the source of $X$ rays from above
- Dorsal decubitus positioning
- Patient lies supine with the source of $X$ rays from the side
- Allows visualization of air - fluid levels
- Left-lateral decubitus positioning
- Patient lies on the left side with the source of $X$ rays from the side.
- If patient lies on the right, the stomach will be on the fighter plane bringing about confusion about whether or not there is an air - fluid level

\section*{THE DENSITIES}
- Air: $\square$ very dark $\square$
- Fat:
- Soft tissues (including water): light grev (1)
- Calcified structures: white $\square$

\section*{ABDOMINAL X - RAY: SYSTEMIC APPROACH}
- Check the patient details
- Assess quality (including presence of artefacts)
- Bowel gas
- Soft tissues
- Bones
- Abnormal calcifications

\section*{AXR: AIR DISTRIBUTION (INTRALUMINAL)}
- Normal
- Gastric bubble (erect film)
- Large bowel
- Very minimal or non - detectable in small bowel
- Checkfor abnormalities
- Size
- Distribution:
- Peripheral, taenia coli markings that do not go the entire circumference $\square$ Large bowel
- Central, valvulae conniventes throughout the circumference [ small bowel

\section*{AXR: AIR DISTRIBUTION (EXTRALUMINAL)}
- Always abnormal
- Key areas to look at
- Su6 - diaphragmatic (erect film)
- Bowel wall
- Liver (from the portal system or the bile duct)

\section*{VARIOUS ABDOMINAL CONDITIONS}
- Paralytic ileus
- Sigmoid volvulus
- Meteorism: dilatation of the bowel tending towards a hexagonal shape (aerophagy)

\section*{CONT.}
\begin{tabular}{|l|l|l|}
\hline FEAIVURE & SOMLE BOWEL OBSTRYICITON & CARGE BOWEL OBSTRRICITON \\
\hline BOWEL DIAMEIER & >3AND <5 & >5 \\
\hline POSITION OF LOOPS & CENIRAL & PERIPHERAL \\
\hline NUMBEROF LOOPS & MANO & FEW \\
\hline FLUID LEVELS (ON ERECT FILM) & MANY, SHORT & FEW, LONG \\
\hline BOWEL MARKINGS & VALVULAE (ALL THE WAY ACROSS) & HAUSTRA (PARTIALLY ACROSS) \\
\hline LARGE BOWEL GAS & NO & IES \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

\section*{PNEUMOPERITONEUM (20 TO PERFORATED GUT)}
- Air under the diaphragm
- Rigler's sign: double wall sign (too beautiful to be true)

\section*{PNEUMATOSIS INTESTINALIS}
- Air within the intestinal wall

AIR SHALL ONLY BE XEPT IN TYE BOWEL FORIT TO BE CONSIDEREED NORMAL AND IN THE RIGHET PROPORTIONS

\section*{GIT CONTRAST RADIOGRAPHY}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_4f41c3911a3c006f938eg-18.jpg?height=626&width=2954&top_left_y=695&top_left_x=0)

\title{
GIT CONTRAST RADIOGRAPHYMODALITIES
}
- Sialography: study of salivary glands using positive contrast
- Ba. Swallow: used to study the esophagus
- Ba. Meal: used to study the esophagus, stomach and duodenum
- Ba. Meal follow through: used to study the esophagus, stomach, duodenum, jejunum or ileum
- Small bowel enema (enterochysis): place tube at the duodeno - jejunal junction and introducing the fluid into the small bowel

\section*{CONT.}
- Barium enemas introducing fluid in retrograde function into the large bowel
- Fistulography: introducing contrast into the fistula. Sinography: int
- Fistula vs. sinus: Sinus is blind - ending while a fistula is connecting to epithelial surfaces
- Sinogram
- Colostography: studying the colostomy using contrast

\section*{SIALOGRAPHY}
- Cannulation of major salivary ducts
- Water soluble contrast media is used
- Demonstrates luminal anatomy and pathology

\section*{BARIUM SWALLOW INDICATIONS}
- Dysphagia
- Odynophagia
- Hematemesis
- Assessment of trachea - esophageal fistula (where lain films not diagnostic)
- Follow up of following esophageal surgery
- N(B: in assessment of perforation, water soluble contrast is used

\section*{NORMAL FINDINGS}
- Physiological constrictions may be seen
- Cricopharyngeus muscle: at the start of the esophagus
- Aortic arch
- Left main bronchus
- Left atrium
- Gastro - esophageal junction

\section*{ABNORMAL FINDINGS}
- Achalasia (seen on single contrast)
- Esophageal webs (seen on double contrast)
- Esophageal candidiasis (seen on double contrast)
- Multiple small ulcers will appear mottled
- Hiatal hernia (seen on double contrast)
- Know the types***
- Esophageal carcinoma (double contrast): irregular strictures
- Esphageal varices (double contrast): serpiginous contrast

\section*{BARIUM MEAL}
- Double contrast examination of choice in adults. Single contrast in children and ill
- Indications
- Dyspepsia
- Weight loss
- Follow up after surgery
- Suspected GI bleeding or unexplained iron deficiency

\section*{CONTRAINDICATIONS}
- Complete large bowel obstruction
- Suspected perforation (50\% mortality if barium leaks into the peritoneum)
- Investigation of choice in suspected perforation: plain radiography

\section*{CONT.}
- Stomach ulcer on barium meal: barium meals pools within the crater of the ulcer with the mucosal folds radiating towards the crater
- Barium meal has some sensitivity in demonstrating stomach ulcers (not as high as endoscopy)
- Duodenal ulcer (pooling)

\section*{BARIUM MEAL FOLLOW THROUGH}
- Indications
- Pain
- Suspecting chronic malabsorption
- Reason to think of malignancy
- Bleeding
- Partial obstruction
- Contraindications
- As for Ba meal

\section*{CONT.}
- Ascariasis: filling defects within the small bowel outlined by the barium

\section*{SMALL BOWEL ENEMA (ENTEROCLYSISI)}
- Better visualization of small bowel than that achieved by barium meal follow through
- disadvantage unpleasant to the patient due to intubation and time consuming to the radiologist
- Indication: same as for barium meal follow through

\section*{BARIUM ENEMA}
- Examination of large bowel
- Currently getting replaced by CT colonography
- 2 methods:
- Double contrast: examination of choice to demonstrate mucosal pattern
- Single contrast: used in children since. To reduce an intussusception
- Contrast introduced per rectal
- Change in bowel habits
- Unexplained abdominal pain
- Melena
- Onstruction e.g. intusussception

\section*{CONTRAINDICATIONS (HIGH RISK OF PERFORATION)}
- Toxic megacolon
- Pseudomembranous colitis
- Rectal biopsy witfin previous 3 days

\section*{ABNORMAL FINDINGS}
- Colon cancer (apple core deformity)
- Colonic polyp
- Ulcerative colitis
- Featureless colon
- Mottling
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_4f41c3911a3c006f938eg-34.jpg?height=269&width=278&top_left_y=1201&top_left_x=3008)

\section*{ULTRASOUND IN GIT IMAGING}
- Limited value (gas produces poor sound penetration)
- indications
- Suspected mass
- Peritoneal fluid
- Calculus
- Appendicitis
- Intussusception
- Hernia
- Jaundiced patient

\section*{CONT.}
- Esophageal cancer: hypoechoic areas
- Hypertrophic pyloric stenosis
- Intussusception
- Appendicitis
- Liver metastases from colonic cancer
- Gall bladder cancer
- CI scan volume rendering
- CI scan virtual endoscopy

<a id="chapter-21"></a>

ABDOMINAL AND PELVIC MASSES IN PEDIATRICS.

\section*{Level IV}

\section*{1. LIVER}

Causes of hepatomegaly include:-
- Diffuse enlargement e.g. hepatic vascular congestion in RHF, fatty liver in malnutrition, malabsorption , leukemia/lymphoma infiltration, liver secondaries, hepatitis
- focal causes e.g. solid tumor- malignant hepatoblastoma, hepatic carcinoma
- Benign- hemangioma, hamartoma, adenoma
- Cystic lesions- hydatid cysts, amoebic/pyogenic abscesses e.t.c.
- primary liver tumors account for 2\% of all malignancies.
- Hepatoblastoma arises from the primitive hepatocyctes. Usually presents in infancy and is rare over 5 years.

\section*{Dxl imaging liver mases}
- Abdominal mass often with marked distension in RUQ
- Plain film- enlarged liver outline, calcification in hepatoblastoma
- US- solid or cystic lesion lesion, biliary obstruction, hemangiomas are echogenic with acoustic enhancement
- CT- size of lesion, evidence of local invasion, extrahepatic metatases
- Tumor markers- $\alpha$ - fetoproteins are elevated
- Tissue diagnosis- US guided core biopsy especially in diffuse disease, or open biopsy in focal lesions
2. SPLEEN

Splenomegaly can be seen in:-
- Infections;
- bacterial- brucellosis, Tuberculosis, Subacute bacterial endocarditis
- viral- infectious mononucleosis
- parasitic- malaria, kalazar, schistosomiasis, hydatid disease
- fungal- histoplasmosis
- glycogen storage disease- Gaucher's
- RES disorders - histiocytosis, leukemia, lymphoma
- Blood disorders- chronic anemias (e.g. hemolytic, pernicious, sickle cell) polycythemia, myelofibrosis
- Portal hypertension- hepatic fibrosis, schistosomiasis

\section*{Diagnosis/ imaging- spleen}
- Plain films- LUQ soft tissue mass
- US for echo pattern- normal spleen is homogenous echoes, can be heterogeneous in infiltrative disorders, hypoechoeic lesions in lymphoma, leukemia
- CT - in staging lymphoma as assessment of liver, par-aortic nodes can also be done

\section*{3. BILIARY SYSTEM}
- Choledochal cyst; commonly occurs as a fusiform dilatation of the CBD
- M:F ratio = 1:4
- Rarely presents before 6 months
- Clinical presentation- obstructive jaundice, upper abdominal swelling (RUQ) which is cystic on US
- Untreated can be fatal fron ascending cholangitis, biliary cirrhosis, biliary peritonitis in cyst rupture, malignant change

\section*{4. MESENTERIC CYSTS}
- Are uncommon. More seen in the 2nd decade of life; Are rare between 1-10 years and are exceptional in under 1 year.
- Clinical presentation- painless abdominal swelling that moves freely when at right angles to the mesentery, acute abdomen in torsion, rupture, hemorrhage or infection.
- Include:-
- chylolymphatic/ lymphangiomas
- enterogenous
- urogenital remnants
- dermoids
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_72b39936526bf491dc20g-08.jpg?height=1535&width=1140&top_left_y=157&top_left_x=157)

UPJ obstruction
pelvic dermoid cyst

\section*{Mesenteric cysts;cont.}
- lymphangioma- are the commonest. Arise from the mesentery of the ileum. Has a thin wall of endothelium and is filled with clear fluid.
US- thin walled cyst.
- Enterogenous- Develop as a diverticulum at the mesenteric border of the intestine which separates in embryonic life or as a duplication of the intestine. Has a thicker wall and is lined by mucous membrane which is sometimes ciliated. Contents are mucinous, occasionally brownish from previous hemorrhage.
US- thick walled cyst with echogenic contents of varied appearance

\section*{5. OTHER CYSTS}
include;
- omental cysts- rare
- cysts of the mesocolon
- urogenital cysts from the Wolfian/ Mullerian remnant
6. GIT- INTUSSUSCEPTION
- presents with acute colicky abdominal pain, palpable abdominal mass

\section*{7. KIDNEYS}
- Are the commonest causes of abdominal mass in children.
- Abnormalities include- ectopic kidney 1:1000can be pelvic hence easily palpable
- Horseshoe kidney
- Polycystic kidneys
- Hydronephrosis- UPJ obstruction, posterior urethral valves, ureterocele
- Wilm's tumor- rapidly enlarging abdominal mass. D/dx neuroblastoma
- Multicystic renal dysplasia

\section*{Multicystic renal Dysplasia}
- non hereditary, congenital
- is usually unilateral
- Is the commonest cause of abdominal mass in the newborn.
- There is failure of the ureteric bud to join with the renal blastema. Resultant mass resembles a cluster of grapes.
- US- large multiloculated; predominantly cystic mass D/dx severe hydronephrosis
- The contralateral kidney has increased incidence of congenital anomalies e.g. UPJ obstruction in 30-40 \% of patients
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_72b39936526bf491dc20g-14.jpg?height=814&width=1005&top_left_y=46&top_left_x=113)
- Hydronephrois
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_72b39936526bf491dc20g-14.jpg?height=913&width=1018&top_left_y=886&top_left_x=116)
- Polycystic/Multicystic renal disease

\section*{Mesoblastic Nephroma}
- fetal hamartoma seen within the first months of life.
- Is benign.
- D/dx- hydronephrosis, multicystic kidney, Wilm's tumor (rare in neonate)
- Plain film- large soft tissue mass usually without calcifications in the renal area
- US- well defined mass with low echoes
- IVU- distorted, displaced calices due to parenchymal involvement
- Angiography- displaced aorta, renal and lumbar arteries with neovascularisation in the mass (tumor blush0 which may be hyper or hypovascular.
- CT- low attenuation mass with enhancement of capsule and septa with contrast.

\section*{Wilm's Tumor( nephroblastoma)}
- is the most common solid abdominal mass in childhood beyond the neonatal period.
- Peak age 3-4 years.
- $90 \%$ are diagnosed before the age of 7 years
- clinical presentation- palpable abdominal mass, abdominal pain, hypertension, irritability and rarely haematuria
- bilateral disease is seen in 5-10\%
- association with aniridia, hemi hypertrophy present.
- plain film- calcifications, soft tissue mass displacing bowel loops
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_72b39936526bf491dc20g-17.jpg?height=859&width=772&top_left_y=81&top_left_x=220)
- Splayed calices
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_72b39936526bf491dc20g-17.jpg?height=814&width=1117&top_left_y=1015&top_left_x=159)
- Renal tumor

\section*{Wilm's Tumor; cont.}
- plain film- calcifications, soft tissue mass displacing bowel loops
- IVU- distorted pelvicalceal system; rarely a nonexcreting renal mass is seen
- US- predominantly well marginated, large solid mass with a 'halo' effect. Purely cystic tumors are rare
- CT- solid mass, large spherical which arises intrarenally and splays, distorts the remaining renal tissue. Has low attenuation, calcifications- more easily seen on CT than on plain film. Prominent pseudocapsule. CT can help to differentiate from neuroblastomas especially in upper pole involvement
- MRI- staging

\section*{Lymphoma}
- non-Hodgkin's lymphoma
- may present with diffuse renal enlargement on IVU or with renal masses (single/ multiple)
- hydronephrosis is seen in ureteric involvement/ compression in retroperitoneal adenopathy
- angiography- avascular masses
- US- hypoechoeic or anechoeic renal lesions, large renal volumes
- CT - retroperitoneal adenopathy

\section*{Polycystic renal disease}
- Autosomal dominant polycystic disease-(ADPD) autosomal recessive polycystic disease (ARDP)
- Difficult to differentiate the two on imaging
- ARDP- hyperechoeic large kidneys with loss of corticomedullary differentiation, dilatation of distal collecting ducts. Is associated with hepatic fibrosis. Hypertension is a common complication. Presents at 2-5 years
- IVU- streaking of contrast in the renal parenchyma on delayed films
- ADPD- has bilateral asymmetrical renal cysts. Associated with cysts in the liver and pancreas. D/dx- tuberous sclerosis

\section*{Multilocular cysts of the kidney}
- are rare; present with an abdominal mass in late infancy/ early childhood
- US- transonic areas with septations
- Malignant risk hence nephrectomy is recommended.

\section*{8. ADRENAL GLANDS}

\section*{Neuroblastomas}
- commonest extracranial malignant solid tumor in childhood. 55\% arise in the abdomen of which $2 / 3$ involve the adrenals
- clinical presentation- may present with metastases, weight loss, pyrexia, irritability, proptosis, bone pain

\section*{Neuroblastoma; cont.}
- US- mixed echogenicity mass from calcifications
- CT- solid mass involving adrenals. Shows extent, liver, IVC or neural canal involvement
- MRI- useful for imaging of the spinal cord
- IVU- downward and lateral displacement of kidneys by tumor
- Plain film- stippled / amorphous calcification, displaced bowel loops, widening of retroperitoneual tissues, enlarged intervertebral foramina
- 99 mTc -MDP bone scan for extent of bone involvement.
9. URINARY BLADDER

Rhadbomyosarcoma
- 1/8th of childhood tumors
- peak incidence in genitourinary system involvement is seen at 1-8 years
- usually is the embryonal subtype; the sarcoma botryoides has a characteristic grapelike macroscopic appearance
- bladder is a common site of involvement; affects boys > girls
rhabdomyosarcoma;cont.
- clinical feature- obstruction, urgency, haematuria
- US- outline primary tumor
- CT for staging
- CXR- frontal/ lateral for metastases
- 99mTc-MDP for bone secondaries
- IVU/ MCU if CT/US is not available for an irregular bladder filling defect

\section*{Bladder tumors}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_72b39936526bf491dc20g-26.jpg?height=1538&width=2360&top_left_y=31&top_left_x=97)

<a id="chapter-22"></a>

ABDOMINAL MASSES IN CHILDHOOD

\section*{ABDOMINAL MASSES IN CHILDHOOD}
> not a 'small adult'
> different constraints in choice of imaging modalities
> different range of presenting pathologies
> greater emphasis on congenital abnormalities
> incidence of malignant disease increases with increasing age
> exposure to radiation must be kept to a minimum in children
> certain modalities more suitable than others
e.g. paucity of abdominal fat leads to good quality ultrasound images BUT reduced quality on CT
> need for sedation/anesthesia in:
- painful procedures
- procedures requiring immobilization e.g.

CT, MRJ

\section*{IMAGING MODALITIES}
> Plain radiographs
> Ultrasound scanning
> Computerized Tomography (CT)
> Nuclear Medicine
> Intravenous Urography (IVU)
> Angjography
> Magnetic Resonance Imaging (MRJ)

Plain Radiographs:
- plain abdominal films are of limited use
- ? abdominal mass
- displaced bowel loop gas - location, size
- $\pm$ calcification - neuroblastoma, teratoma, nephroblastoma, hepatoblastoma
- associated skeletal abnormalities e.g. metastatic disease
- complimentary CXR - ? pulmonary metastasis e.g in neproblastoma, hepatoblastoma

Ultrasound Scanning:
- first examination of choice in neonate and child presenting with an abdominal mass
- may be the only one required to make a diagnosis and assist further management
- localization
- organ of origin
- internal structure of mass - cystic/solid, $\pm$ internal septae - (often an indicator of benign or malignant nature)
- ? abnormal vasculature - Doppler and color flow
- guide percutaneous biopsy for histopathological diagnosis

Computerized Tomography:
- adjunct to ultrasonography
- next investigation of choice
- additional anatomical information
- however - ionizing radiation
- requires sedation for those below 5 years
- only used when distinct benefits are identified

\section*{Nuclear Medicine:}
- evaluation of function e.g.
- renal scan in assessment of renal function cases of hydronephrosis

Intravenous Urography:
- largely replaced by ultrasound and CT
- however - specific cases of renal masses, the appearance on IVU is characteristic and diagnostic

Angiography:
- also largely replaced by US, CT and MRI in the investigation and diagnosis of abdominal masses
- however, useful pre-op assessment of a tumor, particularly liver tumors - can demonstrate vascular supply of tumor from liver? arterial ? portal venous

Magnetic Resonance Imaging:
- no ionizing radiation
- multiplanner scanning and therefore useful tool in investigation of children with abdominal masses
- requires relatively long periods of immobility and therefore sedation / general anaesthesia is required for those under 6 years.
- not widely available
- very good demonstration of abdominal and pelvic masses
- best demonstration of extension of masses into spinal canal e.g. neuroblastoma

\section*{ABDOMINAL MASSES}

Masses presenting in childhood in the abdomen can be divided into 4 broad categories:
1. Retroperitoneal:
- renal
- non-renal
2. Hepatobiliary
3. Gastrointestinal
4. Pelvic/Genital

\section*{ABDOMINAL MASS IN NEONATE}

RENAL (55\%)
Hydronephrosis (25\%)
Multicystic dysplastic kidney (15\%)
Polycystic kidney
Mesoblastic nephroma
Renal vein thrombosis

<a id="chapter-23"></a>

\section*{Imaging of The GIT}

\section*{Dr. Angeline. A. Aywak}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ea78dddf9295cc64c5a0g-02.jpg?height=967&width=1649&top_left_y=103&top_left_x=553)

The discovery of X-rays made radiological procedures play a leading role in the visualization of anatomy and diseases of the gastrointestinal system.
- Later the advent of fibre optic endoscopic examination has changed the role of imaging of GIT pathology.
Even so Imaging still has a vital role in management of gastrointestinal conditions.

\section*{Why Irsage?}
- Imaging is needed to make or confirm a diagnosis and show disease extend.
- Help to plan treatment
- To follow up disease response after treatment.

Listed below are current gastrointestinal imaging modalities
A) Plain X-rays
B) Ultrasound
C) Contrast studies
D) Computerized Tomography
E) Radionuclide Scanning
F) Magnetic Resonance Imaging
G) Angiography

\section*{A. Pleijn X-PAY FilfNS}

With plain X-rays the gastrointestinal organs cannot be separately visualized, however the normal or pathological gas accumulations, air fluid levels, calcification and motility of gas containing intestine can be seen.

\section*{Plain Films}

\section*{Indications}

\section*{Suspected obstructive conditions}
i. Congenital e.g. TOF, Pyloric stenosis, duodenal atresia.
if. Acquired e.g. intussusception, strangulated hernia.
Localization of radio-opaque foreign bodies
Suspected perforation of intestinal tract.
Suspected calculus.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ea78dddf9295cc64c5a0g-12.jpg?height=1085&width=1798&top_left_y=390&top_left_x=347)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ea78dddf9295cc64c5a0g-17.jpg?height=1211&width=1744&top_left_y=383&top_left_x=425)

\section*{B. Utrasound exannination.}

In the hollow organs an increased wall thickness, increased degree of fluid content, pathological motility and status of surrounding environment (organs, peritoneal space) can be clearly visualized by ultrasound.
All solid orgarns and the peritoneal cavity can be assessed by ultrasound.

\section*{Indications for ultiselsound}
- Suspected masses
- Suspected peritoneal fluid collections
- Suspected calculus
- Suspected inflammatory conditions like appendicitis
Suspected intusussception
The jaundiced patient

\section*{Endosonography}
- With this method the internal lumens of the oesophagus, stomach, duodenum, rectum and their surroundings can be examined.
- Wall structure, pathological processes and their relationship with surrounding organs can be visualized.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ea78dddf9295cc64c5a0g-21.jpg?height=1782&width=1035&top_left_y=47&top_left_x=742)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ea78dddf9295cc64c5a0g-22.jpg?height=961&width=1474&top_left_y=674&top_left_x=551)

\section*{Contrast Studjes}

Contrast material is introduced into the system. Ba sulphate, Air, lonic water soluble as well as non-ionic water soluble contrast materials used.
- Choice of contrast depends on suspected pathology.

\section*{Single Contrast.}
- The gastrointestinal lumen is filled with positive contrast material usually (Barium sulfate).
- This then makes easy visualization of pathological conditions such as stenosis, dilatations, intraluminal filling defects, organ contour deformity and external compression.
The examination can also verify function of the system i.e. emptying and peristalsis.

\section*{Double contrast studies}

This examination applies use of two contrast materials a positive one (Barium sulfate or gastrografin) and a negative one (usually air).
- The wall of the organ is relaxed by smooth muscle relaxant. Membrane is coated by contrast material and lumen distended with gas.
This provides more information than single contrast examination as detail)ed visualization of gut mucosa is achieved, the method however does not afford a functional information.

\section*{Types of contrast studjes}

Sialography
Ba. Swallow
- Ba. Meal
- Ba. meal follow thru
- Small Bowel Enema

Ba. Enema
Colostrogram, Sinogram, Fistulogram

\section*{Siglography}

\section*{Barium Swellow}

\section*{Indications}
1. Dysphagia
2. Pain on swallowing
3. Assessment of tracheo-oesophageal fistula
4. Pre-operative assessment of CA bronchus
5. Assessment of left atrial enlargement
6. Assessment of perforation (water soluble contrast used)
7. Follow up following oesophageal surgery.

\section*{Contraindications}
- None

Patient Preparation
- None

Examination of the large bowel. Two methods commonly used.

- Pain
" Mass
- Meleana
- Obstruction as in intussusception

\section*{Contraindications}
- Toxic megacolon
- Pseudomembranous colitis
- Rectal biopsy within previous 3 days

\section*{D. Computerized Jomography}

This method can recognize pathological wall thickening, pathological wall narrowing (with appropriate technique) and status of surrounding organs.
- However it is rather expensive.

Indications

Same as in Ultrasound

\section*{E. Magnetic resonance Insagjory}

\section*{MRI like CT is useful for clarification of status of surrounding organs of an already identified pathology.}

\section*{It thus plays a role in disease staging especially where cancer is involved}
- Radiation is harmful
- to the individual
- to the community
- Order these examinations only where clinically indicated.

Examine patient.
" Make provisional diagnosis
- Select imaging modality likely to yield the best results
- Use ultrasound in preference to nuclear and X-ray imaging,
- Do not order other exam before significant radiological changes can have occurred.

\section*{PLAIN RADIOGRAPHY}
" Plain radiographs are frequently still the first investigation in the acute abdomen
"For non-acute situations, the relatively small yield of information from a plain radiograph does not justify the radiation dose that it entails, unless it is on follow up basis
- Plain radiographic findings must corroborate with clinical findings: sometimes even obvious signs can be non-specific generating a plethora of differential diagnoses. A good example is the presence of air-fluid levels which may be benign or a pointer to obstruction

\section*{ABNORMAL INTRALUMINAL AIR PATTERN: LOCALISATION}

\section*{Comparison of large and small bowel obstruction features}
\begin{tabular}{|l|l|l|}
\hline Feature & \multicolumn{2}{|c|}{Obstruction} \\
\hline & Small bowel & Large bowel \\
\hline Bowel diameter (cm) & >3 and <5 & $>5$ \\
\hline Position of loops & Central & Peripheral \\
\hline Number of loops & Many & Few \\
\hline Fluid levels (on erect film) & Marry, short & Few, long \\
\hline Bowel markings & Valvaulae (all the way across) & Haustra (partially across) \\
\hline Large bowel gas & No & Yes \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_631514d7bfd7c4743cc4g-24.jpg?height=1292&width=2430&top_left_y=164&top_left_x=70)

\section*{INCREASING ROLE OF MRI IN RECTAL CANCER}
"Paradigm shift has taken place in imaging of rectal carcinoma with High Resolution MRI taking centre stage in tumour staging.
- Excellent anatomical depiction can be attained with this modality influencing surgical decision making since the introduction of total mesorectal excision (TME) as standard surgical procedure. The mesorectal fascia determines the potential circumferential resection margin (CRM).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_631514d7bfd7c4743cc4g-66.jpg?height=1054&width=2170&top_left_y=98&top_left_x=0)

Normal anatomy: Axial Turbo SE T2W image shows the mesorectal fat (*), the mesorectal fascia (white arrows) and confluence of Denovillier's and mesorectal fasciae (black arrows).

Sagittal turbo SE T2W image demonstrates the normal anatomy
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_631514d7bfd7c4743cc4g-67.jpg?height=859&width=1041&top_left_y=419&top_left_x=60)

A
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_631514d7bfd7c4743cc4g-67.jpg?height=849&width=916&top_left_y=427&top_left_x=1251)

B

The driver of the paradigm shift as demonstrated in this rectal tumour on CT (A) and HRMRI (B). The MR image confidently puts it as stage 1 tumour since the mscularis propria is intact.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_631514d7bfd7c4743cc4g-67.jpg?height=113&width=122&top_left_y=1710&top_left_x=2321)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_631514d7bfd7c4743cc4g-68.jpg?height=1200&width=2170&top_left_y=350&top_left_x=165)

\begin{tabular}{|l|l|l|l|}
\hline & \multicolumn{3}{|c|}{Imaging method} \\
\hline Suspected diagnosis & Plain radiography & Ultrasound & Computed tomography \\
\hline Appendicitis & No & Yes (young patients) & Yes \\
\hline Cholecystitis & No & Yes & No \\
\hline Acute pancreatitis & No & Yes (cause) & Yes (complications) \\
\hline Bowelobstruction & Yes & No & Yes \\
\hline Bowel perforation & Yes & No & Yes \\
\hline Diverticulitis & No & No & Yes \\
\hline Renalcolic & No & No & Yes \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

\section*{GENErunt INDICriJIONS}

\section*{2, UL-5r ASOUND}

\section*{Is the investigation of 1st choice Absence of ionizing radiation.}

Transducer choice - 5 Mhz sector probe or a higher frequency transducer.
Approach is trans-abdominal or pelvic
No major patient preparation required.

\section*{Applications}
- Differentiate normal from abnormal.
- Normal kidney - echogenic renal sinus, lower echo cortex
- In neonates and infants renal cortex is thinner \& has the same echogenicity as the liver.
- After the third month the kidneys have the same appearance as the adult kidney with central echoes and a sonolucent outer cortex.
The renal pyramids are prominent and sonolucent.
The size and volume of the kidneys and any mass is accurately measured by ultrasound.
\begin{tabular}{|l|l|}
\hline Age & Average renal length (cm) \\
\hline 0-1wk & 4.48 \\
\hline 1wk - 4mo & 5.28 \\
\hline 4-8mo & 6.15 \\
\hline $8 \mathrm{mo}-1 \mathrm{yr}$ & 6.23 \\
\hline $1-2 \mathrm{yrs}$ & 6.65 \\
\hline 2-9yrs & 7-8 \\
\hline 9-12yrs & 9-10 \\
\hline > 12yrs & 10 cm above \\
\hline
\end{tabular}

\section*{INTRODUCTION}
- Imaging modalities in uroradiology have undergone a dramatic transformation in the recent past.
- Earlier plain films were the only available study.
- Subsequently for no less than 7 decades IVU was the pillar of uroradiology.
- Today cross-sectional imaging studies have taken centre stage albeit without a definite flag bearer....

\section*{Plain film. \\ Natural contrast.}
- Perirenal fat gives a lucent outline to the kidneys and allows an assessment of their position to be made.
- The loss of the psoas outline may indicate retroperitoneal pathology but is a notoriously inaccurate sign.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-04.jpg?height=1384&width=1040&top_left_y=491&top_left_x=1457)

\section*{IVU}
- The excretory urogram is a valuable examination of the urinary tract.
- It gives excellent anatomical images of the kidneys and to some extent an indication of their function.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-05.jpg?height=1412&width=1069&top_left_y=463&top_left_x=1430)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-06.jpg?height=696&width=1054&top_left_y=140&top_left_x=0)

\section*{Patient preparation.}
- Bowel preparation is useful but no longer essential. Plumber unnecessary.
- Fluid restriction unless severe does not influence radiographic densities.
- At this severe level it becomes counter productive when it influences tubular function.
- Patients with diabetes and myeloma specifically require hydration.

\section*{Normal nephrogram}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-07.jpg?height=1203&width=1637&top_left_y=349&top_left_x=409)
- Final image of a tomographic sequence demonstrates symmetric nephrograms and pyelograms.
- Renal size is normal.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-08.jpg?height=1426&width=1974&top_left_y=1&top_left_x=263)
- Image obtained at 80 minutes shows a persistent, very dense right nephrogram, a typical finding in acute high-grade obstruction.
- A 2-mm stone was discovered at the right ureterovesical junction.

\section*{Simple cyst.}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-09.jpg?height=1127&width=1445&top_left_y=355&top_left_x=483)
- Nephrotomogram shows a nephrographic defect in the midportion of the left kidney (arrow) with increased parenchymal thickness and distortion of the underlying collecting system. US helped confirm a simple cyst.

\section*{Renal cell carcinoma.}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-10.jpg?height=1216&width=1605&top_left_y=342&top_left_x=489)
- Nephrotomogram shows a mass in the midportion of the left kidney (arrows) producing increased parenchymal thickness and distorting the collecting system.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-11.jpg?height=1401&width=1787&top_left_y=1&top_left_x=344)
- Axis and position alteration.
- Nephrotomogram demonstrates characteristic axis alteration associated with a horseshoe kidney.

\section*{Epitaph for the Urogram ${ }^{1}$ E. Stephen Amis, Jr, MD ${ }^{1}$}

From the Department of Radiology, Albert Einstein College of Medicine and Montefiore Medical Center, 111 E 210th St, Bronx, NY 10467. Received July 29, 1999; accepted August 9.

Address reprint requests to the author (e-mail: amis@aecom.yu.edu).
"The excretory urogram is terminally ill. It survives only because of clinicians and radiologists who do not fully appreciate that more accurate delineation of the urinary tract can be gained with other techniqueslt is now time to move on to different techniques."

\section*{Stephen Amis, Jr, MD}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-13.jpg?height=632&width=487&top_left_y=1243&top_left_x=0)

\section*{Hematuria.....}
- The remaining major indication for excretory urography is hematuria.
- Patients with hematuria require evaluation of both the renal parenchyma and the urothelium.
- The traditional evaluation has been with cystoscopy and excretory urography (supplemented with retrograde ureteropyelography as needed).

\section*{The last IVU sermon....}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-15.jpg?height=648&width=904&top_left_y=1&top_left_x=1595)
"In the near future, excretory urography will be replaced by nonenhanced helical CT to evaluate for stones and by CT urography to evaluate for hematuria and other genitourinary conditions.
- CT urography should be viewed simply as an evolution of urography, which survived for 7 decades as one of the mainstays in imaging of the urinary tract.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-16.jpg?height=1875&width=2498&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=1)

\section*{ULTRASOUND}
- The ability to differentiate normal from abnormal is directly related to the skill of the ultrasonagrapher
- Resolution of pathology at an earlier stage is being recognised by improvement in US
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-17.jpg?height=837&width=1015&top_left_y=653&top_left_x=1380) equipment.
- So updating of skills and equipment is also needed

\section*{ULTRASOUND IMAGE}
- Composed of a variety of bright dots on a film that vary in intensity according to the strength of returning echoes from tissues or structures in the body
- Provides anatomic and nonanatomic information

\section*{Multi-Detector Row CT of the Kidneys and Urinary Tract}
- Multi-detector row helical computed tomography (CT) offers considerable advantages in evaluation of the urinary tract.
- It has the potential to become the single imaging modality used for comprehensive evaluation and treatment planning of most conditions affecting the kidneys and urinary tract, making conventional diagnostic techniques such as intravenous urography and angiography nearly obsolete.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-25.jpg?height=559&width=555&top_left_y=1&top_left_x=0)
- With the introduction of multidetector technology, CT urography, to date, has emerged as the initial heir apparent to intravenous urography; many years of experience have now clearly demonstrated that CT is the test of choice for many urologic problems, including urolithiasis, renal masses, urinary tract
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-25.jpg?height=1442&width=987&top_left_y=280&top_left_x=1509)

\section*{CT Angiography of the Renal Arteries.}
- The main applications of CT angiography include evaluation of potential renal donors, evaluation of suspected renal artery stenosis or aneurysms, and demonstration and location of crossing vessels prior to pyeloplasty for ureteropelvic junction obstruction.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-26.jpg?height=959&width=1082&top_left_y=916&top_left_x=1417)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-27.jpg?height=869&width=2498&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=1)
- Emphysematous pyelonephritis in a 45-year-old woman with sepsis

\section*{MR Urography: Techniques and Clinical Applications}
- Magnetic resonance (MR) urography comprises an evolving group of techniques with the potential for allowing optimal noninvasive evaluation of many abnormalities of the urinary tract.
- MR urography is clinically useful in the evaluation of suspected urinary tract obstruction, hematuria, and congenital anomalies, as well as surgically altered anatomy, and can be particularly beneficial in pediatric or pregnant patients or when ionizing radiation is to be avoided.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-29.jpg?height=1365&width=2499&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=0)
- Prostate cancer metastatic to lymph nodes in a 53-year-old man.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-30.jpg?height=1286&width=2499&top_left_y=1&top_left_x=0)
- Importance of cine MR urography in demonstrating the entire ureters with static-fluid techniques.
- (a) On a coronal thick-slab MR urogram from a cine series obtained in a 52-year-old woman with hematuria, the ureters are poorly delineated.
- (b) Coronal thick-slab MR urogram from the same series shows improved delineation of the ureters (arrows).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-31.jpg?height=1780&width=2498&top_left_y=95&top_left_x=1)

\section*{GENERATOR SYSTEM}
- The basis of the generator system (the cow) is that the daughter is physically separable from the parent.
- This process is termed "milking".
- The eluate contains sodium pertechnetate.
- Since the generator system is
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-40.jpg?height=779&width=824&top_left_y=714&top_left_x=1619) small and can be shipped easily, it makes the routine use of 99 mTc possible in nuclear medicine

\section*{BILATERAL TESTICULAR INFARCTION}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_51410d0cb87d33448055g-41.jpg?height=772&width=2277&top_left_y=475&top_left_x=121)
- Testicular torsion leads to testicular infarction. Testicular ischemia may also result from epididymitis as in this pt.
- Early scintigraphic flow images show markedly increased flow to scrotal regions bilaterally. Tissue-phase scintigram shows increased peri-testicular activity with bilateral central regions of diminished activity.
