\section*{Patient's biodata}

Unique ID - Hosp no, clinic no, etc
Name
Age
Sex
Date
Address - ward, clinic, physical, telephone etc.
LMP (female patients)

\section*{Clinical information}
-There still exists no substitute for proper history and physical examination in clinical patient care
-Signs and symptoms narrow down to a particular system/ organ. In some cases a definitive diagnosis is made while in others differential diagnoses are inferred clinically
-Explicit relevant information of your clinical findings guides the radiologist/ radiographer to resonate with your expectations both in technique and interpretation of findings

\section*{Clinical information}

For female patients of reproductive age, last normal menstrual period is important History of allergies if known need to be included

\section*{generating an imaging request}
-Appropriateness of the examination and modality
-Patient preparation for the examination
-Limitations of the modality

\section*{Deciding the appropriate radiological investigation}

What is the clinical question you want answered by radiology?

What system/ organ is of interest? What imaging modalities are relevant to that system/organ?

How will the radiological investigation influence the decision making in the process of patient management?

\section*{Deciding the appropriate radiological investigation}
"Analyze
1.Patient's stability, immediate and special needs
2.Availability of the preferred modality
3.Cost
4.Radiation safety considerations
5. Details that need to be derived from the imaging modality
6. Expertise of the imaging team

\section*{Deciding the appropriate radiological investigation}

\section*{Patient's stability, immediate and special needs}
"In acute life threatening situations, patients stability overrides any imaging and therefore the latter is done without any danger to the patient's life
"Airway, breathing, circulation (hemodynamic state) need to be sorted out before transfer of patient to the imaging department
"Special needs have to be addressed e.g. hypothermia in neonates and infants. Allergies when performing contrast studies, metallic implants when performing MRI are all risks to be considered. Can you think of any other special needs?

\section*{Deciding the appropriate radiological investigation}

\section*{Availability and cost considerations}

These are not absolute determinants but need to be factored in particularly in regard to the influence the radiological examination will have on the patient's outcome.

\section*{Deciding the appropriate radiological investigation}

\section*{Radiation safety considerations}
"Ionizing vs non-ionizing radiation
"If a clinical question can be adequately answered by a modality that utilizes nonionizing radiation then go for it over one that will use ionizing radiation.
"Children, pregnant mothers carry the greatest risk.
"Some organs are more radiosensitive than others e.g. gonads, thyroid gland, eye lens

\section*{Deciding the appropriate radiological investigation}

\section*{Details expected from the investigation}

Each of the imaging modalities have their strengths and weaknesses when it comes to detailing anatomical structures and pathological processes

Moreover, even for the appropriate modality, extent of the examination including field of view, views required and where applicable contrast introduction need to be optimized.

\section*{Patient preparation}
"Some radiological examinations require special preparation
1.i.v. contrast - renal function tests
2.Upper GI Contrast studies - fasting at least 6 hrs
3.Contrast Enema - bowel preparation
4.IVU - Bowel preparation
5.Abdominal US - Fasting at least 6 hrs
6.Pelvic US - Full bladder

\section*{Examples of radiological study limitations}
1.Plain radiography - Poor soft tissue detail, representation of disease in 2D, ionising radiation.
2.Contrast radiography - ionising radiation, lack of extraluminal details, poor soft tissue details
3.Ultrasound - operator dependence, inability to penetrate gas eg. Bowel disease, bone disease
4.CT - radiation dose, cost, availability
5.MRI - availability, cost, limited expertise, metallic implants, claustrophobia.
6 Angiography - invasive, limited expertise, radiation
7.Radionuclide - availability, expertise, poor anatomical detail

Clinical scenario: patient stabbed with a knife through the left temporal region
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_70255f40b12635510c4bg-18.jpg?height=1255&width=2140&top_left_y=157&top_left_x=97)

Plain radiography in this case of penetrating knife injury to the head could only place the tip intracranially. The CT scan image on the right further demonstrates the intracranial effects. Plain radiography has strength in demonstrating bone and other radiodense objects e.g metals, air natterns but is weak in soft tissue analvsis

Clinical scenario: child swallowed a coin
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_70255f40b12635510c4bg-19.jpg?height=938&width=1875&top_left_y=274&top_left_x=374)

A coin that is well demonstrated on plain radiography but poorly shown on ultrasound. Ultrasound has poor penetration of bowel gas

Clinical scenario: patient with suspected colorectal cancer
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_70255f40b12635510c4bg-20.jpg?height=920&width=2023&top_left_y=283&top_left_x=143)

Barium enema demonstrates mucosal irregularity and stricture involving the caecum but does not not show extra-luminal and extramural effects that are well demonstrated by the CT scan on the right. Intracavitary contrast studies are limited to the cavity and its lining

Clinical scenario: patient with trauma to the left middle finger
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_70255f40b12635510c4bg-21.jpg?height=1401&width=1552&top_left_y=266&top_left_x=325)

Advantage of at least two orthogonal views when investigating trauma. Note the dislocation of distal interphalangeal joint missed on the AP BUT SEEN ON THE LATERAL.

<a id="chapter-51"></a>

\section*{Imaging of the surgical abdomen-the acute abdomen.}

\author{
Dr Alfred Odhiambo. \\ Lecturer ; University of Nairobi.
}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-01.jpg?height=1099&width=1027&top_left_y=776&top_left_x=1470)

\section*{Definition of an acute abdomen.}

A group of conditions characterized by severe abdominal pain in which prompt surgical treatment must be considered to treat perforation, peritonitis, vascular or other intra-abdominal catastrophe

\section*{Principles of imaging.}
- The objective of imaging in the whole of the human body is to demonstrate structures not usually visible to the eye.
- Unlike in the chest where natural contrast is maximal abdominal contrast is limited.
- A soft issue opacity of 2 mm in the lung fields would stand out against air.
- In the abdomen a polyp 20 times that would easily be missed.

\section*{General Principles}
- Role of the radiologist - help the surgeon decide if surgery is indicated
- If immediate surgery indicated on clinical findings, negative radiology should be ignored

\section*{The tools of the trade.}
- Plain film.
- Sonography.
- Barium studies.
- Nuclear medicine.
${ }^{\circ}$ CT.
- MRI.
- IVU.

The Plain A6d. Radiograph (AXR)

Often the most useful initial investigation

Interpretation depends on the gas patterns

\section*{Specific diagnosis is possible in many cases}

Objectivity is improved by viewing r/g initially in the absence of any clinical info.

Choice of Radiograph
Influenced by clinical suspicion \& patient's ability to stand

Supine abdomen = most useful single r/g. In some conditions, e.g. Renal colic, it may be adequate alone.

Erect CXR = best film for free intra-abdominal gas

A horizontal ray AXR
- adds information: fluid levels/ free gas.
- It's either Erect PA or LL decubitus (interpretation difficult ??)

Exposures are optimal for picking Pneumoperitoneum

Acute abd. Mimics may be suspected on CXR:
$\checkmark$ LL pneumonia, Pulm. infarction, pleurisy
$\checkmark$ aortic dissection/leaking aneurysm,
$\checkmark$ MI, CCF, pericarditis
$\checkmark$ Pneumothorax

Some abd. Dx have chest complications, eg. Pleural effusion often complicates acute pancreatitis

Baseline CXR - chest complications and subphrenic abscesses are frequent post-op complications

Supine AXR
- Distribution of gas
- Calibre of bowel
- Displacement of bowel by soft tissue masses
- Obliteration of normal fat lines - eg. Psoas
outlines,
properitoneal fat
line
LL Decubitus
Best for pneumoperitoneum in patients who cant stand Best for showing a dilated gas-filled duodenal loop in suspected acute pancreatitis

> Dorsal Decubitus may show calcification in an aortic aneurysm not detected on the supine view

\section*{Normal Appearances}

\section*{Amount of gas in SB is small. Only short lengths of it are seen Valvulae conniventes are rarely seen}

3 - 5 fluid levels $<2.5 \mathrm{~cm}$ long are normal

3 or more fluid levels $>2.5 \mathrm{~cm}$ are abnormal (ileus/obstruction)

Colon contains gas - haustra are readily identified

Colonic fluid levels of several cm in left colon are normal

Normal Appearances ctd..

S/bowel rule of 3:
-Wall thickness < 3 mm
-Valvulae conn. < 3 mm wide
- Caliber $\quad<3 \mathrm{~cm}$
- Air fluid levels < 3

Bowel calibre limits; rule of 3:
Small bowel 3 cm
TRV colon 6 cm
Caecum $\quad 9 \mathrm{~cm}$

\section*{Mechanical obstruction (GOO)}

\section*{Causes:}

Duodenal or pyloric canal ulceration Extrinsic compression, eg. Ca pancreas

\section*{Huge fluid filled stomach occupies most of the abdomen}

AXR - large soft tissue mass w. little or no bowel gas beyond it

A little stomach gas is usually present allowing identification

Acute Gastric Dilatation (AGD)
Frequently occurs in old people
Associated w/ fluid and electrolyte imbalance High mortality

Causes:
Post-op
Trauma
Inflammatory conditions - peritonitis, pancreatitis, cholecystitis
Diabetic or hepatic coma

Abnormal rotation of one part of the stomach around another part

Relatively rare. Causes severe epigastric pain \& wretching

Most common in the elderly; may occur at any age

In most, stomach is at least partially herniated into thorax - Associated with para-oesophageal hiatus hernia or eventration.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-16.jpg?height=1532&width=1216&top_left_y=60&top_left_x=30)

\section*{Gastric volvulus: supin
Mesentero-axial type Intramural gas Rigler's sign}

\section*{Perforated gangrenous stomach found at laparatomy}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-17.jpg?height=1596&width=1940&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=165)

Gastric volvulus
(Lib)

Distinguishing Small \& Large Bowel dilatation

Small Bowel

No Haustra

Valvulae conniv. Seen in jejunum

Many loops

Centrally located

Small radius of curvature

Small calibre: 3-5 cm

No solid faeces

Large Bowel

Haustra present

No valvulae conniventes

Few loops

Peripheral location

Large radius of curvature

Large callibre: usually > 5cm

Solid faeces present = best sign

Small Bowel Obstruction (SBO) ${ }_{12.5 \%}$. . . 966 .
Africa : strangulated hernia = commonest cause (75\%)
West : > 75\% from adhesions

\section*{Other causes:}

Luminal: gallstones, parasites, intussusception

Intramural: tumours, TB, strictures
(Radiotherapy)
Extrinsic: abdominal masses, volvulus

SBO: supine
- multiple loops
- valvulae

Large bowel not distended
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-21.jpg?height=1487&width=1276&top_left_y=162&top_left_x=19)

\section*{SBO}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-21.jpg?height=1114&width=1082&top_left_y=543&top_left_x=1340)

\section*{S/bowel fluid levels}

\section*{Causes of Small Bowel Fluid Levels}

\section*{Small or large bowel obstruction Paralytic Ileus}

G/Enteritis
Cleansing enemas
Uraemia

Mesenteric thrombosis
Peritoneal metastasis (usually <25mm long)
(SBO should not be diagnosed on the basis of fluid levels alone)

Small Bowel Obstruction (SBO)...
String of beads sign - virtually diagnostic of mechanical SBO. (gas bubbles trapped b/w valvulae in very distended almost completely fluidfilled s/bowel)

Initial AXR may be normal or equivocal, especially in high jejunal obstruction with vomiting. Films taken a few hrs later are often diagnostic.

If not, barium study or CT is done. Barium is safe due the large amount of fluid present.

SBO: Erect
- String of beads sign
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-26.jpg?height=1012&width=1720&top_left_y=180&top_left_x=36)

US Rt. flank in SBO:
Dilated hypoperistaltic fluid filled loops

> CT may have a role if - AXR equivocal
> - partial obstruction
> - conservative management is planned (to avoid missing strangulation)

CT shows:
- Dilated loops ( $>2.5 \mathrm{~cm}$ ) (3cm mid ileum, 3.5 prox. Jejunum)
- Site and level of obstruction
- State of mesentery
- Intra- or extraluminal masses
- Presence of ascites
- Presence of strangulation

CT overall accuracy in SBO is $95 \%$.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-28.jpg?height=1117&width=1114&top_left_y=220&top_left_x=23)

\section*{SBO on CT}
- Cause was adhesions

\section*{SBO: Strangulating Obstruction}

2 limbs of a loop are incarcerated by a band or in a hernia
Blood supply is compromised

Loop may fill with fluid $\Rightarrow$ mass or pseudotumour on AXR
Loop may fill with gas $\Rightarrow$ coffee bean appearance on AXR

\section*{Gallstone Ileus}

2\% of SBO. Typically, middle aged women
Stone erodes through inflamed GB wall into duodenum, rarely into the colon

Impacts in terminal ileum
(rarely in duodenum, colon)
50\% have radiographic signs of SBO
(usually with large fluid: gas ratio)

\section*{Gallstone ileus: supine}
- Abd. pain \& vomiting 5 days
- Gas in CBD

\section*{Intussusception}

Telescope-like invagination of bowel into lumen of more distal bowel. (Intussusceptum into intussuscipiens)

Presents with pain, vomiting, blood in stool \& a palpable mass. Diagnosis usually made clinically.
$=2^{\text {nd }}$ commonest cause of acute abd. in child after appendicitis

Most common 3 months to 2 yrs

Child over 3 yrs - think lymphoma
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-33.jpg?height=1455&width=1471&top_left_y=181&top_left_x=39)

\section*{Ileocolic Intussusception}

Cause: polypoid mass in terminal ileum

Intussusception ctd...
Adult Intussusception:
Accounts for 5\% of all intussusception cases.

20\% are idiopathic; 80\% have known causes:
- Tumour - 50\%
- Surgery - 30\%
- TB, typhoid

Accounts for < 15\% of adult bowel obstruction.
lleoileal type commoner in adults (40\%). Usually from small bowel benign tumours. Ileocolic 15\%. The rest colocolic.

Bloody stool in the majority. Palpable mass in 50\%.

\section*{Intussusception: supine}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-35.jpg?height=1331&width=1159&top_left_y=221&top_left_x=33)
- soft tissue mass across upper abd
-crescent of gas around head of intussusceptum

\section*{TRV US scan}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-35.jpg?height=974&width=827&top_left_y=782&top_left_x=1493)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-36.jpg?height=1769&width=1322&top_left_y=57&top_left_x=39)

Intussusception following ileocolic lymphoma

\section*{US of RIF}
- ileum surrounded by hyperechoic mesenteric fat inside thickened colonic wall

\section*{CT- same case}
- oral contrast in ileal lumen in thickened ascending colon

\section*{Caecal Volvulus}

1/3 of Colonic Volvulus
(2/3 sigmoid; 4\% TRV colon)
< 2\% of adult intestinal obstruction

Caecum \& Asc. Colon have to be on a mesentery
(a congenital variant occurring in 11\%).
$30-60 \mathrm{yr}$ olds
50\%: caecum twists and inverts so caecal pole occupies LUQ

50\%: caecum only twists, remains in RIF or Rt. Abdomen.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-38.jpg?height=1376&width=1379&top_left_y=240&top_left_x=95)

Caecal volvulus

\section*{Caecal Volvulus}

Caecum dilates considerably but 1 or 2 haustra are still seen.

Identification of attached gas-filled appendix confirms the diagnosis

50\% develop mod. to severe small bowel distension

Left colon is collapsed.

\section*{Sigmoid Volvulus}

The elderly (>60), mentally ill or institutionalized most affected

Sigmoid loop twists around its mesenteric axis, usually counterclockwise

Typically chronic with intermittent acute attacks

In $1 / 3$ it's difficult to differentiate from a dilated nonrotated sigmoid or dilated TRV colon looping down.

AXR in Sigmoid Volvulus
Massively distended inverted U-shaped loop

Ahaustral margin which overlaps:
- the lower border of the liver shadow $\rightarrow$ liver overlap sign
- the haustrated dilated desc. Colon $\boldsymbol{\rightarrow}$ left flank overlap sign
- the left side of the pelvis $\boldsymbol{\rightarrow} \boldsymbol{p}$ pelvic overlap sign

Apex above T10 and under left hemidiaphragm

Inferior Convergence on the Left side of the pelvis over upper sacral segments

Air: fluid ratio > 2:1

Sigmoid volvulus: supine.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-42.jpg?height=1428&width=2323&top_left_y=342&top_left_x=55)

\section*{Sigmoid Volvulus}

\section*{Contrast enema is done if AXR diagnosis in doubt:}
$\checkmark$ a curved tapering of barium, the bird of prey sign
$\checkmark$ a corkscrew mucosal pattern at the point of twist
$\checkmark$ in chronic volvulus, shouldering (wall thickening at twist site)

RX:
- Decompress \& stabilise with rectal tube
- Complicated cases: Surgical resection. Mortality 25\%

\section*{Ileosigmoid Znot}

Rare. An abnormally mobile loop of small bowel forms a knot around the base of the pelvic colon

Abrupt onset, fulminant course, intense pain

AXR:
Dilated loop of pelvic colon, lies on Rt. side

Small bowel obstruction

Retained faecal matter in undistended proximal colon

Acute Colitis
Usually acute exacerbations of IBD. Ulceration is responsible for the major complications

AXR is useful to decide between medical and surgical management

Absence of (deep) ulceration and dilatation allows medical management

Areas with active colitis have no faecal residue

Coarse irregularity of the mucosal edge and absence of haustrations suggests marked ulceration

\section*{Acute Colitis: Toxic Megacolon}

An acute fulminant form of colitis with:
- transmural inflammation
- neuromuscular degeneration
- Extensive colonic dilatation

Usually occurs as a complx of - U.Colitis in 2-13\%
- Crohn's dx 2\% or PMC.

Extensive and deep ulceration

Perforation and peritonitis are common. Ba Enema is c/i.

Age: 20-35y. Rx usually by colectomy. Mortality: 30\%.

\section*{Acute Colitis: Perforation}

Likely to occur during an acute attack of Ulcerative Colitis

Result of deep ulceration

Sigmoid = usual site

Free perforation $\boldsymbol{\rightarrow}$ pneumoperitoneum (rare in Crohn's)

Sealed perforations are not detected reliably on AXR

\section*{Pseudo-obstruction ctd...}

\section*{AXR:}

Colon may be very dilated +/- small bowel dilatation

Large amount of bowel gas \& fluid levels are present

Ba enema (or CT) is required to exclude true obstruction and avoid an unnecessary laparatomy

If caecal diameter exceeds the critical 9cm, caecostomy or Rt. colostomy is required to prevent perforation
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-49.jpg?height=1312&width=2332&top_left_y=135&top_left_x=35)

Pseudo-obstruction: supine. Marked large \& small bowel distension till sigmoid area. Ba flows freely into desc. colon

PneumoPm: Signs on Supine AXR
RUQ gas - about ¬Ω of patients have an oval or linear gas
collection adjacent to the liver in:
- the subhepatic space
- Morrison's pouch
- fissure for ligamentum teres
(This is the commonest sign).

Rigler's sign - both sides of bowel wall visualised.
(Requires 1000 ml of air) - WD

Triangular air - gas collections between loops of bowel
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-51.jpg?height=1382&width=2345&top_left_y=184&top_left_x=14)

Rigler's sign in perforated DU
Erect CXR

\section*{Pneumoperitoneum: Mimics}

Colonic interposition - Chilaiditi's syndrome.
Commoner in ascites, cirrhosis, chronic lung dx .

Subdiaphragmatic fat - extension from post. pararenal fat
- more laterally situated than air
- constant position in decubitus views

Curvilinear supradiaphragmatic lung collapse - rare

Subphrenic abscess
Irregularity of left hemi-diaphragm

Cysts of pneumatosis intestinalis

\section*{Intraperitoneal Fluid}

Commonly present in acute abdominal conditions

Even moderate amounts can be difficult to diagnose on AXR.
(US far superior)
Pelvis = most dependent part on both supine and erect films

Earliest sign:
Fluid densities (dog-ears) lateral to rectal gas shadow

\title{
Inflammatory Conditions
}

Acute appendicitis

Acute cholecystitis

Acute pancreatitis

Intra-abdominal abscesses

\section*{Appendicoliths}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-55.jpg?height=1483&width=2457&top_left_y=330&top_left_x=15)

\section*{US in Appendicitis}

Graded compression technique displaces bowel loops

High frequency probe

Accuracy about 80-98\% (GA).
False negatives occur with appendicitis of the tip, retrocaecal appendicitis, perforated appendicitis (compressible in 40-55\%)

False + ve: dilated fallopian tube, periappendicitis, IBD, insipated stool mimicking an appendicolith
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ba96cbc2b0950a3eda46g-57.jpg?height=785&width=1120&top_left_y=35&top_left_x=52)

\section*{Lumen is distended with fluid}

Layers of appendiceal wall still visible

CT in Appendicitis
95\% accuracy in confirming or excluding appendicitis (subspecialists)

Helical CT more accurate than conventional

Oral and/or colonic contrast improves accuracy

IV contrast is not essential

Normal appendix is seen on helical CT in 40\% ( 5 mm slices)

FACT - 5 mm slices over abdominopelvic junction only
(1/3 Radiation dose of abd CT)

\section*{Acute Cholecystitis}

Acute inflammation of GB is typically due to calculus obstructing the cystic duct (95\%)

85\% disimpact spontaneously if stone < 3 mm

Peak age 40-60

3: 1 Female preponderance

Gallstones

Demonstrates blockage of the cystic duct. Diagnostic when GB not seen after 4 hrs .

False +ve occur in malnutrition and parenteral nutrition

Acute Pancreatitis
Most cases relate to gallstones or alcohol abuse

Changes include oedema, haemorrhage, fat necrosis, and sometimes acute suppuration

Diffuse involvement in 52\%. Focal in the rest, commonly in the head.

Pancreatic necrosis raises mortality from 4\% to 85\%

Peripancreatic acute fluid collections occur in 40\%

AXR in Acute Pancreatitis ctd...

Rare but diagnostic:

Gas within pancreas seen as multiple small bubbles $\Rightarrow$ mottled appearance is diagnostic of pancreatic abscess. Prognosis: grave.

Faint mottling over the pancreas due to fat necrosis - v. rare

US in A. Pancreatitis
- Often compromised by small bowel ileus
- Check for gallstones - a treatable cause
- US is normal in mild cases
- US cannot reliably detect devt. of p/ necrosis

Signs:
Enlarged pancreas with indistinct boundaries
Reduced echogenicity ¬´- oedema (but Pancr. duct often enlarged)

Duodenal wall thickening
Peripancreatic fluid
Lt. Pleural effusion - in more severe Dx

Acute pancreatitis

\section*{Renal Colic}

Follows acute ureteric obstruction by calculus

US + AXR combined will pick 75\% of calculi causing colic

<a id="chapter-52"></a>

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_c97bf67288dd32e443d4g-01.jpg?height=1136&width=2153&top_left_y=393&top_left_x=586)
- INTRODUCTION
- EMBRYOLOGY
- IMAGING MODALITIES
- SPECIFIC CONDITION

\section*{INTRODUCTION}
- The fetal hert develops from a series of complex and rapid changes which occur between the second and $8^{\text {th }}$ week of intrauterine life
- During the $3^{\text {rd }}$ and $5^{\text {th }}$ weeks cardiac structures fevelop most actively $\square$ most damage

\section*{EMBRYOLOGY}
- Splanchnic mesoderm
- Endocardial tube is the initial basic structure
- Development characterized by rapid and complex changes
- 5 dilatations
- Common truncus arteriosus
- Bulbous cordis
- Common primitive ventricle
- Pcommon primitive atrium
- Sinus venosus

\section*{CONT.}
- Truncus arteriosus $\square$ aorta and pulmonary trunk
- Bulbus cordis $\square$ smooth part of RV (conus arteriosus) and smooth part of LV (aortic vestibule)
- Primitve ventricle $\square$ trabeculated part of LV and RV
- Pimitive atrium $\square$ trabeculated part of atria
- Sinus venosus $\square>$ smooth parts of RA; coronary sinus; oblique vein of LA

\section*{NORMAL FETAL CIRCULATION}
- Oxygenated blood is carried by the umbilical vein through the ductus venosus and IVC $\square$ RA $\square$ LA through the foramen ovale $\square$ LV $\square$ systemic circulation through the aorta (bypassing lungs)
- SVC \& IVC $\square$ RA, RV an PA
- Most of RV output passess through the PDA into the descending aorta to supply the lower parts of the fetus and umbilical arteries which supply the fetal placental plexus

\section*{CHD IMAGING MODALITIES}
- Plain radiography
- Echocardiography $\square 1^{\text {st }}$ line to assess anatomy and function esp. in congenital cardiac anomalies
- Cardiac catheterization $\square$ definition of vascular anatomy
- Preserved for complex anomalies that cannot be resolved by echocardiography
- This is an invasive procedure, contrast is used.
- CT $\square$ description of cardiac and vascular anatomy in relation to other structures of the chest
- MRI $\square$ quantification of cardiac function and vascular flow in vivo

\section*{CHEST RADIOGRAPH INDICATIONS}
- Suspected congenital heart disease
- Cyanosis
- Cardiac failure
- Failure to thrive
- Difficult in feeding in the neonatal period
- Fainting spells

\section*{CONT.}
- Standard views: erect PA or AP (children) chest; Left lateral
- Role
- Provides clues of underlying congenital heart disease
- Instrumental in planin further investigations
- Limited use in neonated and young children
- May show related skeletal anomalies e.g. in trisomy 21
- Assessment
- Cardiac and visceral situs $\square$ situs solitus, inversus, ambiguous (2/3 of the heart should
be on the left side and $1 / 3$ of the hear should be on the right, if vice versa dextrocardia)
- Cardiac size and shape
- Mediastinum: aortic arch PA, trachea indentation
- Pulmonary vasculature: normal, plethora, oligemia, congestion
- Skeletal features: rib notching: coarctation of the aorta
- Post-surgical stigmata e.g. sternal sutures, implants

\section*{CARDIAC SIZE MEASUREMENT}
- Measure the transverse diameter of the heart and compare that of the chest - $50-55 \%$ in adults

\section*{ECHOCARDIOGRAPHY}
- Gives a wealth of information (anatomical and physiologica) without ionizing radiation, patients discomfort or significant risk
- ID M mode) 2D (most commonly used), 3D
- Doppler echocardiography
- CFI, non-invasive angiogram
- Pulsed Doppler echo (valves)
- Continuous wave Doppler
- Contrast echocardiography
- Microbubbles in the venous circulation

\section*{CONTRAST ECHOCADIOGRAPHY}
- Use of intravascular contrast effects produced when microbubbles of gas present in solution are inected

\section*{INDICATIONS}
- Myocardial perfusion can be investigated directly/ indirectly after intracoronary/venous contrast injection with/ without DSA Better define anatomy not shown well on 2D
- Enhancement of endocardial boundaries increasing sensitivity of wall motion abnormalities e.g. valvular regurgitation, shunts

\section*{DOPPLER ECHO}
- Venus flow from IVC < SVC, pulmonary vessels and across the valves
- Measure direction and speed of blood flowing through the heart and blood vessels
- Check for abnormal communication between left and right side of the heart and regurgitation
- Calculate cardiac output ejection fraction
- Flow towards the transducer $\square$ red away from it $\square$ lye and turbulence flow mixture of red and blue-yellow

\section*{CLINICAL APPLICATIONS OF ECHO}
- Congenital heart disease
- Anatomical defects, shunts
- Stress echo
- Pericardial disease
- Coronary artery disease
- Cardiomyopathies
- Valvular heart disease
- Cardiac masses
- Diseases of the aorta

\section*{ANGIOCARDIOGRAPHY}
- Important technique for cardiac and great vessel anatomy
- Coronary angiography commonest
- Transfemoral approach using Judkins or Amplatz catheters
- Pulmonary angiography, for possible PTE
- Adv; offers high resolution images of the heart and great vessels
- Disadv; requires experienced hands and cardiac monitoring, invasive

\section*{CONT.}
- Indications
- Complex congenital heart disease
- Valvular/ myocardial disease
- Ventricular function
- Technique
- Right sided structures studed by introductng the catheter into peripheral vein
- Left sided $\square$ arterial approach
- Use contrast

\section*{CONT.}
- Equipment
- Fluoroscopy on a C-arm with DSA facilities, pressure recording deviece, ECG monitor, blood oxygen analyser, catheters
- Angled views which place the pathological lesion at right angles $\square$ increase accuracu
- Coplications
- Sudden death
- MI
- arrhythmias

\section*{CARDIAC MRI}
- Excellent anatomic detal
- Disadvantage
- Expensive
- Availability
- Metallic implants

\section*{CLINICAL APPLICATIONS}
- Ventriculaar function analysis
- Myocardial function at rest and during stress
- Myocardial erfusion
- Measurement of coronary flow
- VHD pericardial pathology
- Cardiac tumors and throbi
- Carrdiomyopathies
- Assessing great vessels $\square$ aortic/ pulmonary disease

\section*{PULSE SEQUENCES USED}
- Tissue catheterization $\square$ done using T1 \& T2W contrast enhancement and spectroscopy
- T1W $\square$ best anatomic depiction
- Blood $\square$ show signal void
- Gradient echo - impart bright signal to coherently flowing blood $\square$ white blood
- Fast spin echo $\square$ vascular wall bright , blood black

\section*{C/MRI CONT.}
- Gradient recalled echo-technique $\square$ applied for motion studies, to show flowing blood as well as caddiac motion
- ECG gated MRI of the heart
- Velocity mapping sequences
- Images-tomographic slics, of any selected plane

\section*{CONT.}
- Contrast $\square$ gadolinium
- Myocardial perfusion
- MR angiography: time of flight MRA, phase contrast MRA, contrast enhanced MRA
Myocardial infaction
- Ischemia

\section*{CARDIOVASCULAR IMAGING}

\section*{CARDIAC INVESTIGATION}
- METHODS
${ }^{\text {- }}$ Conventional radiography
- Echocardiography
- Angiography
- MRI
${ }^{\circ}$ CT
- Nuclear imaging

\section*{GROSS ANATOMY}

A 4-chambered structure
- located in the middle mediastinum
- covered by two layers of pericardium
obliquely placed with 2/3 on the left side and 1/3 on the right
o internal structure divided by atrioventricular septum into r/l cardiac chambers

\section*{Cont...Borders}
- Laterally-medial aspects of the lungs and respective hila
- superiorly-trachea and carina
- inferiorly the diaphragm
onteriorly-sternum and anterior mediastinum
posteriorly-posterior mediastinum

\section*{Chamber orientation}

RA-lies on the right and forms the RT c/border
- SVC and IVC enter posterior wall superiorly and inferiorly respectively
${ }^{\text {- }}$ atrial septum -posteromedially
- RV-lies to the LT and anterior to RA
- forms anterior heart border
- separated from RA by tricuspid valve

\section*{Cont... Chamber orientation}
${ }^{\text {- Pul/valve-above RV,above and in front of }}$ aortic root(to the LT)
¬© LA-lies posteriorly,forming posterior c/border in part
${ }^{\circ}$ pulm. Veins enter posterior aspect
- mitral valve separates it from LV
${ }^{\circ}$ LAA-projects forward and to the LT from its superior aspect

${ }^{\text {¬© }}$ LV-forms the bulk of LT heart border

\section*{ATRIAL SEPTAL DEFECT (ASD)}
- Incidence

8-14\% of all CHD; M:F = 1:4
- Types
A. Ostium Secundum ASD (60-70\%)
exaggerated resorptive process of septum primum leads to absence / fenestration of the foramen ovale flap
- occurring either:
B. Ostium Primum ASD (30\%)
defect of atrioventricular endocardial cushion
C. Sinus Venosus ASD (5\%)
defect of the superior inlet portion of the atrial septum superior to fossa ovalis near entrance of superior vena cava (SVC straddles ASD)
D. Lutembacher Syndrome = ASD + mitral stenosis

\section*{ASP}

\section*{Clinicil Fertures}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-22.jpg?height=113&width=1800&top_left_y=530&top_left_x=28)
- presentalition frequently > age 40 secondary to benign course
- mildly symptomatic (60\%): dyspnea, fatigue, palpitations, anhythmias
- severely symptomatic (30\%): cyanosis, heart failure
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-22.jpg?height=814&width=1928&top_left_y=1060&top_left_x=572)

\section*{AS D}

\section*{Revicenter Pertives}

Elein
nemal (if shunt $<2 \times$ systemie blood flow)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-23.jpg?height=90&width=2148&top_left_y=612&top_left_x=176)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-23.jpg?height=87&width=2087&top_left_y=696&top_left_x=178) knob (due to clockwise rotation of heart due to RV hypertrophy and RA enlargement)
- nomal size of LA after shunt reversal (due to immediate decompression into RA) in Eisenmenger syndrome
- enlargement of pulmonary trunk + arteries (pulmonary plethora)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-23.jpg?height=708&width=731&top_left_y=1167&top_left_x=693)

Fig. 24.14 Chest radiograph of an elderly women with an ASD and severe pulmonary hypertension. The main pulmonary artery and hilar pulmonary arteries are very large, with peripheral vascular attenuation.

\section*{VENTRICULAR SEPTAL DEFECT}
- Incidence
- most common CHD (25-30\%) of all CHDs
- in isolation
- as a component of a more complex anomaly e.g.

TOF
- as a concomitant of another cardiac anomaly e.g.

TGA

\section*{VSD}
- Types
1. Membranous VSD
- posterior and inferior to crista supraventricularis near commissure between right and posterior (noncoronary) aortic valve cusps
- Variants include:
- anterior membranous VSD (TOF)
- posterior membranous VSD
- supracristal VSD
- Gerbode LV to RA shunt
2. Muscular VSD (maladie de Roger)
- consists of one or more defect, usually small in the muscular part of the IV septum; when multiple - Swiss Cheese VSD

\section*{VSD}
- Clinical Features
- depends on the size of the defect (larger $\rightarrow$ more symptoms)
- asymptomatic $\rightarrow$ heart murmur $\rightarrow$ respiratory infections, mild dyspnea $\rightarrow$ feeding problems, failure to thrive $\rightarrow$ cyanosis; CHF rare
- spontaneous closure in 60\% by 5 years

\section*{VSD}
- Radiological Features

Plain:
- RV hypertrophy
- enlargement of pulmonary artery segment
- enlargement of LA
- enlargement of LV
- increase in pulmonary blood flow (>45\% of pulmonary blood flow from systemic circulation)
- Eisenmenger reaction
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-29.jpg?height=943&width=1981&top_left_y=552&top_left_x=250)

Fig. 24.6 A. Chest radiograph of an infant aged 1 day. The heart is only slightly enlarged and the child was asymptomatic. B. Chest radiograph of the same infant after 1 month. The heart size has increased and the pulmonary vasculature is now plethoric. The child had developed feeding difficulties, and a ventricular septal defect was diagnosed.

\section*{PERSISTENT DUCTUS ARTERIOSUS}
- Incidence
- 9\% of all CHD; M:F = 1:2
- Clinical Features
- mostly asymptomatic
- in large defect - dyspnea, fatigue, failure to thrive
- congestive heart failure (rare) usually by 3 months of age if L-to-R shunt is large
- continuous murmur
- bounding peripheral pulses (intra-aortic pressure runoff through PDA)

\section*{PDA}
- Radiological Features

Plain: (mimics VSD)
- LA enlargement
- enlarged pulmonary artery segment
- increase of pulmonary vasculature (less flow directed to LUL)
- enlarged RV + LV
- enlarged ascending aorta + aortic arch (thymus may obscure this)
- "railroad track" = calcified ductus arteriosus
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-32.jpg?height=827&width=1178&top_left_y=537&top_left_x=648)

C
Fig. 24.20 A. Chest radiograph of a child with a PDA immediately before -losure. The heart is large, and there is pulmonary plethora. B. Localized riew of the Rashkind duct occluder in position in the same patient. C. Chest adiograph in the same patient 24 h after ductal occlusion. The heart has lecreased considerably in size.

\section*{CYANOTIC CONGENITAL HEART}
- patients with this type of $F$ FID $F$ Fesent with central cyanosis at birth/few hours after birth/few months or years later
- result of flow of deoxygenated blood into the systemic circulation
- anatomical deformities responsible for central cyanosis:
- Conotruncal septum displaced anteriorly, and its lowermost component, the infundibular septum, fails to fuse with the top of the IV septum.
- the combination of an abnormal communication and distal right heart block, leading to a R $\rightarrow$ L shunt e.g. TOF, ER
- common chambers, leading to an admixture of saturated and desaturated blood e.g. PTA, TAPVD
- abnormal connections (discordance) e.g. UTGA, CCTGA (AV discordance)

\section*{TETRALOGY OF FALLOT (TOF)}
- Incidence:
- 12\% of all CHD; most common CHD with cyanosis after 1 year of life
- Pathology:
- underdevelopment of pulmonary infundibulum secondary to unequal partitioning of the conotruncus
- leads to hypoplasia of the pulmonary valve, main pulmonary artery, and the origins of the R and L pulmonary arteries.

\section*{TOF}
- Radiological Features

Plain:
- RVH - lifted rounded apex, obliteration of the retrosternal airspace $\rightarrow$ coeur en sabot (boot-shaped heart ) on frontal radiograph
- pronounced concavity in region of pulmonary bay
- right-sided aortic arch in 25\%
- marked reduction in caliber + number of pulmonary vessels $\rightarrow$ oligemic lung fields
- asymmetric pulmonary vascularity
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-36.jpg?height=939&width=1538&top_left_y=535&top_left_x=481)

Fig. 24.31 Chest radiograph of an infant with tetralogy of Fallot. The trachea is indented by the right-sided aortic arch (arrowed), the cardiac apex is angled upward, and the lung fields are oligemic.

\section*{TRANSPOSITION OF GREAT ARTERIES}

\section*{(TGA)}

Incidence:
- 4.7-10\% of all CHDs

Types:
- two main variants have been described:
- uncorrected TGA (D-transposition) [UTGA]
- congenitally corrected TGA (L-transposition)
[CCTGA]
- Radiological Features:

Plain:
-"egg-on-its-side" appearance of heart - narrow superior mediastinum secondary abnormal relationship of great vessels (aorta and PA midline)
- cardiac enlargement beginning 2 weeks after birth
- right atrial enlargement with convex right heart border
- enlargement of LA (with VSD)
- pulmonary plethora
- ascending aorta with convexity to the right
- right aortic arch in 3\% (difficult assessment due to midline position + small size)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-39.jpg?height=591&width=728&top_left_y=725&top_left_x=220)

Fig. 24.41 Chest radiograph of an infant with D-transposition of the great irteries. The pedicle (mediastinum) is narrow, and there is cardiomegaly and pulmonary plethora.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-39.jpg?height=951&width=1184&top_left_y=551&top_left_x=1079)

Fig. 24.43 Chest radiograph of a patient with L-transposition of the great arteries. There is a long smooth curve to the left heart border due to the abnormal leftward origin of the aorta.

\section*{ACQUIRED HEART DISEASE}
- Acute rheumatic fever
- Rheumatic /valvular heart disease
- Myocardial diseases
- Pericardial diseases
- Miscellaneous heart diseases

\section*{Acute rheumatic fever}

A connective tissue disorder which follows infection with gp A beta-haemolytic streptococci of the throat or skin.
- Usually affects children or young adults.
- Most common between 5-15yrs.
- Endemic in parts of Africa and Asia.
- Still the most common cause of acquired heart disease in children and adolescence.

\section*{c/features}

Often occur 2-3 wks after streptococcal pharyngitis.
- Usually present with fever anorexia, lethargy and joint pains.
- History of sore throat may not be forthcoming.
- Arthritis seen in 75\% of pts.
- Skin rash, carditis and neurological changes are also seen.

\section*{Jones criteria}
- Major:
- Carditis
- Polyarthritis
- Chorea
¬© Erythema marginatum
- Subcutaneous nodules.

O Minor
- Fever
- Arthralgia
- Previous rf
- Raised ESR.
- Raised c-reactive protein.
- leucocytosis

\section*{Mitral valve disease}
- 1) Mitral stenosis(ms)

Almost always rheumatic in origin.
- In the elderly, heavy calcification of the valve apparatus can cause ms.
A rare congenital form of ms is also recognized.
- In rms the valve orifice is slowly diminished by progressive fibrosis, calcification of valve leaflets, and fusion of the cusps and subvalvular apparatus.

\section*{Ms cont}
- Flow of blood from left atrium to left ventricle is restricted and left atrial pressure rises leading to pulmonary venous congestion and breathlessness.
- There is dilatation and hypertrophy of the left atrium and left ventricular filling becomes more dependent on left atrial contraction.
mitral orifice is about 5 cm sq and may be reduced to 1 cm sq or less in severe ms.

\section*{Investigations}

ECG: features of rt ventricular hypertrophy left atrial hypertrophy and fibrillations.
- CXR: enlargement of left atrium and its appendage.
-enlargement of main pulm artery.
-features of pulmonary venous congestion.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-47.jpg?height=1168&width=2156&top_left_y=519&top_left_x=169)

Mitral stenosis and regurg showing enlargement of left atrium and prominence of pulm artery.
Double shadow of left atrial enlargement. Distended pulm veins. Splaying of carina
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-48.jpg?height=1346&width=1248&top_left_y=440&top_left_x=1085)

\section*{Mitral regurgitation}
- Rheumatic disease is the principal cause of mitral regurgitation.
- May follow successful relief of ms.
- Myxomatous degereration of the valve leaflets.
- Elongation or rupture of the chordae tendinae.
- Left atrial Myxomas, cardiac trauma, bacterial endocarditis, Ischaemic heart disease.

\section*{Imaging}

Plain chest radiographs: resemble those of mitral stenosis.

The left atrium is generally larger than in ms and may be aneurismal.
O In acute non-rheumatic mr, CXR may be normal.

O In chronic cases, the heart enlarges with left ventricular predominance.

\section*{Plain cxr}
acute rheumatic regurg.heart size is normal
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-51.jpg?height=1373&width=1283&top_left_y=318&top_left_x=1071)

\section*{Aortic stenosis}

Middle aged and elderly:
-senile degenerative aortic stenosis
-calcification of bicuspid valves.
-rheumatic aortic stenosis.
- Calcific aortic stenosis usually develops on the basis of congenitally bicuspid valves.
- Calcification begins in the $4^{\text {th }}$ decade. Mobility is lost and stenosis develops.
- Valve calcification in this case is invariable since it is the stenosing process. (in mitral stenosis, valve calcification is a result of stenosis)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-54.jpg?height=1750&width=2293&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=46)

\section*{Ischaemic heart disease (CHD)}
- Almost always due to atheroma and its complications particularly thrombosis.
- Risk factors:-
-age -male sex -family history
-smoking -hypertension
-diabetes mellitus -obesity
-sedentary life style -diet

\section*{Investigations}
- Plain radiographs:-
- normal
- Myocardial perfusion studies:scintiscans of the heart are taken at rest and after exercise. Thallium 201 is taken up by viable perfused myocardium.
Thallium 201 is injected during exercise test with immediate exercise images and perfusion images 3 hrs later, after resting.

\section*{Chd cont}
- Myocardial perfusion study.
- Thallium scan showing reversible anterior myocardium ischaemia.
- Images are x-sectional tomograms of the left ventricle.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-57.jpg?height=1398&width=1209&top_left_y=411&top_left_x=1226)

\section*{Complications cont}

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-58.jpg?height=1295&width=1771&top_left_y=545&top_left_x=291)

\section*{Ventricular aneurysm}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_f651ca0f6073e57ae8ebg-59.jpg?height=1270&width=2273&top_left_y=564&top_left_x=78)

\section*{Craniocerebral trauma (Head injury)}

Dr N. M. Kimani Lecturer DIRM $8^{\text {th }}$ Dec. 2016

\section*{Craniocerebral trauma(head injury)}
- Injuries to the skull and brain
-blunt
-penetrating.
- Commonest indications for neuroimaging.
- A major cause of;
- disability,
- morbidity
- mortality
- Has significant direct and indirect financial costs to individuals and society.
- Imaging plays an important role in diagnosis and management.

\section*{Epidemiology}
- Over 10 million people sustain TBIs resulting in death or extensive hospitalization. (WHO;2011 estimates)
- Over 4.5 million deaths occur annually due to TBls
(10\% deaths in the world).
- BY 2020 TBIs will be the leading cause of death and disability.(WHO)
- TBIs account for 6-7\% of alh Recommendations for Traumatic Brain ED visitsurf USArandr

\section*{Kenya situation}
- Over 3,000 Kenyans die as a result of RTA every year and about 40 \% are pedestrians.
- TBI accounts for about $50 \%$ of all RTA deaths
(Saidi, Macharia, \& Atinga, 2005).
- In Kenya, the economic cost of road crashes is $5.6 \%$ of the GDP (about 300 billion KSHS annually).

\section*{Meninges}

Three layers of protective membranes
1. Dura:( 2 layers)

Periosteal-outer layer attached to bone meningeal: inner layer closer to brain.
The two layers are fused except in areas enclosing dural sinuses.
2. Arachnoid: middle layer 'spider web like.'
3. Pia : Attached to brain.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_6a2dc756e167a23443e7g-14.jpg?height=973&width=1060&top_left_y=381&top_left_x=1281)

Pia and arachnoid form the leptomeninges.

T2* MRI is more sensitive than CT in detecting
- all stages of ICH,
- non hemorrhagic contusions,
- injuries in the posterior fossa,
- brainstem and
- DAI.

Susceptibility weighted imaging(SWI) MRI for head trauma increases sensitivity for detection of microhemorrhages and hemorrhagic axonal injury.
(sensitive to compounds which distort the local magnetic field and as such make it useful in detecting blood products, calcium)

\section*{Choice of imaging modality}

- Cost
- Speed of performance
- Diagnostic information desired
- Clinical circumstances
- Limitation of technique

\section*{WHAT TO LOOK FOR ON IMAGING}
- Mnemonic which is used to read an emergency head CT scan is:

\section*{Blood Can Be Very Bad.}
-B: blood EDH ,SDH, IPH,IVH,SAH and extracranial hemorrhage.
-C: cisterns: Presence of blood, effacement and asymmetry in four key cisterns
(circummesencephalic, suprasellar, quadrigeminal and sylvian cisterns).
-B: brain:Asymmetry or effacement of the sulcal pattern, GW matter differentiation, structural shifts and abnormal hypo/hyperdensities.
-V: ventricles:IVH, ventricular effacement/shift and hydrocephalus.
-B: bone:skull fracture.Paranasal sinuses fluid in the setting of trauma should raise the possibility of a skull fracture; intracranial air.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_6a2dc756e167a23443e7g-25.jpg?height=1462&width=1920&top_left_y=200&top_left_x=309)

Lacerations, hematomas.

- Caput succadenium
- Subgaleal hematoma

\section*{CAPUT SUCCADENIUM}
- It is a manifestation of birth trauma.
- Consists of a subcutaneous extraperiost eal serosanguineous fluid collection.
- Results from pressure on the presenting part of the skull against the dilating cervix.
- It extends across the midline and over suture lines and is associated with head moulding.
- Does not usually cause any complication and usually resolves over the first few
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_6a2dc756e167a23443e7g-27.jpg?height=1541&width=1002&top_left_y=333&top_left_x=1355)

\section*{CEPHALOHEMATOMA}
- Subperiosteal hematoma
- Between outer table of calvaria, periosteum.
- Elevates periosteum.
- Does not cross sutures.
- Extracranial equivalent of intracranial epidural hematoma.
- Usually unilateral.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_6a2dc756e167a23443e7g-28.jpg?height=1271&width=945&top_left_y=369&top_left_x=1396)

\section*{SKULL FRACTURES}
1. Linear skull fracture: Sharply marginated lucent line(s).
2. Depressed skull fracture:

Comminuted fragments imploded inwardly.
3. Elevated skull fracture: Elevated, rotated skull segment.
4. Diastatic skull fracture: Widened suture or synchondrosis. Usually accompanied by linear skull fracture.
5. "Growing" skull fracture.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_6a2dc756e167a23443e7g-31.jpg?height=1704&width=913&top_left_y=89&top_left_x=1342)
- Difficult to detect in acute stage.
- Progressively widening, unhealing fracture.
- Lucent lesion with rounded, scalloped margins.
- CSF and soft tissue trapped within expanding fracture.
- Brain is usually encephalomalacic. 2/3 of patients with moderate TBI have an

\section*{Extradural/epidural hematoma}
- Blood collection between inner table of skull and outer (periosteal) layer of dura
- Associated with fractures
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_6a2dc756e167a23443e7g-34.jpg?height=1490&width=1139&top_left_y=224&top_left_x=1261)

\section*{Subdural hematoma}
- Between inner border cell layer of dura and arachnoid .
- - Crescent-shaped extra-axial fluid collection
- May cross sutures, not dural attachments
- May extend along falx, tentorium, and anterior and middle fossa floors .

\section*{NECT:}
- Hyperacute(<6hrs)heterogenous/hypodense, Acute-hyperdense,
- Subacute-iso/hypodense\& Chronic-hypodense.
- MRI: Variable signal depending on stage
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_6a2dc756e167a23443e7g-35.jpg?height=1529&width=1126&top_left_y=345&top_left_x=1280)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_6a2dc756e167a23443e7g-36.jpg?height=1653&width=1222&top_left_y=178&top_left_x=1277)

\section*{Traumatic Subarachnoid hemorrhage}
- Trauma is the most common cause(tSAH found in $33 \%$ with moderate TBI, $60 \%$ with severe TBI).
- Location
- Can be focal or diffuse.
- Focal SAH adjacent to contusion, subdural/epidural hematoma, fracture, laceration.
- Most common Sylvian fissure, inferior frontal subarachnoid spaces.
- Isolated convexity sulci (adjacent to contusion)
- Diffusely in subarachnoid space \&/or basal cisterns.
- Layering on tentorium
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_6a2dc756e167a23443e7g-37.jpg?height=1420&width=1120&top_left_y=310&top_left_x=1379)

\section*{Subdural Hygroma}
- Extraaxial collection of CSF caused by extravasation of CSF from SA space
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_6a2dc756e167a23443e7g-38.jpg?height=648&width=555&top_left_y=199&top_left_x=1438) through a traumatic tear in arachnoid mater
- Acute: Children >> adults
- Subacute and chronic: Following surgery for head injuries in operative bed or opposite site
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_6a2dc756e167a23443e7g-38.jpg?height=645&width=546&top_left_y=892&top_left_x=1315)

1 week after injury

\section*{PNEUMOCEPHALUS}
- Presence of air or gas within skull in any compartment.
- Mechanism: Dural tear.
- NECT(Best diagnostic tool). Very low density (-1,000 HU).
- MR: Foci of absent signal on all sequences,
- Present in 3\% of all skull fractures, 8\% of paranasal sinus fractures.
- Complications: CSF leak (50\%) Infection (25\%).Mortality 15\%.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_6a2dc756e167a23443e7g-39.jpg?height=1107&width=1079&top_left_y=467&top_left_x=1345)

\section*{Intraparenchymal Hematoma}
- Parenchymal vessel rupture from blunt or penetrating forces
- May not lose consciousness (unlike DAI, contusion)
- Hematoma at primary trauma site (usually frontal and temporal)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_6a2dc756e167a23443e7g-40.jpg?height=1293&width=1251&top_left_y=412&top_left_x=1001)
- end
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_6a2dc756e167a23443e7g-41.jpg?height=1318&width=817&top_left_y=432&top_left_x=577)

\section*{INTRODUCTION}
- The fetal heart develops from a series of complex and rapid changes which occur between second and eighth week of intrauterine life.
- During third to fifth weeks cardiac structures develop most actively and are most susceptible to adverse external influence eg. rubella virus or drugs such as thalidomide resulting in CHD.

\section*{EMBRYOLOGY}
- The heart originates from splanchnic mesoderm
- endocardial tube is the initial basic structure
- development is characterised by rapid and complex changes
- Initially straight longitudinal midline tube.
- 5 dilatations develop along it;
- common truncus arteriosus
- bulbous cordis
- common primitive ventricle
- common primitive atrium
- sinus venosus
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ffbec98603f644a08e9fg-04.jpg?height=937&width=549&top_left_y=888&top_left_x=2209)

\section*{Normal fetal circulation}
- Oxygenated blood is carried by umbilical vein (UV) through ductus venosus (DV) and IVC into RA through foramen ovale into LA \& LV, aorta \& systemic arteries.

SVC \& IVC blood is passed into RA, RV \& PA. Most of the RV output passes through PDA into descending aorta to supply lower parts of fetus and umbilical arteries which supply
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ffbec98603f644a08e9fg-06.jpg?height=711&width=1124&top_left_y=703&top_left_x=1917) the fetal placental plexus.

\section*{CHD IMAGING MODALITIES}
- Plain radiography
- Echocardiography- First line to assess anatomy and function.
- Cardiac catheterization- definition of vascular anatomy.
- CT- description of cardiac and vascular anatomy in relation to other structures of the chest.
- MRI- Quantification of cardiac function and vascular flow in vivo.

\section*{CHD IMAGING MODALITIES}
- Plain radiography
- Echocardiography- First line to assess anatomy and function.
- Cardiac catheterization- definition of vascular anatomy.
- Radionuclide imaging - quantify left-to-right shunts, assess relative pulmonary perfusion.
- CT- description of cardiac and vascular anatomy in relation to other structures of the chest.
- MRI- Quantification of cardiac function and vascular flow in vivo.

\section*{Chest radiograph indications}
- Suspected congenital heart disease
- Cyanosis
- Cardiac failure
- Failure to thrive
- Difficult in feeding in the neonatal period
- Fainting spells

\section*{Chest Radiograph}

Standard views - Erect PA/AP chest
- Left lateral

Role- Provides clues to underlying CHD.
- instrumental in planning further investigations.
- limited use in neonates and young children.
-may show related skeletal anomalies
Assessment:
Cardiac and visceral situs- situs solitus, inversus, ambiguous
Cardiac size \& shape.
Mediastinum- Aortic arch, PA, trachea indentation
Py (monary vasculature-normal, plethora, oligemia, congestion
Skelefal features - Rib notching: coarctation of aorta
post surgical stigmata eg. sternal sutures, implants
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ffbec98603f644a08e9fg-11.jpg?height=1780&width=2365&top_left_y=95&top_left_x=0)
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ffbec98603f644a08e9fg-12.jpg?height=1700&width=2069&top_left_y=57&top_left_x=2)

CARDIAC SIZE MEASURMENT r-max. Extension of the heart to the right of the midline I-max. extension of the heart to the left of the midline
Transverse diameter is of the heart is $\mathrm{r}+\mathrm{L}$ td=max. transverse diameter of the thorax
$=50-55 \%$ upto $60 \%$ in children

\section*{Echocardiography}
- Gives a wealth of anatomical and physiological information without ionising radiation, patient discomfort or significant risk
- 1D (M Mode), 2D most commonly used, 3 D
- Doppler echocardiography ;
-CFI; 'non-invasive' angiogram
-Pulsed doppler echo (valves)
-continuous wave doppler
- Contrast echocardiography

\section*{Contrast echocardiography}
- Use of intravascular contrast effects produced when microbubbles of gas present in solution are injected into a peripheral vein or selected area of the heart via a catheter.
- Microbubbles are generated by rapid injection of saline, blood, indocyanine

\section*{Indications}
- Myocardial perfusion can be investigated directly/indirectly after intracoronary/ venous contrast injection with/out DSA
- Better define anatomy not shown well on 2D E.g imaging the anomalous venous connections to the heart
- Enhancement of endocardial boundaries increasing sensitivity of wall motion abnormalities e.g.valvular regurgitation, shunts

\section*{Doppler echocardiography}
- Used to study venous flow from IVC, SVC, pulmonary vessels and flow across the valves
- Measure direction and speed of blood flowing through the heart and blood vessels
- Check for abnormal communication between left and right side of the heart and regurgitation
- Calculate cardiac output/ejection fraction
- Flow towards the transducer- red away from it - blue, and turbulence flow mixture of red and blue- yellow
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ffbec98603f644a08e9fg-17.jpg?height=1523&width=1995&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=476)

A; Parasternal long axis view -shows regurgitant jet consistent with massive aortic insufficiency
B; Apical 4c view-systolic murmer; c:Apical 4c view - severe left vent. Systolic dysfunction.
Tricuspid regurgitation is shown; D;Parasternal long axis - shows aorto-right

\title{
Clinical applications of echocardiography
}
- Congenital heart disease
- Anatomical defects, shunts
- Stress echo
- Pericardial disease
- Coronary artery disease
- Cardiomyopathies
- Valvular disease
- Cardiac masses
- Diseases of the aorta

\section*{Angƒ±ocardƒ±ography}
- Important technique for cardiac and great vessel anatomy
- Coronary angiography commonest
-transfemoral approach using Judkins or Amplatz catheters
- Pulmonary angiography; for possible embolism.
- Adv; Offers high resolution images of the heart and great vessels.
- Disadv ; Requires experienced hands and cardiac monitoring, invasive

\section*{Angiocardiography cont'd}
- Indications:
- CHD, valvular/myocardial disease, ventricular function
- Technique;
-right sided structures studied by introducing catheter into a peripheral vein, femoral or antecubital or basilic
-left sided structures by introducing catheter into femoral artery retrogradely
CM-LOCM $370 ; 1 \mathrm{ml} / \mathrm{kg}$ at $18-20 \mathrm{mls} / \mathrm{s}$ (adults), $1.5 \mathrm{ml} / \mathrm{kg}$
( $0-1 \mathrm{yr}$ ); $1.0 \mathrm{ml} / \mathrm{kg}$ ( $>5 \mathrm{yrs}$ )

\section*{Angiocardiography cont'd}
- Equipment- fluoroscopy unit on a C-arm with DSA facilities, pressure recording device, ECG monitor, blood oxygen analyser, catheters ( pigtail, NIH), cournand catheter-for pressure measurements
- Angled views which place the pathological lesion at right angles to the Xray beam increase diagnostic accuracy of cardiography
- Complications; sudden death, arrhythmias, MI, C. Media s/effects

\section*{CARDIAC MRI}
- Advantages; Provides anatomy, dynamic motion studies of function, flow and chemistry with minimal risk to the patient

Disadvantage - expensive
-availability
- metallic implants

\section*{Clinical applications of Cardiac MRI}
- ventricular function analysis
- Myocardial function at rest and during stress
- Myocardial perfusion
- Measurement of coronary flow
- Valvular heart disease
- Pericardial pathology

Cardiac tumors and thrombi
- Cardiomyopathies
- Assessing great vessels- aortic/pulmonary disease

\section*{Pulse sequences used}
- Tissue characterization-done usingT1W, T2W, contrast enhancement and spectroscopy
- T1W-best anatomic depiction,
- Blood-show signal void
- Gradient echo-Impart bright signal to coherently flowing blood-white blood
- Fast spin echo- vascular wall bright, blood black

\section*{C/MRI-cont'd}
- Gradient recalled echo-technique -applied for motion studies,to show flowing blood as well as cardiac motion
- ECG gated MRI of the heart-
- Velocity mapping sequences
- Images-tomographic slices,of any selected plane

\section*{MRI Cont'd}
- Gadolinium -as contrast used in;
- myocardial perfusion
- MR angiography; time of flight MRA, phase contrast MRA, contrast enhanced MRA
- Myocardial infarction
- Ischemia

TRANSVERSE YMAGE-through ventricles at AV Valve level

\section*{CARDIAC CT}
- Good images of thoracic anatomy and major cardiac structures and large vessels
- CECT first line in imaging aortic rupture or dissection
- Calcification on the coronary vessels
- Evaluation of coronary vessels
- Disadvantages; ionizing radiation, motion artifact

- scans are taken in;stress and resting phases

Stress induced by:-
- Physical exercise test: treadmill, bicycling.
- Pharmacologic stress test: dipyridamole, adenosine, dobutamine.
- The heart scanned in longitudinal, vertical and short axes

\section*{Normal perfusion scans}
- Horse-shoe in vertical and long.axes and doughnut in short axis
- stress and rest images are same in normal myocardial perfusion
- Ischaemia-normal at rest and reduced at stress
- infarcted and scars -reduced in both

- Holt-Oram Syndrome
- Ellis van Creveld Syndrome
- Turner's, Noonans
* Alcohol.
* Prenatal Rubella infection, Toxoplasmosis.
\% Drugs: Thalidomide toxicity---17\% have a cardiac lesion.
* Gestational diabetes.

\section*{Patent Ductus Arteriosus}

\section*{Patent Ductus Arteriosus}
- Persistence beyond 10 days is considered abnormal.
- Accounts for $15 \%$ of all congenital heart diseases.
- Commoner in females (2-3:1)
- Associated with low birth weight, prematurity, high altitudes.

\section*{VSDs.}
- One of the commonest anomaly (second only to bicuspid aortic valves). Incidence $3570 / 1 \mathrm{~m}$ live births.
- $\mathbf{5 0 \%}$ of patients with VSD have associated cardiac anomaly e.g. coarctation of the aorta.
- $80 \%$ are perimembranous \& the vast majority(75\%) of small ones close spontaneously.

\section*{VSD}

Lets us first understand the anatomy.

The Interventricular septum has two parts, the larger muscular and the smaller membranous part.

The membranous part is the posterosuperior region.

Membranous VSDs account for 80\% of all VSDs.

Muscular VSDs tend to close spontaneously.

Its possible to have multiple VSDs at any part of the interventricular septum.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ffbec98603f644a08e9fg-56.jpg?height=1444&width=1118&top_left_y=290&top_left_x=1550)

\section*{Congenital Cyanotic heart disease}
- For clinically detectable central cyanosis, there must be at least 5 g of reduced Hb per 100 ml of aortic blood. Occurs in 3 ways:
1. Direct right to left shunt eg. TOF
2. Transposition of great arteries.
3. Common chambers eg. Common atria, common ventricle, truncus arteriosus.

\section*{TOF}
- Most common cyanotic heart lesion.
- Incidence: 3-5 per 10,000 live births.
- Primary hypoplasia of infundibular septum secondary to unequal partitioning of the conotruncus.
- Conotruncal septum displaced anteriorly, and its lowermost component, the Infundibular septum fails to fuse with the top of the IV septum.
- Features include:
- Infundibular right ventricular outflow tract stenosis
- subaortic VSD
- Over-riding aorta
- Right ventricular hypertrophy

TOF
- Desaturated blood enters RA, flows through RVOT into lungs. Some blood shunts right to left through VSD into ascending aorta.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ffbec98603f644a08e9fg-59.jpg?height=1349&width=1196&top_left_y=372&top_left_x=1647)

\section*{TOF}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ffbec98603f644a08e9fg-60.jpg?height=1164&width=2456&top_left_y=244&top_left_x=421)

Graphic- subvalvular(infundibular) stenosis, small pulm valve, large aortic valve, over-riding aorta, VSD, RVH, rt arch.

CTA cor- hypoplastic MPA, high VSD, over-riding aorta.

\section*{TOF}

Radiography:
- "Boot-shaped heart" = "coeur en sabot".
- Right ventricular hypertrophy.
- Concave pulmonary artery segment.
- Decreased pulmonary vascularity(oligaemia).
> Normal heart size at birth.
- Right aortic arch, mirror image branching (25\%).

\section*{TOF}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ffbec98603f644a08e9fg-62.jpg?height=1175&width=2505&top_left_y=142&top_left_x=423)
- boot shaped heart, concave pulmonary artery segment.
- Right aortic arch, elevated cardiac apex, pulmonary oligemia

\section*{TOF}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ffbec98603f644a08e9fg-63.jpg?height=1162&width=2505&top_left_y=244&top_left_x=406)
- Parasternal long axis- VSD, over-riding aorta,
- CF doppler- right to left flow from RV to aorta.

\section*{Ebstein anomaly}
- Downward displacement of septal \& posterior leaflets of tricuspid valve.
- Insufficient separation of tricuspid valve leaflets and chordae tendineae from $\mathrm{R} \dagger$ ventricular endocardium.
- Massive tricuspid regurgitation.
- Associated anomalies-PFO, ASD in 90\%.
- Normal or decreased vascularity
- Rt. to Lt. shunt thru PFO causes cyanosis.

\section*{Ebstein anomaly}
- Inferior displacement of tricuspid valve leaflets into RV.

Thin-walled, low-pressure "atrialized" segment of RV.
tricuspid valve is grossly insufficient.
- RA blood flow is shunted rt. to It. across an ASD or PFO into LA. Some blood may cross the RVOT and enter PA.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ffbec98603f644a08e9fg-65.jpg?height=1362&width=1247&top_left_y=399&top_left_x=1613)

\section*{Ebstein anomaly}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ffbec98603f644a08e9fg-66.jpg?height=1185&width=2438&top_left_y=304&top_left_x=444)

Graphic-Downward displacement of posterior valve leaflet, incorporated into RV.

MR cine- Dilated RA, low placement of tricuspid valve, atrialized RV.

\section*{Ebstein anomaly}
- Radiography: Severe right sided cardiomegally.
- Echo- Rt chamber enlargement, enlarged tricuspid annulus, tricuspid regurgitation, PFO with Rt. to Lt. shunting.
- Treatment- Tricuspid valve replacement and/ or valvuloplasty

\section*{EBSTEIN ANOMALY}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ffbec98603f644a08e9fg-68.jpg?height=1028&width=2377&top_left_y=324&top_left_x=474)
- Massive globular cardiomegally (RA dilatation), pulmonary oligemia.
- DDX: pericardial effusion

\section*{Ebstein anomaly}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ffbec98603f644a08e9fg-69.jpg?height=864&width=2540&top_left_y=308&top_left_x=380)
A. Subcostal 4 chamber - displacement of tricuspid valve leaflets inferiorly.
B. Color Doppler- severe regurgitation of the dysplastic tricuspid valve.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ffbec98603f644a08e9fg-70.jpg?height=1204&width=2417&top_left_y=223&top_left_x=450)

\section*{Imaging in rheumatology.}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ebc4a0a477edfec83849g-01.jpg?height=1156&width=2106&top_left_y=640&top_left_x=197)

\section*{What is Rheumatology?}
- Rheumatology is the subspecialty of medicine devoted to the diagnosis and treatment of diseases that affect bones muscles, ligaments, tendons, joints, and other body parts including the immune system.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ebc4a0a477edfec83849g-03.jpg?height=1875&width=989&top_left_y=0&top_left_x=0)

\section*{Outline}
- Inflammatory arthritis
- Rheumatoid arthritis
- Psoriatic arthritis
- Reiter's disease
- Juvenile chronic arthritis DDx:
* Osteoarthritis
* Septic arthritis
- Other inflammatory arthritides
- Hemophilia
- Callanan vaccilar dicance

\section*{Why?}

\section*{Sutton's Law}

This law has been ascribed to Willie Sutton, a famous bank robber. When asked why he robbed banks, he reportedly said, "Because that's where the money is."
In the radiographic evaluation of appendicular arthropathies, the "money" is generally in a relatively small handful of disorders.
Even though over 90 different rheumatic diseases are recognized by the American College of Rheumatology, only three entities are commonly seen in most clinical radiology practices, even including those located in

\section*{More laws....}
- In George Orwell's Animal Farm, it is stated that "All animals are equal.
- But some animals are more equal than others.
- " This principle is manifested in the appendicular arthropathies, where some radiographic findings are quite specific and can quickly lead one to the correct diagnosis.
- Other findings are less specific and are usually unhelpful in ordering one's differential diagnosis.

\section*{And the last...}
- The Law of Parsimony
- In medical school, one is taught to take historical points and physical findings and to put them together into one diagnosis which explains everything (the law of parsimony).
- However, once one reaches the ward rotations and opens a patient's chart to the problem list, one sees that most real patients have several disorders going on simultaneously.
- In real life, patients often have more than one arthropathy.
- This is most commonly seen in patients with

\section*{Approach}
- ABCDE'S
- Alignment
- Bone
- Cartilage/Joint space
- Distribution
- Erosions.
- Soft tissue

\section*{Rheumatoid}

\section*{arthritis.}
- Rheumatoid arthritis (RA) is a chronic systemic disease of unknown origin that predominantly involves synovial tissue.
- Early diagnosis and initiation of proper therapy help modify the course of the disease and reduce the degree of severe late sequel.
- Radiology plays a key role in diagnosis and management of RA.

\section*{Tools of the trade.}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ebc4a0a477edfec83849g-09.jpg?height=610&width=910&top_left_y=1&top_left_x=1586)
- Currently, magnetic resonance imaging is the best imaging modality because it depicts soft-tissue changes and damage to cartilage and bone even
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ebc4a0a477edfec83849g-09.jpg?height=477&width=1008&top_left_y=699&top_left_x=1489) better and at an earlier stage than does computed tomography.
- Ultrasound and conventional radiography are more readily available but cannot show the entire spectrum of the disease.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_ebc4a0a477edfec83849g-09.jpg?height=444&width=980&top_left_y=1430&top_left_x=1519)

\section*{Reiter's Disease}
- Radiographically indistinguishable from psoriatic arthritis
- Distribution different from psoriatic arthritis
-Primarily involving feet, ankles, knees
-Hands, hips, SI joints and spine much less frequently involved

\section*{Osteoarthritis}
- Non-uniform joint space loss
- Osteophytes
- Subchondral
cysts and
sclerosis
- Normal mineralization
- No erosions
- Locations
- Common in hands DIP/PIP, $1^{\text {st }}$ CMC, feet, knees (esp. medial compartment), and hips
- Shoulders/elbows generally spared
- Wrists/ankles uncommonly involved

\section*{Late}

\section*{Septic Arthritis}
- Monoarticular
- Early sign
- Joint effusion
- Periarticular soft tissue swelling
- Later
- Uniform joint space loss
- Joint-centered osseous destruction
- Juxtaarticular osteopenia

\section*{Arthritis}
- Periarticular softtissue swelling
- Osteopenia
- No bone formation
- Marginal and peripheral erosions
- Cartilage destruction a late finding
- Location:
- hips, knees, tarsal joints, and spine

\section*{Tuberculous Arthritis}
- Phemister's triad:
-Periarticular osteopenia
-Peripheral/marginal osseous erosions
-Gradual joint space narrowing

\section*{Scleroderma}

\section*{Lupus}

- Bilaterally symmetric

- Reducible subluxations and dislocations
- Normal joint spaces

\section*{GIT IMAGING}

Dr. T. Musila Mutala

\section*{OBJECTIVES}
1. Describe the modalities available for GIT imaging
2. Discuss the indications, contraindications and patient preparation for each of the modalities
3. Highlight the limitations of the described imaging modalities
4. Present examples of pathological processes as demonstrated in each of the modalities
5. Bring out a decision making protocol for imaging in acute abdomen setting

\section*{PLAIN RADIOGRAPHY}
- Plain radiographs are frequently still the first investigation in the acute abdomen
- For non-acute situations, the relatively small yield of information from a plain radiograph does not justify the radiation dose that it entails, unless it is on follow up basis
- Plain radiographic findings must corroborate with clinical findings: sometimes even obvious signs can be non-specific generating a plethora of differential diagnoses. A good example is the presence of air-fluid levels which may be benign or a pointer to obstruction

\section*{ABDOMINAL X-RAY: SYSTEMATIC APPROACH}
- Check the patient details
- Assess quality (including presence of artifacts).
- Bowel gas
- Soft tissues
- Bones
- Abnormal calcifications

\section*{AXR - AIR DISTRIBUTION}

\section*{Intra-luminal}

Normal
- Gastric bubble (erect film)
- Large bowel
- Very minimal or nondetectable in small bowel

Check for abnormalities
- Size
- Distribution: peripheral large bowel, central - small bowel

\section*{Extra-luminal}
- Always abnormal
- Key areas to look at
1.Subdiaphragmatic (erect film)
2.Bowel wall
3.Liver - portovenous, biliary.

- Sialography
- Ba. Swallow

\section*{GIT Contrast Radiography - Sialography}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_75f464ce55e2db90ad4cg-30.jpg?height=1133&width=1229&top_left_y=349&top_left_x=192)

Sialography
- Cannulation of major salivary gland ducts
- Water soluble contrast media is used
- Demonstrates luminal anatomy and pathology

INDICATIONS
1. Pain
2. Swelling
3. Sjogren's syndrome
4. Sicca
5. Post-traumatic, postoperative strictures, cysts, fistulas

\section*{GIT Contrast Radiography - Barium Swallow}

\section*{Indications}
-Dysphagia
-Pain on swallowing
-Hematemesis
-Assessment of tracheo-oesophageal fistula(where plain films not diagnostic)
-Follow up following oesophageal surgery
NB: In assessment of perforation, water soluble contras instead of barium.

\section*{GIT Contrast Radiography}

Normal findings

Physiological constrictions may be seen - cricopharyngeus muscle, aortic arch, left main bronchus, left atrium and gastroesophageal junction.

\section*{GIT Contrast Radiography}

\section*{- Barium Meal}

Double contrast is an examination of choice in adults. Single contrast is used in children and very ill adults.

\section*{INDICATIONS}
> dyspepsia
> weight loss.
- Follow up after surgery
> suspected gastrointestinal bleeding or unexplained-icon deficiency
> partial obstruction

\section*{GIT Contrast Radiography}

\section*{- Barium Meal}

> Contraindications
> -Complete large bowel obstruction
> -Suspected perforation (there is up to $50 \%$ mortality if barium leaks into the peritoneum).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_75f464ce55e2db90ad4cg-37.jpg?height=279&width=272&top_left_y=1101&top_left_x=1969)

\section*{GIT Contrast Radiography}

\section*{- Ba Enema}

Examination of the large bowel.
It is currently getting replaced by CT colonography.
Two methods commonly used.
i. Double contrast: This is examination of choice to demonstrate mucosal pattern.
ii. Single contrast: This is used in children since it is not usually necessary to demonstrate mucosal pattern It can also be used to reduce an intussusception

\section*{Ultrasound in GIT Imaging}

Though of limited value in imaging the GIT, for the reason that gas produces poor sound penetration, it can be useful in the following areas
1. Suspected masses
2. Suspected peritoneal fluid collections
3. Suspected calculus
4. Suspected inflammatory conditions like appendieitis
5. Suspected intusussception
6. Suspected hernias
7. The jaundiced patient

\section*{Ultrasound in GIT Imaging - Salivary Glands}
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_75f464ce55e2db90ad4cg-51.jpg?height=1235&width=1676&top_left_y=326&top_left_x=176)

\section*{Computerised Tomography (CT) in GIT}
1. Gaining popularity as investigation of choice in acute abdomen and trauma
2. Complement other radiological studies of the GIT to determine the extent of disease
3. Evaluatethe mesentery, adjacent solid organs, the peritoneal cavity, and the retroperitoneum
4. Stage malignancies and evaluate for recurrence
5. CT enteroclysis to evaluate the small bowel
6. Virtual endoscopy - CT Colonography has largely replaced Ba enema as the imaging modality of choice in large bowel disease. It is also incorporated as a screening tool for colorectal carcinoma in high risk groups.

\section*{Computerised Tomography (CT) in GIT}

Take note of the following:
1. CT is relatively expensive and unavailable
2. Caution has to be taken since it gives high radiation dose to the patient

\section*{Magnetic Resonance Imaging (MRI) in GIT}

> Though more expensive and less available compared to the modalities we have already discussed it has these main advantages
1. Excellent soft tissue contrast
2. Non- ionising radiation
3. Multi-sequential multi-planar capabilities

Its application includes the same as CT except that it is
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_75f464ce55e2db90ad4cg-62.jpg?height=286&width=279&top_left_y=1101&top_left_x=1965) not used in acute setting.

\section*{INCREASING ROLE OF MRI IN RECTAL CANCER}
- Paradigm shift has taken place in imaging of rectal carcinoma with High Resolution MRI taking centre stage in tumour staging.
- Excellent anatomical depiction can be attained with this modality influencing surgical decision making since the introduction of total mesorectal excision (TME) as standard surgical procedure. The mesorectal fascia determines the potential circumferential resection margin (CRM).
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_75f464ce55e2db90ad4cg-66.jpg?height=1018&width=1150&top_left_y=100&top_left_x=0)

Normal anatomy: Axial Turbo SE T2W image shows the mesorectal fat (*), the mesorectal fascia (white arrows) and
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_75f464ce55e2db90ad4cg-66.jpg?height=1047&width=868&top_left_y=105&top_left_x=1304) confluence of Denovillier's and mesorectal fasciae (black arrows).

Sagittal turbo SE T2W image demonstrates the normal anatomy
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_75f464ce55e2db90ad4cg-67.jpg?height=859&width=1041&top_left_y=419&top_left_x=60)

A
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_75f464ce55e2db90ad4cg-67.jpg?height=846&width=913&top_left_y=425&top_left_x=1253)

B

The driver of the paradigm shift as demonstrated in this rectal tumour on CT (A) and HRMRI (B). The MR image confidently puts it as stage 1 tumour since the mscularis propria is intact.
![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2025_05_16_75f464ce55e2db90ad4cg-67.jpg?height=113&width=113&top_left_y=1710&top_left_x=2329)
